The author of this book, having completed for you, my children, this book, which is a complete part of the science of medicine, and having reached its peak in clarity and explanation, has seen fit to complete it for you with this chapter, which is a part of practical application.
Because practical application in our land and in our time is entirely lacking, so much so that its science almost ceased to be studied and its influence disappeared. And only a few traces remained in the books of the ancients, which hands have distorted and error and confusion have affected, so that their meanings became obscure and their benefits distant.
Therefore, I have seen fit to revive it and compose this chapter in a way of explanation, clarification, and summarization, and to present illustrations of cautery irons and other instruments of work, as this adds to clarity and contains what is needed.
The reason there is no skilled practitioner with skillful hands in our time is that the art of medicine is long, and its practitioner must first exert himself in the study of anatomy, as described by Galen. So that he may understand the benefits of the organs, their nature, temperaments, connections, and separations, and the knowledge of bones, nerves, muscles, their number, and origins; and the pulsating and stationary veins, and the locations of their exits.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the art of handcraft. We have mentioned a part of this in the introduction of this book.
For whoever is not knowledgeable about the anatomy we have mentioned is likely to make a mistake that kills people, as I have often observed in those who presume to know and claim it without knowledge or understanding.
I saw an ignorant physician who incised a swine-like tumor on a woman's neck, severing some of the arteries of the neck.  The woman bled until she fell dead at his hands.
And I saw another physician, while attempting to remove a stone from an elderly man, who had a large stone.  He acted rashly and removed it by tearing a piece of the bladder. The man died about three days later.  I was called to remove it, and from the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I judged that I could not have done it.
And I saw another physician who made a living practicing medicine among the leading figures of our city.
A dark-skinned boy had a fractured leg near the heel, accompanied by a wound.  The physician, in his ignorance, hastily bound the fracture tightly to the wound with bandages and splints, leaving no breathing space for the wound.
Then, he left it to its own devices and neglected it for days, instructing the boy not to loosen the bandages until his leg and foot swelled up and he was close to death.
So I was called to him, and I quickly loosened the bandages. He found relief and his pain lessened, but the damage to the limb had already taken hold, and I was unable to reverse it.
The damage continued to spread throughout the limb until he died.
I saw another physician treat a cancerous tumor.  After a few days, it ulcerated, and the patient's suffering worsened.  This is because, if the cancer is purely black bile, it should not be treated with iron at all.
Unless it is in a limb that can be completely removed.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual work is divided into two categories: work that is accompanied by safety, and work that more often than not results in injury.
And I have warned, throughout this book, of every work that involves risk and fear; you must avoid and reject it.
So that the ignorant do not find a way to speak ill and criticize, take care, be cautious, and be gentle and firm in your treatment.
Employ the best path leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous and difficult-to-cure diseases.
Keep yourselves away from anything that you fear might lead you into doubt regarding your religion and your worldly affairs.  This is more enduring for your reputation and raises your status in this world and the next.
Galen, in some of his precepts, said, "Do not treat a disease poorly, lest you be called a poor physician.
This article is divided into three chapters.
The first chapter.
In cauterization with fire and cauterization with sharp instruments, a categorized section from the heel to the foot, images of instruments, cautery irons, and everything necessary for manual work.
And the second chapter.
On incision, lancing, bloodletting, cupping, surgeries, removing arrows and similar procedures, all categorized and arranged, with illustrations of the instruments.
And the third chapter.
On fractures, dislocations, treatment of sprains, and similar conditions, categorized and arranged from the heel to the foot, with illustrations of the instruments.
The first chapter.
On cauterization.
Before mentioning its use, it is necessary to describe its benefits, harms, and the temperament in which it is used.
I say that discussing the benefits and harms of cauterization is a lengthy discourse, a precise science, and a hidden secret, and many wise men have spoken on it.
And they differed on it.  I have summarized the little that I have taken from their words for fear of being overly verbose.
I say that cauterization is generally beneficial for every bad temperament, with or without matter, excluding two temperaments: a hot temperament without matter, and a dry temperament without matter.
As for the dry temperament with matter, they differed on it. Some said that cauterization is beneficial in it, while others said the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable for a disease arising from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
It is impossible to be cured of a hot, dry disease with a hot, dry remedy.  The one who says the opposite asserts that cauterization with fire may benefit from a hot, dry disease that occurs in people's bodies. Because when you add a person's body and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the person's body cold.
And I say in agreement with him, because experience has revealed this to me on numerous occasions; however, this matter should only be considered by those who have diligently practiced and trained extensively in the art of cauterization. And to understand the differences in temperaments among people, the nature of illnesses themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for the other temperaments, there is no fear of them, especially the cold, moist diseases; all physicians agree on this and do not differ about the benefit of cauterization in these cases.
Know, my children, that the secret of cauterization with fire and its superiority over cauterization with a burning medicament lies in this: fire is a simple element whose effect is limited to the specific organ cauterized.  It will not harm another adjacent organ except with slight damage.
However, cauterization with a burning medicament may extend its effect to other organs.  It might even cause an incurable disease or death in the target organ.  Fire, by virtue of its noble and pure essence, will not cause such harm unless it is overused.
This has become clear to us through long experience, careful attention to the craft, and understanding the facts of the matter.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy explanation.  Were it not inappropriate for this book, I would have presented to you the secret and method of fire's action on the body, and its ability to cure illnesses, with a profound, logical philosophical argument that surpasses your understanding.
Know, my children, that there is disagreement concerning the optimal time for cauterization.  They consider springtime to be the best time.
I say that cauterization is suitable in every season, for any harm caused by the season is surpassed by the benefit derived from cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for a severe, urgent, and dangerous ailment that cannot tolerate delay, lest a greater calamity follow the lesser harm caused by the season.
Do not, my children, entertain the popular notions and the ignorant physicians' imaginings that a cauterization curing a disease will never return and therefore make it a detriment.
The matter is not as you suppose, for cauterization is like a medicine that alters the temperament and dries the humors, which are the cause of the ailments.
However, cauterization is superior to medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its power.
The disease may return at some time, depending on the patient's temperament, the strength and severity of the illness, the confluence of factors within the body, and neglect of proper diet and other contributing factors.
Unless the disease for which cauterization is used is a mild one in an organ with few humors and moisture. For example, cauterizing a tooth for pain, and similar cases, that pain may not return, and this will be, at the least, the case.
And as for the common saying that cauterization is the last resort in medicine, it is a correct statement, not in the sense they suppose, because they believe that no remedy, whether medicinal or otherwise, can be of benefit after cauterization. The matter is otherwise.
Rather, the meaning of "cauterization being the last resort in medicine" is that if we have employed various treatments for a disease, and those remedies have proven ineffective, and then cauterization is used as the final measure and is successful, it is in this sense that cauterization is considered the last resort. Not in the sense understood by the common people and many ignorant physicians.
The ancients stated that cauterization with gold is superior to cauterization with iron. They said this because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its substance, and they said that the cauterized area does not suppurate; however, this is not universally true.
For I have tried it, and found that it does so in some bodies but not in others.  Cauterization with gold is better and superior to iron, as they say.  However, if you heat the gold cautery in the fire, you will not readily discern when it has been heated to the desired degree of gold's redness.
Because cold quickly affects it, and if you increase the heat, it melts in the fire and is fused, thus placing the craftsman in a laborious endeavor.  Therefore, cauterization with iron is quicker and closer to correctness for use, God willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections, organized from the head to the foot, so that the student may readily obtain what he desires, God willing.
Chapter One.
Cauterization of the head: One cauterization.
This cauterization is beneficial for excess moisture and coldness in the brain, which are the cause of headaches, frequent colds that descend from the head to the eyes and ears, excessive sleepiness, toothache, and sore throats. And generally, for every illness resulting from coldness, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and stupor, and similar diseases.
The method for this cauterization is that the patient should first be induced to vomit with a purgative medicine, cleansing the head for three or four nights, according to the patient's strength, age, and habits.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit before you, cross-legged, with his hands on his chest.
Then place the base of your palm on the base of his nose, between his eyes.  Wherever your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-wood cautery, which has this form.
If the bone has been exposed to the extent of a pin's head or a lentil seed, remove your hand.  Otherwise, reheat your instrument, either the same iron or another, if it has cooled. Until you see the bone as I have described.  Then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a cotton pad in it. Apply it to the area and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton pad soaked in clarified butter and leave it on until the burning sensation subsides. Then treat it with the four-ingredient ointment until it heals, God willing.
They say that a wound that remains open allows pus to drain, and this is better and more beneficial.
Some have said to cauterize the skin to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the bone's thickness is burned, then to remove the burned bone, and then treat the area.
Others say that one should use a strong cautery, affecting the bone strongly enough that it falls off like a small piece of quartz or a tiny shard. They claim that this releases vapors from the head and leaves the wound open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals.  I do not at all recommend these two types of cauterization, except perhaps for some people, and it is a risky approach.  Leaving it as it is is preferable.  With good health, a head weakens when its natural connections are broken apart, as we have seen in other parts of the body.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak.  The first type of cauterization is safer and better, in my opinion.  Use it, and you will be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
On cauterization of the head also.
If a chronic headache occurs throughout the head, and this affliction lasts a long time for the patient, and poultices, washes, whips, ointments, and dressings have been used, and especially if the single cauterization we described did not help, then consider... If the patient's head is strong in constitution and not weak, and he experiences severe cold, cauterize again, slightly above the first one. Then cauterize each horn of his head with a cauterization until the thickness of the skin is gone and the bone is exposed to the extent we have described. Cauterize with a cauterization on the back of his head, in the spot known as the "axe" (or "the place of the axe").  Be gentle here, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience severe pain unlike the pain of other head cauterizations; I will describe this cauterization in its place.
The cauterization instrument used for the horns and back of the head should be gentler than the one used for the middle of the head.  This is its description.
Chapter Three.
In the cauterization of the chronic sister.
If, in a fissure of the head, pain occurs with headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then have the patient vomit with drugs that cleanse the head, and use all the other treatment mentioned in the divisions of diseases. If this does not succeed, then cauterization there is in two ways. Either by cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine, or by iron.
As for cauterization with the burning medicine, it is that you take one clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends from both sides, then cut the painful area on the temple with a wide scalpel until there is a space underneath the skin to accommodate the clove.
Then insert it into the skin until it disappears, then hold it firmly with firm pressure, and leave it for about fifteen hours, then remove it and take out the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then apply a dressing.
Place on it a piece of cloth dipped in oil until the area is suppurated, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If you wish, you may do this with some of the burning medicines which I have given in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
And as for cauterizing it with iron, it is done in this manner: heat the cautery iron, which is of this shape:
And it is called the nail-headed cautery because its head is shaped like a nail, with some grooves and a small protuberance in the center.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while you rotate the iron a little at a time, and the amount of skin thickness that you burn should be about half of it. And lift your hand so as not to burn the artery underneath and cause bleeding, then soak a piece of cloth in salt water and place it on the area and leave it for three days. Then wrap the cloth in grease, and then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this vein with the pointed knife-like part of the cautery, and protect yourself from cutting the artery in this chronic vein, especially.
Chapter Four.
Cauterizing the chronic vein.
If you have treated the vein with the previous treatments mentioned and with the treatments mentioned in the sections on diseases, and the treatment has not been effective, and you see an underlying cause that the initial cauterization with medication or cauterization with fire cannot resolve, You should heat the knife-like cautery until it whitens, after having marked the area of pain with a line half a finger's length or thereabouts.  You should hold your hand in place once, pressing firmly until you cut the artery and reach the bone.
However, you should be careful not to touch the jawbone, which moves during chewing, and burn the muscle or nerve that controls it, causing spasm.
Be cautious and watchful of the artery's bleeding after cutting it, for there is risk in severing it. Especially if one is ignorant of the procedure and has no tested experience; abandoning the practice is preferable.
The treatment of accidental bleeding from the artery will be discussed in its proper place in the book, God willing, if you perceive a condition for which this cauterization is insufficient.
If you see the patient's condition as manageable, cauterize the center of the head as described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, you can use the cauterization described in the chapter on severing arteries with the two-bladed cautery, for it is a better and more effective cauterization than this.
Fifth Chapter.
On cauterizing earaches.
If an earache occurs due to cold, and it is treated with laxatives and other treatments we have mentioned in the classification, but the pain does not subside, then cauterize with the cautery called the "point," whose image is this.
Then, after heating it, drip it around the entire ear, or around both ears if the pain is in them. Move the cauterization slightly away from the base of the ear after marking the location with ink.  The cauterization should be about ten points in each ear, or thereabouts. Then treat the areas until healed, God willing.
Chapter six.
On cauterization with the *al-laquah*.
The *al-laquah* that is treated with cauterization is of the type that arises from phlegm, as I mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
One should avoid cauterizing the type that arises from dryness and nerve spasm.
When you have treated this type of *al-laquah* with poultices, cupping, and gargles, and your treatment has been unsuccessful, then the afflicted person should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second slightly below the temple, and the third at the juncture of the lips.
Make your cauterization on the opposite side of the afflicted area, because relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The cauterization should be done in the following way:  A cauterization should be made opposite the upper part of the ear, slightly below the temple, and another one on the temple.  The length of each should be the length of a thumb.
Lower your cautery hand until you burn through about half the thickness of the skin.  This is a picture of the cautery iron; it is a type of scalpel, similar to the one previously described, but slightly milder, as you can see. The knife should have a slight excess of thickness, then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
On cauterization of chronic stroke.
If the stroke becomes chronic and you have treated it as we have described, and your treatment is unsuccessful, and there is no fever in the patient, cauterize four times. One cauterization on each temple, one cauterization in the middle of the head as we mentioned, and one cauterization at the back of the head, as previously described.
The method of cauterization as previously mentioned.  A cauterization on the mouth of the stomach may also be performed, which is more effective, then treat as previously described.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterizing forgetfulness.
The one that originates from phlegm should first be given to the patient large emetics and purgatives to purify the brain.
Then, his entire head should be shaved, and a mustard poultice, as described in the chapter on dressings, should be applied to his back repeatedly, for it is a form of cauterization.
And do this according to the same method I mentioned there. If he recovers, do nothing more; otherwise, cauterize him with three cauteries on the back of his head, arranged in a line from the top of the head to the bottom of the neck.
Place a finger's width between each cauterization. Then treat the cauterization as previously described. If you wish to add more, and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle cauterization.
If you wish to add more, cauterize on the temples. Then treat him until he recovers, and the cauterization should be olive-shaped, in the manner previously mentioned.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterization for paralysis and general relaxation of the body.
You should first cleanse the head with the poultices and as we have described, then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize a mark in the middle of the head, a mark on each temple, and a mark on the back of the head.
And three on the cervical vertebrae; if, in a case of relaxation of the body, more than this is needed, and the patient is able to tolerate it and the illness is severe and entrenched, cauterize four marks on the vertebrae of his back. Cauterize deeply until most of the skin is burned, then raise your hand, and then treat it as previously mentioned until he is healed, God willing.  The cautery should be olive oil.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterizing epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is due to phlegm should be cauterized.  First, his brain should be cleansed with large cupping glasses and by all the other treatments we have mentioned in the divisions, if the patient is large and can tolerate taking medications.
However, if it is a child who cannot tolerate medications, he should use gargles and mouthwashes to cleanse the brain many days beforehand, while also improving his diet.
Then shave his head, then cauterize him once in the center of the head, as previously described in the description, and another cauterization on his back of the head. And on each temple of his head a cauterization.  If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, cauterize him with the cauterizations that were mentioned in the case of paralysis and bodily relaxation, on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back, and the cautery should be olive-colored, following the description previously given.
If the patient is a child, make the cauterization gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humours and thick phlegm, cauterize him with the cauterizations that we have described in the case of paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess of black bile, and the patient's body is moist, purge what will cleanse his brain, as previously described in the division.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of linen like a circle, then place it on the centre of his head. The patient sits upright, holding it from each side. Then take one pound of aged sheep's butter.
Then heat it on the fire to a moderate heat, such that the finger can tolerate it if inserted.  Then pour it into the circle on the centre of his head, and leave it until it cools. Do this to the patient every week, along with good general management, until he is healed, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize him with many small, scattered burns, without holding the cautery in your hand, but rather by waving it; for this type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a linen cloth soaked in butter or chicken fat to him.
Chapter twelve.
On cauterizing water that flows from the eye.
If you ascertain the presence of water flowing from the eye by the signs mentioned in the classification, then immediately apply remedies to cleanse the head and protect it from all moisture, and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize it in the middle of the head, and cauterize twice on the temples, if the water is flowing from both eyes, or from one side if it is in one eye only.
Then, using the cautery, cut all the veins and arteries under the skin.  Let the cauterizations be lengthwise and across the temples, and take precautions to prevent bleeding.
If you see anything of it, cut it at the site with whatever treatment you can manage.  We will discuss in wisdom the art of cutting the arteries and avoiding bleeding.
And cauterize twice deeply at the back of the head, under the bones.
Chapter Thirteen.
In cauterizing chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and originate from veins and arteries on the surface of the head, externally, and you are certain that this is due to excessive cold, thick, phlegmatic matter, cauterize it with the cautery that I have described at the beginning of the descending water. One cautery in the middle of the head, and two cauteries on each temple, and two cauteries at the back of the head under the bones. If further cauterization is needed, cauterize on each side of the eye's tear duct at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
In cauterizing foul-smelling nose.
If treatment with what we have mentioned in the classification is unsuccessful, then quickly remove the patient's crusts for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle cautery with the olive oil cautery iron. Then cauterize with the nail cautery iron two cauteries above the eyebrows, slightly under the hair.  Avoid the artery; do not cut it.  This is the image of the nail cautery iron.
Chapter Fifteen.
In cauterizing relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid is relaxed due to disease or moisture, cauterize the eyelid with a single cautery using this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, cauterize slightly above the eyebrows, two cauteries on each side, spaced apart from the temples, and the length of each cautery should be the length of the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the cauterization, but only to the extent that one-third of the skin is burnt.  And the image of the cautery iron should be of this form.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If the eyelashes turn inward and injure the eye, then the cauterization has two types. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a burning medicine.  As for cauterizing it with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to remove his eyelashes if he is one who plucks them, until they are long and even.
If it has injured him when they are growing, bind his eyes with a bandage so they do not move until they grow.  Once they have grown and become even, place the patient's head in your lap, then mark the eyelid with ink in the shape of a leaf of a date palm.
Start the mark near the eyelashes, then place a cotton pad soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a lizard under the eyelid.  Then heat an iron, this is its form.
Then cauterize it, little by little, several times, in the shape that you marked, until the surface of the skin, which is entirely in the shape of a date palm leaf, is burned.  A sign of your successful work is that you will see the eyelid has shrunk and the hair has risen from the flesh of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days, then apply a cotton pad with ghee until the scab comes off.  Then treat it with ointment until he heals, if God wills.  If any hair grows back later and the eyelid relaxes.
Then repeat the cauterization at that spot as you did initially.  If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not prick the eye.  As for cauterization with the burning medicine, tell the patient to let the eyelashes grow long and straight.
Then make a shape of a date palm leaf from paper.  Then take ordinary soap and quicklime, each weighing a dirham or so, pulverize them thoroughly.  Do this quickly so they don't dry out. Then spread some of it on the paper you shaped like a date palm leaf and place it on one or both eyelids.  Place a cotton pad soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on the medicine and press it slightly, moving it whenever the patient feels the burning of the medicine, because he finds it burning like fire.  As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger.  When the burning subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And see if you find the eyelid has risen, as it rises when cauterized by fire or by cutting.  Otherwise, apply more medicine to the area where the medicine hasn't taken effect and hasn't blackened, until you complete your work and the eye is cauterized. Then place cotton with ghee on it to soothe the burning.
Then treat it with the palm ointment or another, until it heals.  You must be extremely careful during the procedure to prevent any medicine from falling into the eye.  If the eyelid relaxes after a few days, and you fear something like hair in the eye.
Then reapply the medicine to the relaxed area of the eyelid specifically, as you did at first, and treat it until it heals. This is a picture of a date palm leaf.
Know that people's eyes differ in size and age, so adjust your cauterization accordingly.  The correct method is not hidden from those who have expertise in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
Cauterization of the fistula that appears in the eye socket.
If you have treated the fistula as described in the sections on diseases, and your treatment has not been successful, then it is necessary to cauterize it in this manner.
Command the patient to place his head on your lap, and have an attendant hold his head firmly, ensuring no movement or shaking.
Then place a cotton ball moistened with egg white or the juice of the spurge plant over his eyes, then heat the cautery iron, which is depicted in this form.
It should be hollow, like a shaft of an eagle's feather from one end, the end used for cauterization. If you wish, it can be pierced on the other end, or if you wish, it can be solid, like a hot poker.  However, this hollow type is better for your work, God willing.
Then, squeeze the fistula if it is open, removing the pus and drying it, or if it is not open, open it and remove its pus.  Then, place the extremely hot cautery iron on it, holding it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
And when cauterizing, move your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose, so that your hand does not miss the mark or disturb the patient, and the cautery iron does not fall onto the eyelid and damage it. If you reach the bone on the first cauterization, then stop; otherwise, reheat the cautery iron a second time if needed.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton ball with ghee and treat it with a drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply a strong, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed, and treat it as described in its section, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing the fistula, mentioned by some early writers, is to carefully cut open the fistula and then insert a thin tube, a funnel, into the opening.  This is its illustration.
Pour in molten lead weighing one dirham.  Hold the funnel firmly in your hand, ensuring the patient remains completely still so that the molten lead does not run into their eye.  It is necessary to place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or water over the patient's eye.
The lead will burn the fistula and cure it remarkably. If the fistula heals with the cauterization and treatment described, then stop.  Otherwise, the nostrils must be pierced, and the fistula returned to the nasal passage, as will be described in the specific section on that procedure.
The Eighteenth Chapter.
On cauterizing a cleft lip.
A cleft, called a fissure, often occurs in the lip, particularly in the lips of young boys.  If, after treating this cleft with the methods described in the previous sections, the treatment is unsuccessful, take a small, knife-like cautery iron and apply it in this way.
Make its [the cautery's] cavity as thin as a knife, then quickly place it hot in the same cleft, until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft. Then treat it with qīrūṭī (a type of ointment) until it heals, God willing.
The Nineteenth Chapter.
On cauterizing a nasal fistula in the mouth.
If a tumor appears at the base of the gums, or on the palate, or at the roots of the teeth, then it ulcerates and bursts, and a fistula forms from the running pus. If treatment is unsuccessful, then a cautery iron should be heated to fit the fistula.
Then insert it [the heated cautery] into the hole of the fistula and hold it until the heated iron reaches its depth and end. Do this once or twice, then treat it afterward with the previously mentioned treatment until it heals, God willing.
If the matter stops and heals, then good. Otherwise, the area must be examined, and the decayed bone removed according to what will be mentioned in its section, God willing.
The twentieth chapter.
For loosening teeth and gums.
If the gums have loosened due to moisture and the teeth have moved, and treatment with medications has been ineffective, then place the patient's head on your lap, and then heat the cautery, whose image will come after this, after placing the tube on the tooth.
Quickly insert the hot cautery, and hold your hand for a little while until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth. Then lift your hand, and repeat the cautery several times as desired. Then, the patient should fill their mouth with salt water.
He should hold it for an hour and spit it out.  Then the loose tooth will become firm, and the relaxed gums will tighten, and the corrupt moisture will dry up.
The twenty-first chapter.
On cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is caused by cold or worms, and medication is ineffective, cauterization can be performed in two ways: either with oil or with fire.
As for cauterization with butter, take cow's butter, boil it in an iron spoon or a shell, then take a cotton ball, wrap it around the end of a rod, dip it into the boiled butter, and apply it to the aching tooth quickly, holding it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth.  If desired, dip wool or cotton in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, then put the heated iron on top to reach the base of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, use a brass or iron tube, somewhat thick-walled to prevent the heat from reaching the mouth of the patient. Then heat the cautery iron and place it on the same tooth, holding it until it cools, repeating this process several times.
The pain will disappear either on that same day or the next. After the cauterization, the patient should fill their mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out.  This is the image of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you choose, as appropriate.  This is the image of the tube.

Chapter Twenty-seven.
On cauterizing the cold liver.

Chapter Twenty-eight.
Cauterization of a liver tumor.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know if the tumor is in the liver's flesh or its membrane, if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will experience a heaviness and pain, but without severity.
But if it is in the liver's membrane, the pain will be accompanied by intense severity, and if you find that the doctors have exhausted their efforts to treat it, the patient should lie on their back, and then mark the swollen area with ink.
Then, the cautery, which resembles a chisel, is heated in the fire.  This is its image. Cauterize it with a single cautery, so as to burn all the skin and reach the membrane, so that all the pus is expelled, then treat it with the treatment for abscesses until it heals.
This type of cautery should only be performed by someone whose experience in medicine is extensive, and who has repeatedly treated these diseases, for only then should one undertake such a procedure.  To avoid it is preferable.
Chapter Twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients recommended cauterizing a cold fistula with the roots of the zara'und in this manner: Take a single, longest, dried zara'und root, as thick as a finger, dip it in oil, and then hold it in the fire. Then cauterize once between the collarbone and the neck, and two small cauterizations a little below the veins, angled towards the area under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, angled slightly backward; another cauterization in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach.  Three cauterizations from behind, one between the shoulders, and two on the sides of the loins, below the cauterization between the ribs.
One should not make the cauterization deep, but rather on the surface of the skin, as a slight burning. Some of the ancients have mentioned that some people used an iron cautery similar to a saw, heating it and inserting it between the ribs until it reached the tumor itself.
The suppuration will come out, as we mentioned with the liver tumor, and in this case, with cauterization of the abscess, the patient may die immediately, or a fistula may develop at the site, with no cure.
Chapter Thirty.
In cauterization of the spleen.
If, after treating spleen disease using the methods previously described, your treatment proves ineffective, cauterization can be applied in three ways, all of which are valid: one is to cauterize the spleen three or four times in a row along its length.
In the same manner as the cauterization of the liver, described previously, the cauterizations should be spaced a finger's breadth apart or slightly more.  Use the same type of cauterizing tool as for the liver. Do not insert your hand deeply into the cauterization, and the patient should be lying on their back.
The other method involves the elbow.  Raise the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's elbow ends, raising the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left elbow ends; raise the skin the width of the body so that the cauterizations run the length of the body.
Then, insert the cautery probes, very carefully, until they penetrate the skin on the other side; then, remove the cautery.  The cauterizations will be four. If you wish, you can use the three-probe cautery instead.
Then treat the cauterized area after allowing it to discharge pus for many days; this is more effective than any other treatment previously mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-one.
Concerning the cauterization of dropsy.
Cauterization is effective in dropsy of the abdomen, especially. If you have treated the patient with various treatments as described in the divisions and your treatment has not been effective, you should cauterize the abdomen four times around the navel and once on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver and another on the spleen.  Two more behind the back, one opposite his chest and one opposite his stomach.  The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin.  Then, leave the cauterization open to discharge pus for a long time.
Do not neglect the patient's treatment after the cauterization, as is appropriate to gather the necessary benefits so that healing may be expedited.  The shape of the cauterizing iron used for the back is pointed, as described previously. The shape of the cauterizing iron for the abdomen is oval.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.

Leave the cauterizations open without treatment for a long time, so that the water drains out. Then treat them with the other methods we mentioned.

Chapter Thirty-six.
On the cauterization of the fistula that occurs in the perineum and its surrounding areas.

Chapter Forty-One.
On cauterizing the sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint, caused by cold and dampness, and you have treated the ailment as described in the section, but the treatment is ineffective and the condition persists, then it is necessary to thin the thick humors with rotten seed, or cactus seed, or similar.
Then cauterize.  The cauterization has two types: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.  Cauterization with fire is in many forms, one of which is to cauterize on the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations, like this.
And this will create a beneficial depth.  The distance between each cauterization should be the width of a finger. The cautery should be olive-colored.  And one may cauterize one cauterization in the middle, on the head of the hip joint itself, making four cauterizations.  Or, if you wish, cauterize with the circular method mentioned previously for hip dislocation, using only one cauterization.
So that the circle surrounds the entire hip, burning the entire thickness of the skin. This is the description of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, then cauterize with two cauterizations on the thigh, at the location the patient indicates as painful, and one cauterization above the heel, a little towards the outer side, with four fingers' distance. The cautery should be knife-like, and the depth of the cauterization should only be the thickness of the skin.
And if he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize there with a pinpoint cautery three or four times, or more if necessary. If he indicates pain below the knee, towards the leg, cauterize there with a single, knife-like cauterization.
Be careful in all your cauterizations not to reach a nerve or major artery, lest you inflict a serious injury or prolonged affliction on the patient.
I have observed cases of cauterization above the heel where the cauterization was excessive, causing gangrene of the leg, reaching the foot and causing its complete perforation, thus ruining the entire leg.  Subsequently, diarrhea and death ensued. If the pain is in both areas, cauterize them in the same manner.
Some scholars have described a hip cauterization technique using a metal cup-like instrument.  Its diameter is half a span, and its opening is the thickness of a date pit or slightly less.  Inside this cup is another cup, and a third cup within that.
The cups are spaced apart by the width of a thumb's knuckle.  The cups are open on both sides and are about one or two knuckles high.  A handle of iron is securely attached to the cups. This is its form.
Then, it is heated in the fire until it glows red and emits sparks.  Then, it is placed on the hip of the patient, who is lying on their right side.  Three circular cauterizations are applied at once.  The patient is left for three days, and the wound is dressed with oil.  The wound is left open for several days.
Thereafter, it is treated with ointment until healed.  The author of this book stated that this type of cauterization is rarely used due to its unpleasantness and frightening appearance.  Few can bear it; however, it is a good cauterization for those who can endure it and for whom it is appropriate.
As for cauterization with burning medications, it involves creating two cups resembling the throat where the hinges of a door are placed, made of copper or iron.
Their height will be about two knuckles, or thereabouts.  The space between them is the thickness of a finger.  The bottom and top are open, and they are joined together in this manner.
Then, they are placed on the hip of the patient, who is lying on their right side.  Hold your hand gently.  Then, pour a little heated, sharp water between the circles. Hold it for about a sandglass's worth of time. The patient must endure the burning and stinging sensation; it feels like fire.
Until the stinging subsides.  Then, remove the instrument and wipe all the sharp water from the hip with fresh water.  Leave it for three days, then apply oil to it until the blackness of the burn disappears. Leave it for several days, allowing the pus to drain, for this is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold in the same way as you did for the hip, for what holds the water.  This is the description of sharp water, and its efficacy is proven in my treatise on the improvement of medicines.
Take one part of lye and one part of quicklime.  Grind them together and place them in a new pot with a single small hole at the bottom, large enough for a pestle to enter. Place another glazed pot beneath the bottom of the first pot.
Pour fresh water over the lye and quicklime until it covers them by a finger's breadth, after pressing them well with your hand.  Leave the pot until the sharp water descends into the glazed pot below. Then collect all of that water and pour it over fresh lye and quicklime.
The resulting water will then be very potent, effective in many medical treatments and cauterizing other parts of the body, because it acts like fire itself.  Some remedies for cauterizing the hip include, for instance,  thavsiya, balaذir honey, and lime mixed with soap, all ground into a paste.
Galen, recounting an ancient remedy, claimed it was a cure for hip pain and sciatica.  He greatly esteemed it, claiming that it needed no other treatment and cured in a single application.
So much so that perhaps a man would be carried into the bath and emerge cured.  The method is to take fresh green sițraj, or if not available, fresh dried sițraj, finely grind it with some fat, and apply it to the hip where the pain is, or the leg, or the thigh.
Then bandage it and leave it for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning subside. Afterward, take him into the bath. When his body is moist, place him in a basin. The pain will depart, and he will be healed, God willing. If not, repeat the dressing ten days later.
He will be healed, God willing.  Understand that no one should use these remedies for cauterization unless the body is empty.  Dioscorides mentioned that goat's dung, when used for cauterizing sciatica, is beneficial.
The cauterization should be performed as follows: Take wool, soak it in old oil, and place it on the deep area between the thumb and the forearm, closer to the forearm.  Then take dry goat's dung.
Heat it in the fire until it becomes embers, then place it on the wool and leave it until it extinguishes. Repeat this until sensation between the mid-forearm and the hip subsides, and the pain is relieved, God willing. This type of cauterization is called cauterization with dung.

Chapter Forty-five.
On the cauterization of hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin, and some of the intestines and bowels descend into the testicle, and this has happened recently, the patient should be told to abstain from eating that day and to use laxatives to evacuate his bowels.
Then, he should lie on his back before you, and you should tell him to restrain himself until the bowel or intestines come out.  Then, you push them back in with your finger.  Then, mark with ink under the hernia, on the pubic bone, a mark resembling a semicircle, with its ends pointing towards the upper part of the body.
Then, heat a cautery iron, this is its form.
Until it becomes white, emitting sparks.  Then, push the intestines or bowels back into his abdomen.  Then, a servant should place his hand over the area so that the intestines do not come out again.  A space has been created between the patient's legs, and a pillow has been placed underneath. Another servant holds his legs together.
And another on his chest holds his hands. Then, place the cautery iron on the same mark, with your hand holding the cautery iron upright until it reaches the bone, or repeat the process if it did not reach the bone the first time.
Take care to prevent the intestines from protruding while cauterizing, so that you do not burn them, causing either death or a great injury to the patient.  Know that if you do not reach the bone with the cauterization, your work will not succeed.  The cautery iron should be appropriate in size for children and for adults.
Then, treat the cauterized area with oil after three days to remove the harshness of the burn, then treat it with other ointments until it heals.  Let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound is closed.

If he follows this treatment, he will be completely cured, God willing.  I will describe the treatment of hernias by incision in its appropriate section, God willing.  As for a hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is still starting, if you want to prevent it from getting worse, cauterize it with a circular cautery appropriate to its size. Chapter Forty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula.

Chapter Forty-seven.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
As for those afflicted with leprosy, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially those cases of leprosy that arise from the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.

One cauterization on the tip of the nose; two on each cheek; two on the cervical vertebrae; six on the thoracic vertebrae; one large one on the coccyx, at the base of the spine, and another above it on the same area; and two on each thigh, one on each hip.
