Know that the patient does not experience pain from the cauterizations as much as a healthy person, because his body is numb.  The cauteries should be of various sizes, according to the organs and joints, as described in the properties of the irons.
Then treat the cauterization with fine crushed carob, along with honey and other treatments, until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter Forty-eight.
Concerning cauterizing numbed areas: If a part of the body is numb and treated with medicines, ointments, and bandages, but does not heal, cauterize the same numb area with cauterizations appropriate to the size of the affected part of the body, taking care to keep the cauterizations slightly below the surface of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  Cauterization may also be used for some numbness that affects the hand or foot, in the spinal vertebrae near the exit point of the nerve that controls that limb, to eliminate the numbness.
Only those knowledgeable about the anatomy of the body and the exits of the nerves that move the body should undertake this.
Chapter Forty-nine.
On cauterizing leprosy.
If leprosy persists despite all medical treatments, cauterize it with a slight depth, proportionate to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and the color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose ointment,  horsetail leaves, dove blood, or hawk's blood, one part of each. Mix them all together, apply to a cloth, and bind to the affected area until healed.
Chapter Fifty.
On cauterizing cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it turns. Some physicians have suggested a forceful cauterization in the center, but I do not think this is advisable, as I anticipate ulceration, and have observed such cases repeatedly.
Therefore, the correct approach is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we said, or with multiple small cauterizations.
Chapter Fifty-one.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If a fistula has occurred and is slow to mature, either due to the inherent nature of the condition, the age of the patient (if elderly and anemic), or the passage of time, and you wish to accelerate the fistula's maturation, cauterize around it with numerous small, pinpoint cauterizations.
Then leave it, for this will hasten its maturation. If you wish to further cauterize it, heat the cautery iron to this [specific] temperature.
Place it in the center of the fistula until it penetrates the skin, and let the cauterization be from below, to facilitate the flow of pus. Then treat it as necessary until healed.
Chapter fifty-two.
On cauterizing ulcers.
Ulcers are a corruption that spreads through a limb, consuming it like fire consumes dry wood.  If you see an ulcer in a place suitable for cauterization with fire, heat a cautery iron, using many small and large nails, according to what is appropriate for the location of the ulcer.
Then cauterize it from all sides until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing remains.  Then leave it for three days, and apply powdered sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scab and corruption are removed.
Then treat it with healing ointments. If after three days you see that the flesh grows healthy without corruption, then leave it alone. Otherwise, repeat the cauterization on any remaining affected areas.  The ulcer may also be treated with a strong medicine.
For it serves the same purpose as fire, more quickly effective.  I have mentioned the treatment of ulcers with strong medicine in the classifications; take it from there when needed.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This affliction, a rough, painful condition, frequently occurs on the soles of the feet.  Cauterization can be performed in two ways: either with fire or with a strong caustic liquid.
As for cauterization with fire, heat a hollowed-out cautery iron, resembling an eagle's wing, crafted from metal, to completely encircle the nail.  The edges should be thin.  Then lower it, hot, onto the nail, and rotate your hand with the cautery iron around the nail until the cautery reaches the depth of the nail.
Leave it for three days to begin to suppurate. Then dress it with crushed wild barley mixed with salt, leaving the dressing on overnight.  This will remove it from its roots. Then treat the wound with a healing ointment until healed; if the nails are not inverted.
It often occurs on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet.  Therefore, one should take a tube of copper, iron, or eagle's wing, and place it on the nail or wart.  Then pour a small amount of strong liquid into the tube.
Hold the tube with your hand, gently rotating it, so that the edges of the tube affect the root of the nail, and the strong liquid finds its way to the root of the nail.  The patient should endure the stinging of the strong liquid for a short time.
Then leave it; the nail will come out by its roots.  Do this one by one until all those in the body are dealt with.  Then treat the affected areas after they are removed with ointments that promote healing.
Chapter Forty-five.
In the treatment of the scabies.
If scabies occurs due to cold in the nerves or from a fever of a quarter or other cause, it is necessary to cauterize four or five times on the back, between each cauterization, cauterizing on the chest as well. And cauterize his stomach with an olive oil cautery; the scabies will subside and hasten the maturation of the cold illness.
Chapter Fifty-five.
In the cauterization of a developing boil on the body.
Sometimes ugly boils may arise in the body from thick, corrupt cold materials.  At their first appearance, a gentle cauterization should be applied to the head of each boil using a burning stick, a long asparagus stem, or a lens-shaped cautery.
Boils may be cauterized in this manner at their first appearance, so that the active cause of them does not increase and the patient is cured; however, this should be done only after the patient has been bled.
Chapter Fifty-six.
In cauterizing bleeding occurring when an artery is cut.
Bleeding frequently occurs from an artery that has been cut in a wound that is exposed from the outside, or when a tumor or a body part is incised or cauterized, and similar cases; and it is difficult to stop the bleeding.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger over the mouth of the artery and press it gently until the blood is contained beneath your finger and nothing escapes. Then, place several small and large olive-wood cauteries in the fire and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then, take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the location where the artery has been opened, and place the cautery on the same vein after quickly removing your finger and holding the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If the bleeding spurts out when you remove your finger from the mouth of the artery and the cautery cools down, quickly take another cautery from the ones in the prepared fire and continue doing this one after another until the bleeding stops.
And be cautious not to burn a nerve, for this would cause another affliction for the patient.  Know that if an artery is bleeding, it cannot be cut, especially if it is large, except by one of four methods: either by cauterization, as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already been severed. If it is severed, its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop; or it can be tied with strong threads; or medications that can stop the bleeding and constrict the vessels can be applied.
As for those who attempt to cut it with a bandage, or by constricting it with rags, or by applying burning things, and the like, it will not be cut in that way at all, except rarely. If someone encounters such a situation and is not attended by a physician or medicine, then they should immediately place their index finger on the mouth of the wound itself, as we described.
He should press it firmly until the blood is contained and flows from above the wound, over the artery and the finger.  The finger should not be removed until the blood solidifies and thickens, and the bleeding stops. During this time, one should observe what cauterization or medication is needed.
The Second Chapter.
On incisions, lancing, phlebotomy, and surgical procedures in general.
In the previous chapter, we discussed all diseases that are suitable for cauterization with fire and burning medications, their causes and reasons, and the tools and methods of cauterization, presenting this material as chapters from the heel to the foot.
And I will follow the same path in this chapter to make the desired material easier for the student to understand.
Before I begin, you must know, my children, that this chapter contains even more uncertainty than the first chapter on cauterization.
Therefore, the caution must be greater, for in this chapter, the act often leads to the emptying of blood, which is essential for life, when opening a vein, making an incision on a tumor, lancing an abscess, treating a wound, extracting an arrow, or cutting into a stone, or similar procedures. All of these are accompanied by risk and fear, and death often results.
I advise you to avoid any questionable actions, for in this craft, you may encounter people suffering from various illnesses.  Some may be weary of their disease and consider death as a relief.
Because of the severity of their illness and the length of their suffering, there are symptoms that point to death.  Some may offer you their wealth, hoping for health, but their illness is deadly; therefore, you should not be aided by those of this character in any way.
Let your caution be stronger than your desire and eagerness. Do not undertake anything without certain knowledge of the favorable outcome.  Use knowledge and warnings about the potential outcome of healing in all treatment of your patients.
For in this, you will gain praise, glory, remembrance, and thanks. May God guide you, my children, with wisdom, and not deprive you of correctness and success.  There is no god but Him.
I have arranged this chapter into sections, following the previous chapter on cauterization, from the head to the foot, so that you will find what you need, God willing.
Chapter One.
Concerning the treatment of water that collects in the heads of children.
This ailment often affects infants at birth if the midwife applies pressure to the baby's head without gentleness. It may also arise from a hidden cause that is unknown.
I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than infants, and all those I saw who were affected died quickly.  Therefore, I deem it best to abandon treatment.  I have seen an infant whose head had filled with water, and the head enlarged each day. Until the infant could not sit up by himself due to the great size of his head, and the moisture increased until he perished.
This moisture either collects between the skin and bone, or beneath the bone on the periosteum. If the moisture is between the skin and bone, and the swelling is small, a single incision should be made across the center of the head, approximately two spans long, so that the moisture drains.  This is the method of incision.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling larger, make two intersecting incisions in this manner:
If the moisture is under the bone, and the sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides, and the water sinks when you press it with your hand inwards, this is not hidden from you. Then, you should make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner.
After the incision, all the moisture will drain out. Then, the incisions should be tightened with strips and bandages.  Afterwards, the head should be wrapped with dressings, and oil applied until the fifth day. Then, the bandages should be removed, and the wound treated with ointments and compresses.  Maintain a moderate bandage on the head and feed the patient only dry, low-moisture foods.
Until the limb strengthens and heals. Another method of incision is to look where the bone of the swelling and accumulation of water appears, for it may be located more in the back of the head, or in the front, or to the right, or to the left.
You should aim your incision where the swelling and water accumulation have appeared.  Incise as best you can, being careful not to cut an artery, lest a hemorrhage occur and the patient dies from the bleeding, having expelled the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Severing the two arteries behind the ears, known as the "hissers".
If someone experiences a severe affliction in the eyes or chest, and this lasts a long time without being cured by medicines, the best treatment in this case is to cut these two arteries.
If you wish to cut them, you should first shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the artery.  Then, tie the patient's neck with their garment.  Look for where the vein pulsates, in the two recessed areas behind the ears.  They are rarely hidden, except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, and cut them with a dissecting knife, severing them to the bone.  This should be done at the side of the head. If you wish, insert the knife under the artery and cut upwards. The cut should be about two clenched fingers long.
If the vein is cut, blood will flow out rhythmically, surging forward and continuously. If the artery is not visible, you should measure from the ear approximately three fingers' breadth, mark it with ink, and cut to the bone.  And what is necessary...
About six ounces of blood will likely flow at once, though perhaps more or less, depending on the patient's strength and the fullness of their arteries.  Then, examine the wound. If any of the peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it off.
So that a hot swelling does not develop, then bandage the wound with a linen thread, and treat with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
The arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from a chronic headache or acute attacks of severe heat and humidity affecting the muscles of the temples, or a severe chronic headache, and similar afflictions, and treatment by various medical methods is ineffective, then we have tried in these cases to cauterize or dissect the arteries of the temples as described.
The procedure for dissecting them is that the patient's hair in the temples should be shaved, and the visible artery in the temple should be located, as its pulse will be apparent.  It is rarely hidden, except in exceptional cases.
Or, if the artery is hidden due to extreme cold, the patient should tighten his neck with his clothing, then you should rub the area with a cloth or apply warm water until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the lancet, which is of this form. Then carefully dissect the skin with the lancet until you reach the artery.  Then insert a hook and pull it upward, until you remove it from the skin and free it from the membranes beneath it on all sides. If the artery is thin, twist it with the end of the hook. Then cut off a section of it, large enough for its ends to separate and contract, without causing bleeding.  If it is not severed and disconnected, the blood will not actually recede.  Then drain the blood, from six to three ounces.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong doubled thread, whether of silk or bowstrings, so that putrefaction does not rapidly occur before the wound heals, causing bleeding.
Then cut off the excess between the ligatures.  Do this at that time, or at a later time. If you wish, cauterize it to the bone with a scalpel-like cautery, so that its edges are severed. This will achieve the same result, or even a better one, as we have said.
However, if the patient has a fever or a heated temperament, cauterization is helpful in consuming moisture, and therefore even more beneficial.  After dissecting the arteries, the area should be packed with old cotton and covered with firm bandages.  After healing, treat it with dry medications that promote flesh regeneration and with lint until it heals, God willing.
If during your procedure bleeding occurs from the artery, promptly cut it, either by cautery or by filling the area with alum and tightening your hand until the bleeding stops.  If you lack any of these things, place your finger until the blood coagulates.
Apply very cold water to the area until the pain subsides and the tissue tightens as it should.  A less invasive and easier method than dissecting the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the two spots with ink.
Heat it very intensely until it reaches the bone, and cauterize the vein in two places so that the distance between its ends is widened.  It will not adhere with this cautery.  This is an image of the cautery.
Its blades should be sharp, similar to small daggers, but significantly less sharp than a knife. If they were as sharp as a knife, the cold would reach them quickly, and the flesh would not separate rapidly. If they have a bit of thickness, the heat of the fire will hold in them.
This quickly cuts the flesh, and is the best, easiest, and simplest method. The distance between the blades should be the thickness of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
In treating the constant, hot, running of tears from the eyes.
If the tears are constant and flowing from the veins on the skull's exterior, and no medicinal treatment helps, and you see the patient's face has reddened. He feels a crawling sensation on his forehead, like ants crawling, and his eyes are sunken, moist, their lashes eroded and their eyelids glued shut due to the intensity of the tears.  Earlier practitioners of medicine resorted to this procedure for treatment, calling it "treatment with the sword."  It involves instructing the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then, three parallel incisions are made on the forehead, along its length. Each incision is about two fingers long, aligned with the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second one slightly offset from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from the contact between the jaws.  After each incision, maintain a distance of three fingers clasped together.  Have with you prepared sponge or many dry cloths to absorb the blood.
Then insert the sharp-pointed lancet, which has this shape. From the incision next to the temple, to the middle incision, you scrape away all the skin between the incisions, along with the periosteum on the bone.  Then, do the same from the middle incision to the third incision.  Then remove the lancet. And insert into the first incision another instrument, called a scalpel, sharp on one side and smooth and blunt on the other. This is its shape.
Turn the sharp side of the scalpel towards the flesh adhering to the skin, and the smooth side towards the bone. Push it until it reaches the middle incision, cutting all vessels descending from the head to the eyes, without reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do this from the middle incision to the other incision.  After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, press the areas of clotted blood.  Then place a cotton wick in each incision, and apply a bandage soaked in a mixture of liquid, oil, or vinegar and oil.
To prevent inflammation.  On the third day, dissolve the ligaments and apply plenty of lukewarm water.  Then treat with basil ointment mixed with rose oil and other remedies for wounds until healed, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When someone suffers from frequent, severe, persistent discharges, and their eyes appear sunken and small, their vision weakened, and their eyelids ulcerated with eyelashes falling out. And there will be severe, painful pain deep within the head, and continuous sneezing.  Recognize from these symptoms that these materials and discharges come from many deep veins.  The best treatment for this is to order the patient to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there will be severe, painful pain deep within the head, and continuous sneezing.  Recognize from these symptoms that these materials and discharges come from many deep veins.  The best treatment for this is to order the patient to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it slightly horizontally, beginning the incision from the left temple to the right temple, and the incision should be to the bone. Keep your hand away from the moving temporal muscles when chewing. Once the bone is exposed and all the blood is wiped away with a sponge...
Then separate the edges of the incision with worn cotton or linen thread.  Afterwards, apply compresses and soak them with medicine and oil to prevent a hot swelling.  Once the inflammation has subsided, and you see that the hot swelling has resolved, you should rub the bone until the growth of flesh begins there.
Then treat it with a dried preparation that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts wheat flour and four parts galbanum, preparing a salve from them, and using it to promote flesh growth in wounds of this type.
Chapter Six.
Concerning the treatment of things that fall into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or a substance similar to a stone, such as iron or glass; or a plant seed, such as chickpeas, seeds, and the like; or a liquid, such as water and vinegar, and the like.
Or, if it is an animal, if a pebble or something similar that doesn't grow in the ear has fallen in, expose the ear to the sun.  If you see the pebble, apply a little violet or cerucean oil to it, and then try to remove it by moving the head or by inducing sneezing with a blunt instrument. And close the nostrils when a sneeze comes, after placing a bandage of cloth or wool around the ear and tilting the ear upwards.  This treatment often removes it. If it does not come out, try to remove it with a gentle forceps, the kind of which this is a depiction.
If it does not come out with the forceps, then try to remove it with a gentle, slightly bent, blunt probe.  If it does not come out with that either, make a copper tube and insert the end of the tube into the ear opening, gently.
Seal the area around the tube with softened pitch mixed with oil, so that the air has no path other than the tube.  Then pull it out with a strong pull.  It often comes out this way. If it does not come out by the methods described, then take a small piece of the gum of the Nabataeans or of the gum used to catch birds, and place it on the end of the instrument.
After wrapping it tightly with lint, then gently insert it into the ear opening after the ear is dry.  If it does not come out by all the methods described, then proceed to incision before a hot swelling or contraction occurs.
The method of incision is to first bleed the patient at the temples, drawing out blood according to their strength.  Then, have the patient sit before you, tilting their ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear, near the cartilage, in the lowest part.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone.  Then remove it with whatever instruments you can.  Then stitch the incision immediately and treat it until it heals.
And if the object fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, attempt to remove it using the methods mentioned. If it does not come out, then take a thin, delicate forceps of this form.
Try to cut that type of fallen seed in the ear with it, but only do this if you are sure the seed has become dampened by the ear's vapor so that it is broken into many small pieces.  Then remove it with a blind probe, a fine wick, or by suction.
As mentioned, it is easier to remove it.  As for water that has entered the ear, the patient should first use a nasal insufflator (kunds) to sneeze.  They should have filled their ear with some old cotton, while lying on the ear with the water, and if it comes out in this way...
Otherwise, take many small, smooth pebbles, warm them slightly by fire, and have the patient insert one into the ear canal.  The patient should lean on one leg on that side, and strike another stone against the stone in the ear.
He should continue doing this with pebble after pebble until all the water is expelled.  Water might also be expelled by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and burning the other end until it is mostly burned, then repeating with another feather.
Repeat this until all the water is expelled, or draw it out with a tube, as previously described with the pebble.  As for removing an animal that has entered the ear, if it is small, like a flea or similar, treat it as described in the classification.  As for...
If the creature is large and visible, try to remove it with linen cloths and tweezers.  Removing it is easier than anything else that might be in the ear.  As for removing worms that have developed in the ear, if treatment with what we have mentioned in the classification in the section on insects has not been successful.
Then you should look into the ear in the sun.  If you see anything of the worm, remove it with linen or fine tweezers. If nothing appears, take a tube, of this shape.
Narrow at the bottom, wide at the top.  Insert the thin end into the ear as much as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly with it, repeating until all the worms are expelled. If it does not come out, seal around the tube with wax, as I described to you in the case of the pebble.
If it does not come out using the above methods, use the insects described.  Earlier practitioners have used them to kill the worms, as you will find in the section on insects.  Applying ointments and medications to the ear can be done with this instrument, of this shape.
As you see, make it of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, wide at the top.  If you wish, the barrel inside the tube can be made of solid copper.  If you wish, you can take a spindle and wrap tightly with cotton at its end.
Then place the ointment, or juice, or any medication you desire, into the tube, while it is in the ear. Then, insert the spindle wrapped with cotton from above, and gently squeeze with your hand until the ointment flows into the ear cavity and is felt.
Have the patient inside, and whatever you place in the ear, warm it slightly in the fire. Beware that the thing you are dripping into it is not excessively cold or hot, as the ear cannot tolerate it.
Chapter Seven.
In the treatment of ear blockage.
Some children may emerge from their mothers' wombs with their ear canals not pierced.
Some people may also experience blockage in the ear canal due to a wound or growing flesh within it.  This blockage can occur deep within the ear canal, beyond the sight's reach.
It may also be present slightly outside the canal and visible.  Blockages deep within the ear, invisible to the eye, are more difficult to treat.  If the blockage is visible, then one should place the patient's ear in the sun. And look at it. If you see the blockage outwardly, then open it with a gentle scalpel.  This is its appearance:
Its tip should have a small, limited protrusion, and the rest of the scalpel should be smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the blockage is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it gently until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, take a smooth, gentle probe, warm it slightly in fire, and then insert it into the ear canal.  If you feel the blockage and the patient experiences heaviness of hearing, remove it gently.
Be careful not to injure the ear membrane. Then, place a wick into the ear, sized to fit the hole, and apply Egyptian ointment. Repeat this for several days until the wound is safe from adhesions.  Alternatively, soak a wick in water and sprinkle powdered za'aj on it.
Use this.  If you see the ear has become swollen with a hot swelling, then remove the wick and replace it with another wick soaked in a concoction made with rose oil, so as to alleviate the hot swelling. Then continue treatment until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear.  Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding mentioned in the chapter on hemorrhages.
Chapter Eight.
On treating warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  They should be grasped with a forceps or a needle and cut off at their roots with a scalpel, then a powdered zage is applied to the area.
If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-shaped cautery iron.  Cauterization is better after the excision.  It often returns if any part of the root remains.  If cauterized with fire, the roots are burned and it does not return.
Chapter Nine.
On treating colds affecting the eyelids.
A condition resembling a cold in its severity and firmness often appears on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called a cold, which is the accumulation of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids.  If the cold is protruding on the surface of the eyelid, moving in every direction, the treatment is simple: make a transverse incision.
Then carefully peel it away from every side until it detaches, and then hang it up by a hook and cut it. If cutting it is not possible except after penetrating the eyelid with the cut, that will do no harm to the patient. If the cut is large, bring the edges together with sutures and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no need to worry about it, as ointment will fix and mend it. If the cold is leaning inwards towards the inner surface of the eyelid, turn the eyelid inside out, and hang the cold up with a hook without needing an incision, and trim it from all sides.
If the eyelid is penetrated by the cut, that will not harm anything. Then wash the eye with salt water after cutting the cold, and treat the area with what will cause healing until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
On the treatment of the sty that appears on the eyelid.
A sty is a swelling that occurs in the layers of the upper eyelid. It is most common in children, and it weighs down their eyes and causes them colds in the night hours, and they are unable to look at sunlight. Because tears come quickly to them.
Therefore, you see them leaning on their faces, and they sleep on them, and their eyelids beneath their eyebrows are damp, possibly swollen, and the sty is clearly visible.  If you press the area with your fingers, you feel the sty between them.  The treatment is to have the patient rest their head on your lap.
Then take a linen cloth, prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick to fit around the sty on all sides.  Then place it on the sty and press with your fingers on all sides to collect the sty in the center of the circle.
Then, with a lancet, make a horizontal incision through the middle of that moisture, an incision no larger than the incision made in bloodletting.  As for depth, it is necessary to cut through the entire skin until you reach the sty.
In most cases, the sty emerges from the incision as a white piece of fat.  Pull it out with a cloth wrapped around your fingers, moving your hand back and forth until it heals.  Be careful not to enlarge the incision, lest the lancet injure the eye.
If the sty does not appear immediately after the initial incision, gently increase the incision a little until the sty protrudes.  Then, pull it out as we described.  Then, dip a cloth in vinegar and water and apply it to the area, securing it with a bandage. Some people crush salt.
Place it in the incision to dissolve any remaining moisture, then treat it until it heals.  If a hot swelling occurs at the site, treat it with cooling compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On types of eye dressings.
If superfluous eyelashes grow on the eyelid, outside the natural course, beneath the natural eyelashes, and are chronic, they harm the eye and cause various illnesses such as persistent tearing, eyelid relaxation, white discoloration, and thickening, ultimately causing the eye to become useless.
Eye dressings are of four types: either by cauterization with fire, or by a sharp medicine, as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization; or the dressing may be by cutting and stitching, or by applying reeds, as I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns, then insert a needle with thread underneath the eyelid and pierce the needle and thread through the upper eyelid, near the hair itself. Pull the thread upwards on the eyelid.
Turn it with a spatula, then cut the inside of the eyelid, away from the extra hair, using a blunt scalpel from the larger to smaller incision, then pull the thread and place a small pad of cotton or cloth underneath the eyelid, then stitch the eyelid with thread.
It should resemble the shape of an asa leaf, but the shape should be according to the amount of eyelid elevation required, as this may vary between people. Some may require a specific amount of eyelid tissue to be cut, depending on the extent of eyelid relaxation.
Some may require a smaller cut, all according to the degree of eyelid relaxation.  Then, using the scalpel, cut along the lines that you marked, starting from the larger incision to the smaller incision.  Each cut should be near the natural hair, approximately the thickness of a spatula.
Then, insert the forceps into one of the skin's corners and scrape off the whole skin, then stitch the two edges together using a fine wool needle and thread, and wipe away the blood.  Adhere any remaining thread to the eyebrows with adhesive substances.
You may or may not perform this procedure; it is up to you. Then, the sutures and threads remain for about three or four days, and then treat it.  If you prefer, leave the wound unstitched and treat it with something that dries and contracts it, as the eyelid will elevate when the wound is closed.  Suturing is preferable.
This method of the operation of the upper eyelid, previously described, entails some effort for the patient, but is a good method with no danger.  Another approach to the operation of the upper eyelid is to shape the eyelid like an asa leaf, as we have described. Then raise the eyelid with three forceps, either separate or grouped in this way.
Then, cut off the excess eyelid with small scissors, in this manner, with careful precision.
If you are unable to hold the forceps and the shaping is not satisfactory, take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape. Insert another thread near the larger opening and a third near the smaller opening.  Hold the threads evenly with your fingers and then lift your hand in a steady manner.
Lift all of the marked skin as described, then stitch the edges of the wound and treat it until healed.  If a hot swelling appears at the time of cutting or incision, relieve that swelling with *qīrūṭī* or similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also experience a reversal of its folds.  In this case, the procedures mentioned previously—cutting, stitching, cauterization with fire, or cauterization with a sharp medicine—should be employed.  Cauterization with fire or with a sharp medicine is more gentle on the patient than cutting and stitching.  Cutting and stitching are more laborious in these cases.
Cauterization with reeds is performed in this manner: Turn the eyelid over and make the incision from the inside, as previously described. Then prepare two thin reeds or sticks, their length equal to the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
Nibble the ends of each reed on both sides where the threads adhere, then gently bring them together over the remaining portion of the eyelid.  Tighten the reeds firmly from both sides and leave them for several days. The tightened skin will die, turn black, and decay.
Until it falls away on its own. If it takes longer, nibble it with the forceps and then treat it until healed. When it adheres, the eyelid will rise, and the folds will not irritate the eye.  This is the shape and size of the reeds, to be made exactly to this length and width:
Chapter Twelve.
Removing irritating hairs from the eye with a needle.
If there is one or two hairs, take a thin needle, thread it with a smooth, thin silk thread, tie the ends with a very gentle knot, and make the thread about a span long.
Then attach another, shorter, thin thread to the tweezers. Tie the ends if desired. The patient should place their head on your lap, close to the light for better visibility, as the work is delicate. Then, using the tweezers and the needle, gently insert the needle into the base of the extra hair.
Penetrate the eyelid with the needle, carefully removing the hair from above the natural hair, then pull the thread upward through the tweezers, and remove the hair(s) – one, two, or three at most – from the tweezers.
Then, pull gently, until the hair comes out of the eyelid with the tweezers and along with the natural hair. If the tweezers come out but the hair does not, pull the tweezers downwards with the thread you attached, pulling them out from the upper opening.
Then, reposition the hair in the tweezers and pull them out, repeating this until the hair is visible through the upper opening. At that point, remove the tweezers and thread, close the eye, and keep it closed for two or three days, until the hair is securely in place and the flesh grows around it.
If the hair is short, either attach a longer strand of natural hair to it so it can be pulled out, or leave it until it grows longer, then remove it.
Chapter Thirteen.
On treating the stye that occurs on the upper eyelid.
The stye that affects this area is called a "rabbit's eye" stye.  It can be either natural or accidental.  Accidental styes result from the infection of a wound, cut, burn, or similar injury.
The treatment involves incising the abscess, separating its edges, and inserting a linen thread between them, tying it off until healed.  Do not use substances that dry and constrict in treating it.
If you do this, the stye will return even worse.  Instead, use softening substances such as fenugreek, and a decoction of licorice root in water, linseed, and diachylon ointment, dissolved with some oils, and apply this mixture to the thread and use it for treatment.
The key to its treatment is to prevent it from adhering in the same way as before.  The method described above is only a part of treating the stye; it does not restore the area's original appearance.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of styes that occur in the lower eyelid.
This stye, which arises from the lower part, is truly the stye; it is natural.  An accidental stye results from a wound, laceration, or burn, and the like.
The method is to take a needle with a doubled thread, pierce the flesh, passing it from the inner to the outer corner until the thread is at the ends of the flesh.  Then, pull the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, then do this. Otherwise, take a spatula and place it on the wound. Turn the eyelid, and cut two incisions in the inside of the eyelid.  The edges of the incisions should extend from the corners of the cut, meeting to form an angle. So that when they meet, the shape will resemble this shape:.
Then, remove the flesh as much as the sharp side of it is below the eyelid.  Afterward, bring the separated parts together with two stitches, sewing them with wool thread. Then treat it as mentioned previously with relaxing and softening remedies until healed.
If the ulceration arose from a cut, suture, or cauterization, then a small incision should be made, also as before, without touching the eyelashes.  Then separate the lips with a softener, as described previously.
In summary, for treating ulcers, whether above or below, perform the procedure according to the appearance of the ulcer.  It can vary greatly in form, and the skilled surgeon devises a method that is possible.
Until the form returns to or nearly resembles its natural state.  The nature of the disease itself indicates the appropriate treatment and tools, in most cases.
Fifteenth Chapter.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may affect many people, causing the upper eyelid to adhere to the white of the eye, hindering its free movement and natural functions.  Therefore, a blunt instrument should be inserted under the eyelid.
And lift it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not as sharp as surgical scalpels, but rather somewhat blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the procedure and the scalpel slips and injures the eye with its sharpness.  Your incision should be like gently peeling away the adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its normal position and the entire adhesion heals.  Then, instill salty water into the eye to wash it, or instill a solution of zinjārī (likely a specific herbal preparation) in water, and then separate the eyelid and eye with a linen thread.
Place moistened white of egg on the eye.  After three days, use medicated poultices until healed.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the excision of the toenail and deep flesh growths.
The toenail is of two types: either nervous, resembling a firm, thin membrane, or non-nervous, resembling a stiff, white moisture; if touched by iron or attempted to be grasped with a hook, it breaks.
A hook does not adhere to either type.  Both types begin from the larger root, gradually progressing until they cover the eye and obstruct light and eye movement.  The procedure for their excision is for the patient to place their head on a cushion.
Then, he opens his eyes and you lift the eyelid with your hand, then grasp the toenail with a slightly curved hook and pull it upwards.  Then, take a needle, thread it with a horsehair or cowhair and strong thread, bend the needle tip slightly, and insert it into the center of the toenail.
Pierce it with the needle, tie the toenail with the thread, pull it upwards, and peel away the side of the toenail that lies next to the pupil with the hair as if spreading it until its end. Then, cut the remaining portion at the root with a gentle scalpel or small scissors, leaving the natural flesh of the root.
So that a constant flow of tears does not result from its excision. The toenail may be pulled out with the hook alone, or with the thread as we stated, then the smooth scalpel peels the portion which has this image.
Care must be taken to avoid touching the cornea with the scalpel, lest it creates a tear in the pupil.  Then, after the excision, place some powdered salt or zinc oxide into the eye, and leave it until the next day, then treat it as necessary until healed.
If the toenail is not sensitive and you cannot insert a needle into it or secure it with a hook, this type of toenail is often treated with medication, healing as described in the classification.  If you want to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a gentle, smooth, blunt scalpel, like this image. Carefully scrape the toenail from above, gently. If you see that it is crumbling and only the scraping has affected it, put a few drops of zinc oxide, red oxide, or powdered salt into the eye from your medicine dropper, and compress the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure until it is all gone, unless a hot tumor appears in the eye. Leave it, and treat the hot tumor until it heals, then resume the scraping until it heals. And concerning the protruding flesh [of the affected area].
If the protrusion is causing significant discomfort to the eye, suspend it with a hook, and cut off a portion of it. Do not over-cut to avoid excessive tearing. Then, apply red or zinc oxide drops into the affected area until healed.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cutting the warty growths and excess flesh in the eye.
In some people's eyes, red, layered flesh may grow until it covers the pupil, or nearly covers it, or spills onto the eyelids.  The eyelids might even turn outward, resembling a rose.
The treatment is to have the patient rest their head in your lap, then open their eyes, and carefully remove all of the red flesh with appropriate hooks.
Or hold it with a probe or forceps, then cut the flesh piece by piece until all is removed, and protect the eye so that it is not injured during the procedure. Your cutting can be done using either the scalpel we described for cutting the toenail, or with small scissors. Like the way one gathers thorns, as their image will appear in the following chapter.
Once the removal is complete and all the fleshy growths are gone, fill the eye with finely ground salt, or drop into it red or similar corrosive medicines.  Apply a bandage made from egg white to the outside of the eye to prevent inflammation.
If any fleshy growths remain, and bleeding occurs, and you fear inflammation, leave the eye and treat it to relieve swelling. Then return to the procedure until healed.  Do the same with any excess flesh of this type that appears in the eye.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye by natural means or by surgical intervention with iron. Do not insert your hand into any of these procedures until you see that the organ can tolerate the procedure.
Chapter Eighteen.
Concerning the extraction of objects from the eye.
Red threads intertwining on the eye hinder vision and weaken the eye over time.  Therefore, first, look to see if the eye containing the threads is strong and has no other ailment besides the threads.
Then, the extraction is done by having the patient place their head on your lap, and gently lifting the threads with one or two fine hooks, shaped thus:
Or, two hooks joined together as in this form:
Then, gently extract the threads with a fine pair of scissors.  Wipe away the blood periodically until you see the eye is free of the threads and the bleeding has stopped.  Protect the eye; do not injure it with the scissor tips.  Work during the middle of the day, in the shade.
Be very meticulous in your work so as not to cut anything but the threads.  When finished, drop green or red ophthalmic solution into the eye to consume any remaining threads.  If you cannot remove all the threads at this time, bandage the eye to soothe any pain.
Leave it for several days until the pain and inflammation subside, then repeat the procedure in the same way until it heals.  This is the manner in which the scissors should be used.
Leave it [and let the patient's head rest on the student's lap; another student should wipe away tears and examine the front and back of the eye, and you]—the correct procedure is to open the eye using adjustable or triangular devices held by a student.
Closely observe, with the patient's head resting on the student's lap, and another student wiping away tears while examining the front and back of the eye. You have the choice of starting the extraction from the upper or lower part of the eye.  Preferably, extract two threads from the upper eyelid, one near the end and one near the beginning of the arch.
Grasp the threads between the middle and index finger, then hook threads underneath from the opposite side.  Then hold them between the pinky and ring finger, and gently pull your hand so you see all threads emerge.  If necessary, cut the threads using small scissors or a gentle scalpel.
Thread the needle and draw out all the threads until they rise above the conjunctiva. Do not cut the threads abruptly; if tears and blood mix, it will make things difficult (get smeared) for you.
Then cut with scissors any threads extending beyond the eyelid, both above and below, so that only those on the cornea remain.  If you can carefully lift the remaining threads off the cornea with the needle, that's better, although it might not be possible in every case.
If you cut them, the area will be irritated for several days, alleviating the pain and swelling.  Then repeat the procedure.
Chapter Nineteen.
In returning the nasal septum.
Doctors call the septum a "nasura" if, after treating it with cauterization or a strong, burning medicine as previously described, it does not heal.  The only remedy in such a case is to incise the swelling when it is ripe and to remove all the moisture or pus within.
Until the bone is exposed. If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness on it, scrape it clean with an instrument of this shape:
The instrument is called a "khashna al-ras." It is made of Indian iron and has a round head like a button. It is engraved with fine grooves, like a file or a scraper.  Place it on the corrupted part of the bone and rotate it between your fingers while gently squeezing your hand, until you know that the corruption has been removed.
Do this several times, then bind the area with dry, astringent remedies.  If the area heals and flesh grows back, and the discharge stops, and it remains for forty days without recurrence, without discharge, or any new problem.
Therefore, know that it has healed.  Otherwise, there is no other recourse than to return the nasal septum to its original opening in this way: expose the bone again with an iron instrument or a strong medicine.  Once the bone is exposed, take a probe like this:
The tip of the iron instrument should be triangular, and its shaft conical, tapered towards the end, as you see.  Place it on the bone itself, near the nostril, with your hand slightly away from the eye.  Then, turn your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
If he finds air escaping when he holds his hand over his nose, then bind the area with dry, astringent remedies, as I have instructed you.  If the flesh grows back and the area hardens, then the matter that was protruding outwards will return to the nose, making it easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Concerning the reduction of a protruding eye.
If the entire eye protrudes and there is no damage or loss of vision, then you should ease the patient, then push it back into the eye socket, then place a cupping glass on the back of the neck without restrictions and gently suction it.
Then place a poultice made of acacia, aloes, frankincense, and centaury on the eye.  Then secure the eye from above on the bandage with many layers of padding.  The method for the padding is to take many soft strips of cloth, fold each strip into four layers and make many such strips.
Then place them one upon another, as much as needed for the eye.  Then secure them with a firm bandage, tightly.  Repeat the cupping with suctioning, without restrictions. Then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye bandaged for a day and a night.
If the ligaments relax during this time, you must tighten them again. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, then do so, otherwise, repeat the bandage, padding, tightening, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On cutting the optic nerve.
If a rupture occurs in the sclera, and the eye protrudes, appearing like the optic nerve, and this disfigures the person, and you want to cut it, then the operation is as I describe.
Insert a needle into the root of the optic nerve from below to above. Then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the side of the tear, passing through it, leaving the first needle in place.
Then cut the place where the doubled thread bends, and tie some of the optic nerve around the needle and tighten it firmly. Then remove the needle and place on the eye wool moistened with egg white, and leave it until the threads fall away with the optic nerve, then treat the eye with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound heals, the optic nerve may be tightened from all sides with a circular bandage until the eye protrudes firmly.  Then, with a thin scalpel, probe deep into the eye, so that the whitish fluid drains and the eye descends and withers in position, then tighten it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of the [eye] socket.
This ailment, called the socket, is a collection of fluid in the eye that resembles descending water, but is not.  The procedure is to have the patient sit upright on a chair, and then take hold of their head from both sides with your hands.
Move it until you see the fluid descend with your eyes, then hold it still, so that it doesn't move, and the light will emerge, and the patient will see things as they used to.  If it does not descend, we know that it is water. If the fluid does not descend as described,
Otherwise, have the patient sit before you, then take a slender scalpel and make a small incision just above the cornea where the conjunctiva touches the cornea in the limbus, so that the matter may come out.
If it comes out, drop warm water, mixed with honey, or water boiled with licorice and honey, into the eye, then treat with other methods until it is healed.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have mentioned the types of water in the classification, and which are suitable for drops, with detailed and explained descriptions.  Take from there what is correct.  Then, the patient should sit before you, seated facing the light, near the sun.
Tie and firmly hold the sound eye, then raise the eyelid with your left hand if the watery eye is the left one, or with your right hand if it is the right one.
Then place the tip of the applicator near the limbus, with the thickness of a millet seed, on the white of the eye, on the side of the smaller eyelid, and then push the applicator firmly while rotating it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel the applicator has reached an empty space.
The applicator should proceed to a depth corresponding to the distance from the pupil to the edge of the iris (limbus). The copper is seen by the observer as if it were in the same plane as the eye due to the clarity of the cornea.  Then, the applicator should be raised to the position where the water is located.
Then press it downwards repeatedly. If the water comes out immediately, the patient will see what has been revealed to their sight immediately, with the applicator in their eye, and then rest a little. If the water rises, lower it again without removing the applicator.
If it settles and does not rise, gently remove the applicator by slightly twisting your hand.  Then dissolve a little pure Andronan salt in water, and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then apply moist cotton or gauze to the entire outside of the eye.
With rose oil and egg white, and bind the healthy eye. As for us and the people of our time, we place ground cumin with egg white on it. If the applicator does not enter the eye due to its hardness, because some people have very hard eyes. It is necessary to take the scalpel called the *bayrid*, which has this form/image.
Pierce only the adhering tissue with it, do not penetrate deeply.  Instead, gently pierce the applicator in a suitable place, then insert the applicator as previously described.  When your work is complete, prepare a secure, darkened room for the patient to lie on their back.
He should refrain from all movements and coughing. Give him food that softens his nature, and do not allow him to move his head to the right or left at all. The bandage remains in place for three days. Then, in that darkened room, remove it and test his sight.
Show him objects, then reapply the bandage for seven days.  You should not do this during the treatment or after the applicator is removed, but rather, avoid it until the water has quickly risen with the great intensity of sight.
If a hot swelling appears, the eye should be released before the seventh day, and treat the swelling in a way that relieves it. Once the swelling subsides, then release the sight, place a cloth over the face so that it trains the sight from underneath, for a number of days, in the darkened room. Then...
He is gradually released from the room and allowed to manage his affairs.  Know that the learner cannot dispense with observing the procedure multiple times before undertaking the procedure.  I have heard some Iraqis say that they make a hollow applicator in Iraq that draws out water.
I have not seen anyone in our country do this, nor have I read it in any book of the ancients.  It may be a recent development.  Here are images of different types of applicators, for him to learn about their forms.
It is made of copper, specifically.  Its tip is precisely this thin, triangular shape, with a sharp point.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On treating flesh growing in the nose.
Various superfluous fleshy growths may sprout in the nose, some resembling a scorpion with many legs, others cancerous, hard, and dark-colored, and still others soft and not dark-colored.
For those fleshy growths that are soft, not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun, open their nostril, insert the forceps into the growth, pull it outward, then cut off the part grasped with a gentle, sharp scalpel on one side.
So that you know that all the flesh is gone; if any part remains uncut, scrape it off gently with a delicate instrument, until nothing is left, lest you be overwhelmed by blood or a hot tumor appear.
If it is a malignant tumor, then immediately cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone.  Then, after the excision, place vinegar and water or a drink in the nose, so that the nose opens, and moisture flows from it into the throat.
Know then that it is healed; if the moisture does not flow properly, know that there is a fleshy growth inside, on the upper, porous bones, which the instrument did not reach during excision.  In that case, a thick linen thread should be taken.
Make many knots on it, placing each knot a finger-width apart or less. The patient should manipulate the end of the thread into their nose with a probe or any available tool, shaped like a button.  Pull on the thread to reach the nostrils.
It will come out through their throat.  Children often do this in school; it is easy for those who wish to do it. Then gather the two ends of the thread—the one that came out of the mouth and the one remaining in the nose.
Then, using the knots on the thread, dissect the flesh.  Do this until you see that the flesh has been severed by the thread's knots.  Then remove the thread.  Place the thread, which has been soaked in Egyptian ointment, in the nose after cleaning the blood.  Do this for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all remaining flesh.  Finally, a lead tube is placed in the nose for a few days until healed.  If further drying treatment is needed, use that.
This is an image of the instrument used to drip ointments and medicines into the nose.
It is made of silver or copper, like a small, open lamp, with a channel for the medicine.
And if you wish, make the tube closed, like a reed, and flatten the instrument's applicator.
It has a handle at the end, as you see, to hold it when heating the ointment or any other liquid substances.
Chapter Twenty-five.
On warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Warts often grow on the tip of the nose, increasing in size and number over time, until their appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance and remove it completely.  Then, apply either cautery or a burning medicine that acts as cautery to the area.  If it is left until it grows large, examine it.  If it is hard, stony, dark-coloured, and insensitive,
do not use iron on it, for it is a cancerous tumor.  I have often seen that cutting such a tumor brings great harm to the patient.  If the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark-colored, and you judge that it can be completely removed by cutting, then thoroughly excise and cut it without hesitation or delay. Treat the area with something that dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear. If its connection is disrupted by a wound or the like, know that when a disruption of connection occurs in any of these cartilages, rarely does the treatment succeed, except in some people.
Therefore, if one of these things happens to someone, first see if the wound is fresh with blood.  If so, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures, and then treat it until it heals. If the disruption of connection has separated the edges and each gap is sound, then it is necessary to scrape each gap with the thickness of its visible skin until it bleeds.
Then, bring the edges together with sutures, tighten them, and sprinkle powdered shiyān and lubān on them.  Place a plaster of palm ointment, or other gluing ointments, on top.  Leave it tightened for two or three days, then loosen it.
And change the medicine, leaving it until the threads break on their own. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The method of suturing is to bring together the disrupted connection either with needles, as we described in the suturing of the abdomen, or with thread, as I explained there.
Chapter Twenty-seven.
On removing knots that appear on the lips.
Many people may develop small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling gooseberry seeds, others smaller or larger.  It is necessary to turn back the lip and cut each knot, extracting it with forceps.
Cut it from all sides, then fill the area after cutting with powdered alum until the bleeding stops. Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the areas with astringents until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-eight.
On cutting excess flesh in the gums.
Excess flesh often grows on the gums, which the ancients called *aphol*.  It should be grasped with forceps or a dissector, cut at its base, and allowed to bleed.  Then powdered alum should be placed on the area.
Or one of the drying astringents.  If that flesh returns after treatment, it often does.  Cut it again and cauterize it, as it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-nine.
On cleaning teeth with an iron instrument.
Rough, unsightly scales may accumulate on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums.  They may darken, yellow, or turn green, causing decay to reach the gums and disfigure the teeth.
The patient should sit before you, head resting on your lap.  Remove the tartar and sand-like material from the tooth where you see scales, until nothing remains.  Do the same with any discoloration, such as blackness, greenness, or yellowness, until the tooth is clean.
If the cleaning is successful from the first attempt, then it is fine. Otherwise, repeat the cleaning the next day, and again on subsequent days, until the desired result is achieved.  Know that teeth require various instruments of different shapes, according to the task at hand.
Because the instrument used to clean the inside of a tooth is different from the one used for the outside, and the one used for between the teeth is of a different shape.  You should have a variety of instruments prepared.
Chapter Thirty.
On the extraction of teeth.
One should attempt to alleviate the toothache with every possible method, and delay extraction if possible, for there is no recovery after extraction as it is a precious substance.  Only if extraction is absolutely necessary should it be performed.
If the patient insists on extraction, one must be certain that the tooth's pain is definitively present. Often, the patient is deceived by the pain, believing it is in a healthy tooth and extracting it, then the pain does not subside until the diseased tooth is extracted. We have seen this happen repeatedly with barbers.
If you are certain the pain is in the specific tooth, then you should use a strong forceps to cut around the gum on all sides.  Then, gently move the tooth, bit by bit, with your fingers or fine forceps, until it loosens.
Then, firmly grasp the tooth with the large forceps, holding the patient's head between your knees so it does not move.  Pull the tooth straight to avoid breaking it. If it does not come out, take one of the instruments and gently insert it under the tooth on all sides, moving it as you did initially.
If the tooth is decayed or has a hole, you should fill the hole with a cloth and seal it tightly with a thin, strong material to prevent it from fracturing when you apply force with the forceps.  Carefully cut around the gum on all sides.
Use caution to avoid breaking the tooth, as a piece remaining would cause the patient further harm, greater than the initial pain.  Avoid the actions of ignorant barbers who extract teeth without using the methods described.
They often inflict great harm on people; the least of which is a broken tooth, with the roots remaining whole or partially. Or, the tooth may be pulled out with some of the jawbone, as we have seen repeatedly.  After extraction, the patient should rinse the area with a drink, vinegar, or salt water.  If bleeding occurs, as is often the case, then pulverize some alum and apply it to the area.
Otherwise, if the alum does not help, use a delicate forceps.  The forceps should have long handles and short grips, and be thick to prevent bending when grasping the tooth.  This is a picture of the large forceps.
The handles should be thick, so that when you grasp them, they do not yield or bend.  The tips should be short.  Make them of Indian iron or tempered steel, with the tips firmly joined together, interlocking like teeth for a secure grip.
The tips can also be shaped like a file, which also provides a strong grip.

Short, slightly thick at the end, and not pointed, lest it breaks. If the root is removed by this, otherwise use these other instruments, the image of which is here: The first is triangular at the end, with some thickness.

Triangular, bent at the end, with some slight thickness, lest it breaks, and do not make it pointed. Because there are illnesses for which the ancients did not describe instruments, due to the diversity of their types.
