Chapter Thirty-two.
In the extraction of erupting teeth.
If teeth erupt in a way that is not natural, their appearance becomes undesirable, especially in women and slaves.  Therefore, it should be examined. If a tooth has grown behind another tooth...
If it cannot be extracted or repositioned, remove it.  If it is attached to another tooth, cut it off using this instrument, which is depicted here:
It resembles a small beak, made of sharp, Indian steel.  Cut it in several days, due to the tooth's hardness, so as not to dislodge other teeth.  If it is protruding and firmly rooted, cool it with an Indian file, which is depicted as follows.
It should be entirely of Indian steel, with very fine engravings. It should be like the file used to make needles.  Cool the tooth little by little over many days, gently, so as not to dislodge and drop it. Then smooth it further and scrape it with some scrapers.
If a tooth has broken off, causing pain to the tongue when speaking, it should also be cooled until the roughness of the fracture disappears, and it becomes smooth, no longer hurting the tongue or disturbing speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In securing loose teeth with silver or gold thread.
If old teeth become loose and shift due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot eat anything for fear of them falling out, and treatment with astringent medicines has been ineffective.
The solution is to tie them with gold or silver thread. Gold is better because silver tarnishes and rots after a few days, whereas gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium thickness and thinness, just enough to fit between the teeth.
The method of securing is to take the thread, and insert its loop between the two sound teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, one or more, until weaving reaches the sound tooth on the other side.
Then, repeat the weaving back to the starting side, pulling gently and judiciously so that they do not shift.  Tie the thread tightly at the tooth roots to prevent it from coming loose.  Then cut the excess thread with scissors, tie the ends together and twist them with a file, hiding them between the sound tooth and the loose tooth.
So as not to injure the tongue.  Then leave it tied like this as long as it remains.  If it loosens or breaks, tie it with another thread.  He will enjoy it like this for all time.  This is the image of the teeth and the method of weaving in two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
A single or two teeth may return to their place after falling out, and be secured as described, and remain.  Only a skilled artisan can do this.  They may also carve bone from the ox, shaping it to resemble a tooth and place it in the socket from which the tooth has disappeared. And ties it, as we said, so it remains and is enjoyed.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On severing the ligament that appears beneath the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament that appears beneath the tongue may be either natural, present at birth, or accidental, from a wound that has healed.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth, hold their head in your lap, and lift their tongue.  Then, sever the nerve ligament transversely so the tongue is released from its constraint.  If there is some stiffness or entanglement, and this is from a healed wound, insert a blunt instrument and slice it across until the ligament is severed and the knots are loosened.
Be careful not to cut too deeply into the flesh, and sever an artery, causing bleeding.  Then, after the cut, rinse the area with rosewater or vinegar and cold water.  Then place a linen wick under the tongue, for the patient to hold in place each night.
To prevent reattachment.  If bleeding occurs, apply powdered alum to the area.  If the bleeding is profuse, cauterize the area with a suitable lentil-shaped cautery iron. Then treat it with other remedies until healed.
Chapter Thirty-five.
Removing the toad that has formed under the tongue.
A tumor resembling a small toad may form under the tongue, hindering its normal function.  It may even enlarge, filling the mouth.  The procedure is to open the patient's mouth facing the sun and examine the tumor.
If it appears dark or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation, do not attempt treatment, for it is cancer.  If it is tending toward a whitish color with moisture, use a forceps to grasp and carefully split it with a fine scalpel, removing it from all sides.
If bleeding occurs during the procedure, apply powdered borax until the bleeding stops. Then resume the procedure until the tumor is completely removed. Finally, have the patient gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with other appropriate treatments until healed.
Chapter Thirty-six.
On the treatment of tonsil tumors.
Any tumors growing in the throat may present glands inside the throat resembling the glands that appear externally, called tonsils.  If treatment as described in the division did not bring healing.
If the tumor is hard, dark-colored, and insensitive, do not use the iron.  If it is red and thick, do not use the iron either, for fear of blood loss.  Instead, let it mature, and it will either soften or burst on its own.
If it is white, round, and thin, this is what should be cut.  Before doing so, observe if its inflammatory swelling has completely subsided or if some of the swelling has decreased.
Then, have the patient sit facing the sun, head resting on your lap, mouth open.  Take an assistant, and have them press the patient's tongue downwards with an instrument, of this form:
Made of silver or copper, thin like a knife.  When you press the tongue with this and the tumor is clear and visible, take a probe and insert it into the tonsil, pulling it outward as much as possible, without pulling any of the membranes with it.
Then cut it with an instrument, like a pair of scissors, but with both ends bent so that the tips of each are extremely sharp.  Made of Indian iron or Damascus steel.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel, of this form:
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other.  After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner.  Then, after the cut, have the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, he should gargle with water in which pomegranate rind or rue leaves, or similar astringents, have been boiled, until the bleeding stops.  Then treat it until healed.  Other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils, and these are cut in the same way as the tonsils.
I treated a woman for a tumor that had grown inside her throat, approaching the size of a small, rather insensitive lump, which nearly blocked the throat.  The woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and the tumor had prevented her from eating and drinking.
She was close to death if she had remained alive for another day or two.  The tumor had grown two branches which extended to her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserting a hook into one of them, pulling it, and a suitable piece came away.
Then I cut it where it emerged from the nostril, then I did the same with what protruded from the other nostril.  Then I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut off some of it.  Only a small amount of blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began drinking water and eating.  We continued to cut away from that tumor repeatedly for a long time, and the tumor would grow back after what we cut away, until this task consumed both me and her.
I then cauterized the tumor inside the throat, hoping to stop its growth. Then I left the area. I do not know what God did for her after I left.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
On the removal of a tumor of the larynx.
If a tumor, called *'unba'* descends to the larynx and swells, and is white and oblong, it is called a *'amud'*; but if it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called *'unba'*. If treated as mentioned in the division, and the treatment is not successful...
If the hot tumor has subsided and it is thin, it should be cut. But if it is a collected, round mass without length, and is blood-red, dark-red or black, or without feeling, then its excision should be avoided.
For there is risk to the patient. Therefore, if you see it with the characteristics described, whiteness and length, the patient should sit facing the sun, and his tongue should be pressed with the instrument whose description is previously given. Then the hook should be inserted into the *'unba'*.
Pull it downward and cut it with one of the two instruments that I mentioned for cutting the tonsils.  Only the portion exceeding the natural limit should be cut, without further removal, for if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after the cutting, use what we described for the cutting of the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is reluctant to have it cut, then a cauterization should be employed without fear or caution. The method of cauterization is with a sharp medicine, and the patient's head should be placed in your lap.
Then, press the patient's tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take the sharp water, mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with quicklime, making it neither thick nor thin. Fill the depression of this instrument with it, and this is its shape.
The end of the instrument, where the medicine is placed, has a depression like the depression of a *mard* spoon.  Place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself.  The patient should lie on his side, so saliva may drain from his mouth from inside his throat to avoid anything going back into his throat and harming him.
Then, hold the instrument containing the medicine and apply pressure to the uvula for about half an hour, until you see it blackened and the burning of the medicine subsides. If you prefer, take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the end of a *mard* spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicine.
Insert the *mard* spoon with the cotton into the tube from above, so that the cotton adheres to the uvula. Repeat this several times until you have achieved the desired cauterization of the uvula.  Then leave it; it will wither and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, do so.
After cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in clarified butter, removing any remaining medicine. Then, the patient should rinse his mouth with cool water.  Treatment should also be applied externally with lotions and internally with gargles until healed.  This type of treatment is safer than surgery and avoids fear.
The uvula can also be treated with gentler methods than cauterization and surgery, using incense in this manner:  Take frankincense, saffron, sage, fumitory, wormwood, chamomile, and *qaysum*, and other similar herbs. Combine all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
Bring it to a boil, the pot sealed with earthenware, and a hollow tube is placed in the middle of the lid. This tube is made of silver or copper.
Insert the end of the tube, which contains the pomegranate, into the patient's mouth so that the steam ascends to the uvula through the tube, causing the uvula to contract.  Repeat this several times until it withers.  Avoid using this treatment at the very onset of the swelling.
For it often aggravates the swelling.  Instead, one should do this when the hot swelling has subsided.  If you do not have this instrument, take a hollow tube and fit a piece of eggshell to the end of it to prevent burning the patient's mouth.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth.  This is a good and safe treatment.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other foreign objects lodged in the throat.
Often a bone, fish thorn, or other foreign object lodges in the throat.  One should remove any visible object that can be seen, after pressing the tongue down with an instrument, in the light.
To make clear what is in the throat.  If something is hidden in the throat, you should induce vomiting before the patient's food has digested in the stomach.  Perhaps the foreign object will be expelled through vomiting, or the patient might swallow a piece of cloth or a stem of a vegetable.
Or a piece of dry bread, or you take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a thread, and have the patient swallow it. When it reaches the location of the thorn, pull the thread quickly.  Do this repeatedly.
Often the thorn or bone adheres to it and comes out. If it does not come out by the methods mentioned, then use an instrument made of lead, in this manner:
It should be slightly thicker than a reed.  At its tip, there is a curve, which the patient inserts into their throat gently, with their head raised upward, carefully avoiding touching their throat so as not to cause coughing, and pushing out the bone or thorn, or the physician inserts it with their hand.
Having the patient insert it is better, because they know the location of the foreign object.  Push downward or pull upward with the instrument, as is appropriate, until it is removed.
Chapter Thirty-nine.
Removing a leech lodged in the throat.
If you have treated the leech using the medications mentioned in the previous section, and it is unsuccessful, then look into the patient's throat in the light of the sun, after pressing the tongue down with the instrument described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a gentle, firm forceps. If that is not successful, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech. Then, insert a heated, protected iron rod into the tube.
Do this several times.  The patient should abstain from water all day.  Then, he should take a jar full of cool water, open his mouth over it, rinse with it, and not swallow a drop.  He should move the water with his hand from time to time.
If the leech feels the water, it will fall off. If it does not come out as described, fumigate the throat with urine and with hydromel using the instrument described in the section on fumigation of the larynx. Do this several times, and it will fall off.
The method for fumigation is to take a vessel containing embers heated by fire, and the vessel covered with a lid having a hole in the center.  Place the end of the instrument in that hole.  Then, put the incense into the vessel, and the patient places his mouth at the end of the tube.  He closes his mouth to prevent the incense from escaping, until he is sure that it has reached his throat.
If the leech falls off at this stage, if not, the fumigation is repeated several times.  The patient endures thirst and eats salty things and garlic, and does not drink water.  It is necessary that the leech comes out with this procedure. This is the form of the instrument for pulling the leech from the throat if it is visible.
It resembles pincers, as you see, except that it has this bend that enters the throat, and its ends resemble a bird's mouth.  There is the roughness of a file on them, so if it grasps something, it does not let go.
Another instrument is used to press the tongue; it is a wide ring of iron, sized to fit the mouth over the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, keeping the mouth open against the patient's will.  He cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument that opens the mouth in this exact manner.
Chapter Forty.
In it, there is discussion of the lancing and incision of tumors.
Tumors are numerous and varied, each described individually in this book.  They differ in lancing and incision in two ways: one is the nature of the tumor itself, and the other is the humors it contains.
The second type differs according to the locations in the body where they occur.  A tumor occurring in the head is not the same as one occurring in the buttocks, and a tumor in fleshy tissue differs from one in a joint. Each requires specific treatment.
Some tumors should not be lanced until the pus has matured and is fully formed.  Others, such as those near the joints, should be lanced while still unripe and incompletely matured.
Because if a tumor near a joint grows until it causes the surrounding tissue to putrefy, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing the limb to become useless, or the tumor may be close to a vital organ.
Because if you delay lancing it until it matures, you will harm the vital organ, or if it is near the buttocks, you should lance it while it is still unripe.  It is necessary to lance an unripe tumor near the buttocks to prevent the pus from spreading deep into the buttocks, causing a fistula.
And it will become incurable to a degree.  You must know when to lance fully matured tumors, when the tumor pain subsides, the fever is gone, redness and throbbing are reduced, the head of the tumor softens, and other symptoms diminish.
The incision should be made at the lowest part of the tumor if possible, to facilitate the drainage of pus downwards, or at the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor.  If the tumors are on the hands or feet, the incision should be made lengthwise.
And the areas of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries—in short, all flat, non-flexing areas.  In flexing areas, the incision should follow the contours of the area.  If the tumor is in fleshy areas, it is best to leave it unlanced.
Until its maturation is complete, as we said. If you lance it before then, the pus discharge will be prolonged, causing much harm and dirtiness.  The edges and depths of the tumor might become hardened. Some tumors may be incised across the body, if necessary, or according to the needs of the affected limb.
Small tumors should be incised once, while large tumors require a wide incision or several incisions, commensurate with the tumor's size.  Some tumors may need to have the skin cauterized or cut away if they have become like a rag.
Some have, to a degree, died, as occurs in many swellings and abscesses.  Some are incised with a three-angled cut, and others are cut like a rue leaf, such as the scrotal tumor.
Some require a circular or crescent-shaped incision, and similar cuts.  If the tumor lacks a head, like a smooth, flat tumor, a simple incision is sufficient.  If the tumor is large, has accumulated for a long time, and has been incised, one should not rush to drain all the pus at once.
Instead, drain some of it, then press the swelling back for a day, and then drain some more pus.  Repeat this process gradually until all of it is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an elderly person, the animal spirit often weakens with a sudden discharge of pus.  The patient might die without you realizing it, so be extremely cautious in this regard.
After incising these tumors, you should cleanse the wound and examine it. If the tumor wound is small or the incision is simple, use lint or worn cotton. If the tumor is large and the incisions numerous,
Then you should insert a wick into each incision until they meet.  If the tumor has cut or eroded some of the skin, you should fill it with worn cotton or linen fibers, without moisture, and hold it in place for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, soaked into a linen cloth, and apply it to the bleeding spot repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the treatments and remedies described in many places in this book, and in the sections on classification. If it is winter and the tumor area is highly sensitive, you should soak the bandages in warm drink and oil.
And apply them to the area.  If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply bandages soaked in water and oil or in cold drink and oil, until the third day, as we said, when you should then release the tumor and cleanse it.
And use similar treatments until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the detailed method, each tumor has been described, how to treat it, in a summarized, categorized way.
Chapter Forty-one.
On the incision of tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp, a type of swelling contained within membranes.  They resemble a hen's egg, and their types are numerous; some are fatty, others contain moisture like mud.
Some contain moisture like broth and soup, and similar things; others are hardened and solid.  All can be incised and removed without danger, unless a blood vessel is encountered during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument, whose image will follow after this, called the *mudass*, so as to learn what they contain. If they contain moisture, incise them lengthwise with a simple incision in this manner:
Begin the incision from line ~B~ to line ~C~.  If the moisture has drained, dissect and remove the sac containing the moisture entirely. Do not leave any part of it, for it frequently recurs if any remains.
Then dip a cotton swab in the astringent ointment, if available, or in salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it until the next day, as it will consume any remaining sac. Then, reapply the cotton swab with the astringent a second and third time if necessary.
Until you are certain that no part of the sac remains.  Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the swelling contains fatty tissue, incise it in this manner:
Make a deep incision, and insert forceps into the edges of the wound.  Dissecting from all sides, make every effort to remove the membrane containing it. If an artery is encountered, proceed as described previously.  Likewise, proceed with the swelling if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as previously mentioned.  The incision on a hardened swelling is easier, because it has little blood and moisture. Some of these swellings in the head in some people have absolutely no moisture. I incised a swelling in...
The head of an elderly woman, and found the swelling as hard as stone, rough, white, and unbreakable.  If someone were to throw it, it would shatter.  Other swellings occurring in the head, unlike those in children, are not like this.
At the origins of the ears, make a simple incision across the entire area, and create a pathway from the bottom to allow the material to flow downwards. Then treat it with the appropriate remedy.
Chapter Forty-Two.
Concerning the incision on swine-like swellings that often appear in the neck. These swellings frequently appear in the neck, under the armpits, and in the groin. They may appear elsewhere on the body.  Sometimes, a single swelling appears in the neck; other times, there are many, and some develop from others.
Each such swelling exists within its own membrane, like those in merchandise and head tumors, as we described.  There are many types of these swellings, some stony, some containing moisture, and some malignant and unresponsive to treatment.
Those that I found favorable to touch, whose appearance was close to the skin color, moved in all directions, and were not attached to the neck's nerve, tendon, or artery, and were not deeply embedded. Therefore, a simple incision should be made from top to bottom of the body, along the line from ~J~ to line ~B~.
Peel it away from all sides, and stretch the edges of the skin with a hook or two or many hooks, if needed, as we said for head tumors.  Remove it little by little, being careful not to sever a vein or nerve. The scalpel should not be excessively sharp.
So that your hand does not cause excessive cutting or unsettle the patient, cutting what you do not need to cut.  If you sever a vein or artery and are prevented from continuing, then place pulverized alum or some of the styptics which stop bleeding into the wound.
Tighten the wound and leave it until the swelling subsides, the wound relaxes, and it is about to fester.  Then the bleeding will stop, and you can resume your work until it is finished.  Then, examine with your index finger if there are any remaining, smaller pieces, and remove them.
If a large vein is found at the base of the piece, you should not cut it off at its base, but should tie it with a doubled and tight thread and leave it until it falls out on its own without harm.  Then, fill the wound with used cotton, having soaked it in Egyptian ointment, and then treat it.
If you have cut away the entire piece, you should bring the edges of the wound together and stitch it immediately, after ensuring that there is absolutely no residue left. If you find a remaining part of the piece of flesh, it is necessary to do this.
You should aim for the base and root of the piece with the cuts, and employ the sutures and what we have mentioned.  For pieces containing moisture, apply a simple bandage where you see their point of ripeness, and make the bandage from below, as we have said.
Then, after the bandage, apply a poultice of Egyptian ointment, or similar, to consume any remaining corruption. Once you see the wound is clean, treat it with healing ointments until it is healed.
Chapter Forty-three.
On slitting the throat for a tumor occurring within the throat.
The ancients mentioned this slitting of the throat, but I have seen no one in our country perform it.  This follows their words.  As for those with sore throats, one should avoid slitting the throat, as it is of no benefit.
In order that all tumors and lungs may be diseased; but those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if the trachea is not affected, then slitting the throat is necessary to escape the harm of suffocation.
Therefore, if we wish to do this, we should slit the throat below three rings of the trachea or four small slits horizontally between two rings, as wide as the slit in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This is the appropriate place for the slit.
Because it lacks flesh and its blood vessels are distant, if the surgeon is timid, he should extend the throat skin with a hook and then slit the skin until, when he reaches the trachea, he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should slit the peritoneum that we have described, and evidence for slitting the trachea is the phlegm that comes from it with what is punctured and the cessation of sound. He should leave the wound open for a time.  When the time during which suffocation was feared has passed, he should bring together the edges of the wound from the skin.
And he cuts along the line alone, without the cartilage. Then the medications that promote flesh growth are used until it heals.  The author of this book says, in explanation of this passage, that this is what is done if they see that the patient's throat is obstructed by one of these tumors that we have mentioned.
When the patient was close to death, and feared asphyxiation, they proceeded to slit the throat so that the patient might breathe through the wound and be saved from death.  However, they were instructed to leave the wound open until the illness's course was completed, which usually takes three days or so.
At that point, they were instructed to stitch the wound and treat it until it healed.  What I personally observed was that a servant took a knife and thrust it into her throat, severing a portion of her lung's trachea. I was called to treat her, and found her weakening as if she had been slain. I uncovered the wound.
I found that the blood coming from the wound was minimal, so I became certain that she had not severed an artery or vein. Air was escaping from the wound.  I quickly sutured the wound and treated it until it healed.  The servant experienced nothing further than a slight hoarseness in her voice.
And after a few days, she returned to her best condition.  Therefore, we say that slitting the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On the incision of the tumor that appears on the larynx from the outside, and which is called the laryngeal tumor.
This tumor, called a laryngeal tumor, is a large tumor the color of the body.  It is common in women and comes in two types.
Either it is natural or accidental.  As for the natural one, there is no remedy.  As for the accidental one, it is of two kinds: one like fatty tumors, and the other like the tumor that arises from arterial blockage.  In incising it, there is danger. Therefore, one should not use a knife at all, unless it is small. If you probe and examine it with a probe and find that it resembles a fatty tumor and is not connected to any vessels, then incise it as one would a tumor. And remove it with the sac that contains it, if it is in a sac. Otherwise, remove it completely, then treat the area with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-five.
On incising various tumors.
Tumors are of many types, and all their types have been mentioned in the classification.  Here, we must explain the difference between a tumor and an abscess, for this is problematic. I say that an abscess presents with fever, intense pain, and frightening sensations until the heat subsides. And when the pus is complete, then the fever and intensity subside. A tumor does not present with fever, pain, or inflammation. It is contained within a serous sac, a special casing, and is the same color as the body. Its initial stage resembles a lentil, then it becomes like a fig, larger or smaller.
It is of two types: either fatty or containing moisture. The colors of moisture are numerous, as mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when treating a tumor, one must first probe and examine it using the instrument called a 'midas' (or 'probe'), as its image will be found in the following chapter.
Estimate the size of the tumor, then withdraw the probe and observe what follows. If a liquid substance of any color is expelled, incise it with a simple incision, as described for other tumors. If no liquid follows the probe, know that it is fatty.
Incise it with a dissecting incision, as I have taught you, and suspend it by forceps. Carefully dissect the skin from all sides. If possible, extract the sac containing the tumor intact.
If the sac ruptures during the procedure and you cannot remove it intact, which frequently occurs, then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains. If any part remains, however small, the tumor is likely to recur.
If you are unsuccessful and a small portion remains, cauterize the wound with sharp cauterizing agents after you are finished, and stitch it. Apply something to reduce inflammation and treat it with other treatments until it heals. If the tumor is large, suture its edges and treat it to promote healing.
If a vein, whether pulsating or not, obstructs your progress and bleeding occurs, immediately cauterize the affected area with powdered alum, and leave it tightly bound for two or three days until the wound becomes festered and the blood flow subsides. Then proceed to remove any remaining portion of the tumor.

Chapter Forty-seven.
On treating the breast of men.
Some people's breasts may swell at puberty until they resemble women's breasts, remaining swollen and unsightly.  If one dislikes this, one should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast, in this manner.
From line ~b~ to line ~c~. Then scrape away all the fat, then fill the wound with the medicated ointment, then stitch together the edges of the wound and treat it until it heals. If the breast sags and relaxes because of the bone, as happens in women,
Then one should make two crescent-shaped incisions on its upper sides, with each one connecting to the other at their ends, so that the larger line encircles the smaller one, in this manner. Chapter Forty-nine.
On incising the tumor that appears near the artery or vein, called an aneurysm.
If the artery is injured and the skin above it adheres, a tumor often arises from this.  Similarly, swelling and tumors may also arise in veins.
The signs by which one can tell whether the swelling and puffiness are from an artery or a vein: if it is from the artery, the tumor will be oblong, situated deep within the body, and if you press on the tumor with your finger, you will feel a sort of crackling sensation.
And that from the vein, the tumor will be round and on the surface of the body.  Incision on these tumors is dangerous, especially in the armpit, groin, neck, and many other parts of the body. If they are very large, one should avoid treating them with iron.
And those that are also in the extremities or in the head should be avoided.  If it is an arterial swelling, make a longitudinal incision in the skin, then widen the incision with forceps, then dissect and free the artery from the surrounding tissues.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle underneath and pass it to the other side.  Tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I explained to you concerning the arteries in the neck.  Then pierce the area between the ligaments with a lancet until all the blood is released.
The swelling will subside, and then you use a treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall off. Then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the swelling is due to a vein rupture, you should grasp as much of the swelling with the skin as possible. Then insert a needle below the area you are holding, and pass it through with a doubled thread, bringing it out from the other side. Then tie the swelling firmly, according to what I have already shown you in tying the knot on both sides of the thread. If you fear the threads will slip, insert another needle with another thread under the entire swelling where the first needle crosses.
Tie your threads in four places, then cut the swelling open in the middle.  When the contents are expelled, cut away the excess skin and leave what has been tied. Then place a compress soaked in potion and oil on it. Then use the treatment that involves twisting and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
In the swelling that arises from nerve twisting.
As swelling occurs in the artery and vein, so too does swelling occur in the nerve if a blow is sustained or from excessive exertion or the like.  This is more common in the wrists and heels.
And in every location where the joints move, it is a hard swelling, similar in color to the rest of the body.  It is mostly painless.  If pressed firmly, the afflicted person feels a sensation resembling numbness.  The swelling is not concentrated deep within the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, but does not move forward or backward.  For those in the joints, it should not be treated with iron, as it might cause a festering wound.  And for those in the head or forehead...
Cut the skin with a scalpel. If the swelling is small, grasp it with a forceps and cut it from the root. If it is large, suspend it with a hook and scrape it off, then remove it and stitch the wound, treating it until it heals.
The Fifty-first Chapter.
Concerning the removal of warts that appear on the abdomen.
Warts, called "fungal" because of their resemblance to fungi, frequently appear on the abdomens and other parts of the body of some people.  Their base is thin and their head thick; sometimes their lips are fused together.  Some are small, others very large.
I observed a man with two warts on his abdomen that resembled fungi in every way.  The bases were thin and white; their lips were fused together, and moisture constantly flowed from them.  I removed them, and their weights were approximately eighteen ounces and six ounces, respectively.
In performing the removal, observe if the affected area is moist and the wart's color is white, moist, and thin-based.  Cut it with a broad scalpel, and have a cautery iron present, as much blood frequently flows during the excision.
If blood flow overwhelms you, cauterize it immediately.  If the affected area is timid and recoils from cutting with the iron, take a strong lead thread and tie it around the wart with this description. Leave it for two days, then increase the tightness of the lead thread, continuing to tighten it.
As it acts upon the wart, it will eventually detach and fall off of its own accord, without further effort. If the wart has a thick base, there is some risk and danger in removing it entirely, particularly if it is in the abdomen. However, it may be possible to remove half or a portion of it.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and then treat the wound until it heals. Beware of cutting a wart that is dark, insensitive, and unsightly, for it is a cancerous tumor. We will discuss cancer after this.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
In treating navel growths.
Navel growths can arise from various causes: either from a rupture in the abdominal peritoneum, allowing the intestines or bowels to protrude, as is seen in other hernias; or from blood emanating from a vein or artery, as previously described; or from trapped wind within it.
If it is due to a rupture in the peritoneum and protrusion of the intestines, then the color of the growth will resemble the body's color, it will be soft without pain, and its position will be unusual. If it is due to the protrusion of the intestines, its position will be even more unusual than what we described. When you press it with your fingers, it will disappear and then reappear.  It may also produce rumbling, and it will often become significantly larger with bathing or strenuous exertion.  If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
If it does not disappear when pressed with your hand, and does not increase or decrease in size, it is likely due to blood.  In this case, the growth will display a bluish-dark coloration. If it is from newly grown flesh, the growth will be firm, hard, and remain constant in size.
If it is due to wind, the touch will be soft.  In such a case, one should refrain from treatment, for there is risk and deception in this, as I informed you in the chapter concerning tumors originating from veins and arteries.
If the protrusion of the navel is due to intestines or viscera, instruct the patient to hold himself still and stand upright, stretched out.  Then, apply compresses around the entire navel. Then, have him lie on his back before you.  Then, make a wide incision around the navel, at the location you marked with the ink.  Then, pull the middle of the growth upwards with a large hook.  Then, tie the incision site with a strong thread or silk thread, in a firm knot.
The knot of the ligature should be a slip knot. Then, open the center of the pulled growth above the ligature, insert your index finger, and search for the intestines. If you find them caught by the ligature, loosen the slip knot and push the intestines back into the abdomen. If you find a viscus, pull it out with a hook.
Cut off the excess. If you encounter a vein or artery, tie it off. Return to your work. Take two needles and thread strong threads through them. Insert the two needles into the incision you made around the growth, deep within it. Then, pull the growth tight at four points on the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the pulled flesh becomes infected and falls off on its own, or cut it when it becomes thin and infected.  Then, treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If the protrusion of the navel is from flesh that has grown there.
Or if it is from moisture, you should cauterize the growth, as we said, and remove the blood or moisture found within.  Then, treat it with what will heal the area.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how to treat it with medicines, and warned against treating it with iron so as not to cause ulcers. We have also mentioned cancer that originates in the uterus and warned against treating it.
The ancients stated that if the cancer is in a place where it can be completely removed, such as cancer in the breast or the thigh, and similar organs from which it can be extracted entirely, especially if it is small and newly formed, however, if it is advanced and large, it should not be approached.
I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me who achieved that.  If it is advanced, as we said, the procedure should proceed, causing the patient to sweat profusely several times, and then cauterize it if there is a clear fullness in the veins.
Then the patient is placed in a position that allows the procedure, then the appropriate hooks are placed on the cancer, and it is scraped from every side with the skin thoroughly, until nothing of its roots remains.  Allow the blood to flow, do not cut it quickly.
Indeed, squeeze the affected areas and drain all the thick blood with your hand or with any suitable instruments.  If during the procedure a large amount of blood is encountered from a severed artery or vein, constrict the vein until the bleeding stops, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.

Chapter Fifty-seven.
In the purification of boys and the treatment of their errors.
Circumcision is nothing more than the separation of a connection, like other surgeries. However, since it is a procedure performed by us, intentionally, and especially on boys, the best course of action must be determined.
The easiest path to safety, I say, is that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, because it was not used in their traditions, but is something we have adopted.
Through experience, I found that the majority of surgeons and barbers use purification with knives and scissors, and use thread, ligatures, and cutting with a nail.  I have tried all these methods.
I found nothing better than purification with scissors and tying with thread, because purification with a knife often causes skin damage, since the foreskin has two layers.  Perhaps the upper layer of skin is cut, while the lower layer remains.
One is forced to make another cut, causing further pain.  Purification with a cautery iron carries the risk of cutting the tip of the urethra, as it might have entered the opening.  As for purification with a nail, the skin might tear, ruining your work, or the boy's foreskin might be too short.
Many are born such that they do not require purification, as I have seen.  However, purification with scissors and tying with thread, experience has shown me, is superior, since the scissors have a balanced cut because the blade above is similar to the blade below.
When you grip the scissors with the blades balanced, the cut is even and takes place at the same time.  The tied thread then becomes like a wall around the urethral skin from all sides, with no error.  The procedure should begin by reassuring the boy.
Especially if he is someone who understands a little, that you are merely tying the thread around his urethra and leaving it for another day.  Then, please him and give him joy in any way you can.  What his mind accepts, and then, have him stand before you, upright.
Do not let him sit, and keep the scissors hidden in your sleeve or under your foot.  The boy's eyes should not see them or any of the tools.  Then, insert your hand into his urethra, inflate the foreskin and lift it upwards, until the glans of the urethra is exposed.
Then, clean it from any dirt that may have accumulated.  Then, tie the marked area with a doubled thread.  Then, tie a second thread a little lower.  Then, hold the lower thread firmly with your thumb and forefinger.  Cut between the two threads. Then, lift the foreskin upwards quickly and expose the glans.  Then, let the blood flow a little; this is better and lessens inflammation of the urethra.  Then, wipe it with a damp cloth. Then, dust it with the ash of burnt dried pumpkin, as this is the best I have tried, or with the dust of the charcoal; this is also excellent.
Then, lift the foreskin upwards quickly and expose the glans.  Then, let the blood flow a little; this is better and lessens inflammation of the urethra.  Then, wipe it with a damp cloth. This is a suitable scissor design for purification.
The blades should be straight, sharp, and nail-shaped. The blades' lengths should equal the handles' lengths. Cut it [the skin] evenly.  If you can't hold it with a fingernail, pull it with a hook and cut it.  If, after three days, the area below the glans remains swollen and inflamed, leave it until the inflammation subsides and gently peel it off.

Chapter Fifty-Eight.
On treating urine retention in the bladder.
Urine retention in the bladder occurs due to obstruction by a stone, coagulated blood, pus, or overgrown flesh, or similar causes.  If you treat it with the methods of treatment described in the classifications, it will be cured.
If the urine does not pass, and you suspect the obstruction is from a stone that has damaged the neck of the bladder, the patient should be placed on their knees, then a person should sit on their back, extending their stomach towards the back of the sitter, and the patient should hold themselves as tightly as possible.
This will cause the bladder neck to push the stone backward, allowing the urine to pass.  If the urine does not pass by this method, and the patient's condition worsens, extraction with an instrument called a catheter, of which this is an image, should be employed.
It is made of silver, thin and smooth, hollow like a bird's feather tube, about a foot and a half long, with a gentle funnel at its tip.  The method for drawing the urine with it is to take a doubled thread and tie strong yarn or cotton to one end.
You insert the thread end into the catheter's tip and trim any excess yarn with nippers so that it fits snugly within the tube, like a button. Then, the catheter is lubricated with oil, butter, or egg white, and the patient sits on a chair.
The bladder and urethra are moistened with warm water and oil or other lubricating substances.  Then, the catheter is gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the root of the urethra. The urethra is then pulled upwards towards the navel, and the catheter is pushed further inside.
Once the urethra has been pulled up, the catheter is pushed further inside until it nears the anus.  Then, the penis is lowered, the catheter remaining inside, and the catheter is pushed until it reaches the bladder, where the patient feels that it has reached an empty space.
This is done in this manner because the urinary tract has curves.  Then, the thread with the yarn is pulled gently; the urine follows the yarn.  Then, it is removed, and the urine is drained, and the catheter is returned.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.

Chapter Sixty.
On removing stones.

I say that bladder stones are most often found in children, and a sign of them is that the urine expelled from the bladder is thin, like water.
Sand appears, and he rubs and plays with his penis.  It often hangs down, and the anus often protrudes in many of them.  Children usually recover from it before reaching fourteen years of age.  It is difficult in the elderly, and in young adults, it is intermediate between the two.
Those with larger stones have easier treatment, and the opposite is true for smaller ones.  When it comes to treatment, it is first necessary to irrigate the patient with an enema to remove all the impurities in his intestines, as this can prevent the stone from being detected during examination.

Either he will die or he will have constant dribbling of urine, because the wound will not heal. But try to push it out, or devise a way to break it using forceps so that you can remove it in pieces.

Insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains, it will lead to the corruption and festering of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt. Treat each type with a corresponding remedy until healed.
