Sixty-first Chapter.
In removing stones in women.
Stones rarely develop in women.  If one of them has a stone, its treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons, one being that the woman may be a virgin, and the second that you will not find a woman who allows herself to be examined by a physician.
If she is chaste or of the family, and the third that you will not find a woman skilled in this craft, especially manual work, and the fourth that the location of the incision on the stone in women is far from the stone's location, requiring a deep incision.
And in that there is danger. If necessity demands it, a skilled female physician should be sought; however, one is rarely found. If one is not found, seek a virtuous, compassionate male physician or bring in a skilled female midwife or a woman knowledgeable in this craft to provide some guidance.
She is brought in and instructed to perform all examinations on the stone. First, she is to examine if the woman is a virgin.  If so, she should insert her finger into the vagina and probe for the stone. If she finds it, she should press it beneath her finger.
If she is not a virgin but a married woman, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the woman's vagina and search for the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and applying firm pressure.
If she finds it, she should guide it from the mouth of the bladder downward as far as possible, until it reaches the root of the thigh. Then she should make the incision on the side of the vagina, near the root of the thigh, whichever side feels the stone in that area.
Her finger must remain on the stone, pressing it. The incision should be small at first. Then, the probe should be inserted into that small incision. If she feels the stone, she should widen the incision as much as she believes necessary to remove the stone. Know that there are many types of stones.
There are small and large stones, smooth and rough, long and round, and those with crevices. Recognize their types so you can determine what to do. If blood flows excessively, apply pulverized alum to the area, and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops. Then return to your work.
Until the stone is removed. Prepare the instruments mentioned for removing stones from men to assist in your work. If excessive bleeding occurs and you determine the bleeding is from a severed artery, apply a styptic to the area.
Tighten it with the dressings firmly and leave it. Do not repeat the procedure. Leave the stone; do not remove it.  The patient may die. Then treat the wound. When the intensity of the bleeding subsides after a few days, and the area becomes infected, return to your work until the stone is removed.
Chapter Sixty-Two.
On the puncture of watery blisters.
A watery blister is the accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum underneath the scrotum's skin surrounding the testicle, called the scrotum. It may also be in a special membrane prepared by nature on one side of the testicle.
It may appear as another testicle, occurring between the scrotal skin and the white peritoneum we mentioned.  This is rare.  These blisters result from weakness affecting women, to which this material is attracted.  They may also be caused by injury to women.
This moisture can take on various colors: yellow, blood-red, dark-brown, or whitish-watery, the latter being the most common.  These are the signs by which it is recognized.
Where the water accumulates, if it's in the white peritoneum we mentioned, the swelling will be somewhat elongated and round, like an egg, and the testicle will not be visible because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a special membrane of its own, the swelling will be round on one side of the testicle.
For this reason, one might think it is another testicle. If the moisture is between the scrotal skin and the white peritoneum, it is palpable. To determine the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe previously described.  Judge its interior by the substance that emerges after probing.
If we move on to treatment with leeches, the patient should be bled, if possible, and if you see their body is full, then they should lie on their back on a slightly elevated surface, with many cloths beneath them, and you should sit to their left.
Instruct a servant to sit on the patient's right, extending the patient's penis toward one side of the scrotal skin, and towards the abdominal wall.  Then, take a wide scalpel and make a longitudinal incision through the scrotal skin from the center, towards the pubic area.
The incision should be made in a straight line parallel to the line dividing the scrotal skin in half, until you reach the white peritoneum.  Separate it carefully, taking care not to cut it. Make your separation from the side that is closest to the testicle, and continue the separation as much as possible.
Then, dissect the water-filled peritoneum widely and remove all the water.  Then, separate the edges of the incision with forceps, lift the peritoneum upward, avoiding the scrotal skin below, and cut the peritoneum as much as possible, either in one piece or in sections.
Especially its thin side; if you do not completely cut it, you cannot be certain that the water will not return.  If the testicle protrudes from its skin during the procedure, then replace it, and stitch the edges of the scrotal skin together.
Then treat it as you would any other surgical wound until healed.  If the testicle has been damaged by another ailment, it is necessary to tie the vessels in the sac to prevent bleeding, then separate the testicle from the sac, remove the testicle, and then treat it as we described.
If the accumulated water is in both areas, know that it is a hydrocele.  Incise the other side in the same manner as you did the first, and if you prefer to do the procedure only once, do so. Then wipe away the blood.
Insert wool soaked in oil or rose oil into the incisions. Place another layer of wool soaked in a mixture of water and oil over the testicles and the abdominal region.  Then place folded linen cloths (bandages) on top. Tie it from above with the six-sided bandage in this manner:
Then take two cloths, stitch them together, and fill them with fluffy wool in this manner.  Make the straps for binding from wet cotton or wool and secure them tightly with a belt around the waist.  One strap should be positioned from the buttocks to the back.
The second strap should extend towards the pubic area and the other two straps should be placed on the thighs, below. The remaining two straps should be placed on the groin.  All straps should be tied together with a waist belt.  This hydrocele can also be treated by cauterization instead of incision with a metal instrument.
This involves using a gentle, heated scalpel to cut the scrotal skin, exposing the white membrane containing the water. Then, use another heated cauterizing instrument in this manner:
It resembles a Greek eye; then, cauterize the membrane with it while it is hot until all the moisture is expelled.  Then, pull the membrane with forceps and use the cautery to strip away the surrounding layer, cutting it as much as possible to remove all of it, taking care not to burn the testicle.
Know that this method is safer and less prone to bleeding than the incision method.  If, during the incision or cauterization process, you encounter a hot swelling, bleeding, or any other complication, then you should treat each problem with the appropriate remedy, as previously described.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot tolerate this procedure because of its unpleasantness, then use the cautery method as described, if it proves appropriate based on the previous evidence.
If the discharge is watery, the patient should sit on a raised chair. Then, cauterize the swelling below with a wide scalpel, and ensure the cauterization extends along the body, maintaining a wide opening so that all the water drains out. Then, place cotton on the area and bandage it.
Leave it to drain any remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not drain during the cauterization process, it is likely due to the white membrane obstructing the wound opening, preventing the water from exiting.
In that case, you should insert a goose quill, or the instrument used to extract the water from the afflicted, previously described, or slightly enlarge the wound opening.
Know that the water may return and accumulate after six months or so. If it does accumulate, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should rest during the days.
Sixty-third chapter.
Concerning the rupture of fleshy tissues and their treatment.
Know that rupture of these fleshy tissues is often a perilous condition leading to death.  Therefore, we recommend leaving it alone and avoiding treatment, though I will describe the procedure and types of swelling.
I say that many swellings occur in the bodies that compose the flesh, from numerous causes.  Either from excessive heat concentrated in the flesh, or from a blow.  The swelling's color matches the body's color, and contains no water.
The swelling is firm and may be hardened, its color like mud, and without sensation. It may be due to the constriction and swelling of blood vessels, as previously mentioned, or to the swelling of veins.  If it is due to the swelling of arteries, the swelling will dissipate.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be operated on at all.  However, if it is due to swelling of the veins, nothing will dissipate when pressed with the fingers. This type can be ruptured, and in conjunction with the surrounding flesh.
This involves cutting the scrotum, then pulling the testicle upwards, removing it from the white peritoneum, freeing the suspension from the vessels, tying off the vessels, and cutting off the suspension after freeing it from all sides of the testicle. If the testicle has adhered to the growing flesh.
Then the testicle must be extracted and cut. If the adhesion is between any of the membranes or vessels, then all such adhesion must be freed and cut in a circular fashion.
If the flesh is growing at the point of adhesion, which is behind, then all of it must be cut, and the testicle removed as we said.  Once your procedure is complete, treat the large wound with moist wool in rose-oil and a potion, then treat it with further remedies until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Four.
On treating the dropsy associated with the dropsy (or, a specific type of dropsy).
Dropsy is a swollen, twisted tumor, resembling a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the limbs. It hinders the afflicted person from movement, exercise, and walking. The treatment for this affliction is similar to what was mentioned before.
However, it is necessary to mention the procedure as the ancients did. I say that the patient should sit on a raised chair, then the lower limbs should be pushed downwards, and the scrotum's skin should be held with your fingers, along with the vessels close to the penis.
A servant of yours should hold it and pull it forcefully, then make a slanted cut with a wide, sharp scalpel, close to the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.  Then, strip them from every side, as I have described to you in the dissection of the arteries in the temples. Then, insert a needle with a double thread into them.
Tie it at the initial points where the dropsy appeared and also at the end, then cut it in the middle with a vertical incision along the body and remove the accumulated turbid and corrupted fluids.
Then, treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds that take time to heal, so that the vessels that nourish one of the limbs fall off. There is no harm in that. If the dropsy affects all the vessels, one limb must be removed with the vessels, lest the egg be deprived of nourishment. Before cutting the vessels, it will wither and be of no use.
Chapter Sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal dropsy.
This dropsy occurs when a rupture appears in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen, near the groin area, causing the intestines to protrude into one of the limbs.
This rupture is either from a tear in the peritoneum or from its extension.  These two types arise from many causes, such as a blow, a jump, a shout, or lifting a heavy object, and the like.  If it is from an extension of the peritoneum, it occurs gradually over a long period of time.
It does not occur suddenly.  If from the peritoneum's extension, the swelling is gradual, reaching considerable depth because the peritoneum compresses the intestines.  If from a tear in the peritoneum, it initially causes severe pain abruptly, and the swelling is readily apparent under the skin nearby.
The intestines may protrude outside the peritoneum, and with them, fluid may escape, thus making this dropsy intestinal and fluid-filled.  There may also be wind, or the intestines may collect waste material, which will then be trapped, causing the patient's death.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially if compressed.  Treatment of these types of illness with iron is dangerous; one should therefore avoid it.  The procedure is to instruct the patient to push the intestines back inside their body cavity if they can be returned.
Then, the patient lies on their back with you in front, and their legs are raised.  Then, the skin around the groin is pulled upward, and the entire skin of the testicles is cut lengthwise.  Hooks are inserted into the edges of the cut, to the extent necessary to open the wound.
The incision should be large enough to allow the testicle to emerge.  Then, the peritoneum under the skin of the testicles is peeled back until the white, tough peritoneum is exposed on all sides.  At that point, insert your index finger into the area below the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum beneath the testicle's skin and the second peritoneum, release the adhesions behind the testicle.  Then, with your right hand, pull inwards on the testicle's skin, while with your left hand you pull the white peritoneum upward and lift the testicle with the peritoneum towards the incision.
You instruct the attendant to pull the testicle upward, and you completely release the adhesions behind it.  You use your finger to check for any twisted intestines within the tough white peritoneum; if you find any, push them back into the abdomen below.
Then, take a needle with a thick thread, twisted from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin near the incision.  Then, cut the ends of the thread, so that there are four threads. Then, intertwine some of the threads together in a firm knot and tie the peritoneum, which we said is beneath the scrotal skin, tightly on both sides.  Then, also tie off the ends of the threads tightly, so that nothing of the vessels nourishing it can reach it. To prevent a hot tumor from developing, and make a second ligature outside the first ligature, a distance of less than two fingers.  After these two ligatures, leave a portion of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin the size of a finger.
To prevent a hot tumor from developing, and make a second ligature outside the first ligature, a distance of less than two fingers.  After these two ligatures, leave a portion of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin the size of a finger. Cut the rest of it in a circle, and remove the testicle along with it.  Then make an incision below the scrotal skin that bleeds and discharges, as described previously. Then use wool soaked in oil and place it on the wound, and use the ligature that we have described.
The white peritoneum, which we said to cut carefully to prevent bleeding, often results in this.  Leave the ligature until it falls off on its own. If it is slow to fall, then soak it in hot water and apply something that will make it sweat, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the usual treatment for surgical wounds until it heals.
Sixty-sixth Chapter.
In this case of wind-related swellings, I have seen no one dare to treat it with iron.  The ancients mentioned making it as we described in the swelling with a cyst, by gently tying the vessels below the incision.
Then it is incised in the middle, and the swelling is treated to open it until the vessels fall out, and the wound is treated as we described until it heals.
Sixty-seventh Chapter.
In the hernia that occurs in the groin.
The hernia in the groin may occur, as we said, and nothing from the intestines descends into the female organs.  If it does descend, it is slight and always returns.
But if time passes, the tear in the peritoneum increases, until the intestines and testicles descend into the scrotum.  This arises from the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin, as we said. This is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and protrudes the place, bulging.  Its treatment is by cauterization, as I previously described.
And it may be treated with iron in this manner: the patient lies on his back before you, then you make a transverse incision in the swollen, protruding hernial area about three finger-widths wide, and then cauterize the peritoneum under the skin.
Until the white peritoneum beneath is exposed, then you take a reed, place it on the protruding area of the peritoneum, and press it into the depth of the abdomen. Then you sew the two protruding places on the reed end of the peritoneum, and attach them together with stitching.
Then remove the reed.  Do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I instructed you in treating intestinal hernia. Then treat it with the treatment for surgical cases.
If the stitches break, clean them and close the wound until it heals, as the peritoneum will contract and the hernia will not increase.
And this is the best approach in this situation, as it is closer to safety.
Chapter Sixty-eight.
On the relaxation of the scrotum's skin.
The skin of the scrotum often relaxes in some people without the underlying tissues relaxing, making its appearance unsightly.  Therefore, anyone wishing to treat this should lay the patient on their back.
He should cut all the relaxed skin away from the thick skin, and then bring the edges together. If you prefer, first stitch the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, securing them, and then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the treatment of other surgical wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Sixty-ninth Chapter.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I should not mention it in this book.  However, I have mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that a physician will be knowledgeable about it if asked, and will know how to treat those who have been affected, and the other, that we often need to castrate animals for our benefit.
Like sheep, oxen, and cats, and similar animals.  I say that castration is of two types, either by crushing or by incision and cutting.  The method of crushing is that we sit the animal in warm water until its testicles relax and soften and hang down.
Then you crush them with your hand until they are broken and no longer discernible to the touch.  As for castration by incision and cutting, you must hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the suspensory ligaments and make a single incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles protrude, then cut them after removing the coverings, leaving only the thin membrane covering the vessels. This method of castration is better than crushing.
Because crushing may leave some of the testicles behind, so the animal may desire mating.  Then the wound is treated until healed.
Chapter Seventy.
In the treatment of hermaphrodites.
The hermaphrodite in men comes in two types. One appears as a space or a fold in the scrotum resembling a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may flow from the fold in the scrotum.
In women, there is one type. It is above the vulva, on the pubic area, like small, erect, prominent testicles. One of them resembles a man's penis, and the two together resemble the vulva.  Treatment for these three types.
The two types of men and the single type of women require the removal of the excess flesh, so that its effect is resolved and then treated with the treatment for other wounds until healed.  As for the second type of man from whom urine flows from the scrotum, there is no action or cure for it at all.
Chapter seventy-one.
On the cutting of the foreskin and the fleshy growth from the vulva of women.
The clitoris may increase in size beyond the natural state, becoming discolored and unsightly.  In some women, it may even enlarge to the point of resembling that of men, leading to the possibility of sexual intercourse.
Therefore, one should grasp the excess clitoris with one's hand or forceps and cut it off, but do not cut too deeply, especially into the root, to prevent bleeding.
Then treat it with surgical remedies until it heals.  As for the fleshy growth, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb, filling it.
And perhaps it extends outwards, like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients called it the 'tail-like disease'. It should be cut, like the clitoris, and treated until it heals.
The Seventy-Second Chapter.
On the treatment of ruptures.
A rupture is when a woman's vulva is not perforated, or the perforation is small, and it may be either natural, born with it, or accidental.  The accidental rupture is from a prior cause, and it may arise from excess overgrown flesh.
or from thin or thick peritoneum, and may be located deep within the uterus, or on its sides, or at its top or bottom.  It prevents intercourse, procreation, childbirth, and possibly menstruation.  Knowledge of this is acquired through observation by the midwife.
If the obstruction is visible and nearby, but if not visible, she should examine it with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is from thin peritoneum and is close to the labia, then proceed to puncture it.  To do this, form a sort of fold on the labia, and then place both index fingers on it.
And the woman should be lying on her back with her legs spread, then forcefully spread the labia until the thin peritoneum is punctured and the obstruction opens.  Then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area.  The woman should be cohabited with every day, so that the area does not adhere again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, it should be incised with a broad scalpel resembling a leaf of a date palm. If the obstruction is from overgrown flesh, then suspend it with forceps and cut it.  Have with you medicines to stop bleeding without cauterization, such as acacia, sheean, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then a wide lead tube should be used to prevent the wound from closing quickly.  Hold it for a few days, and use a wick of dry linen, then treat with the rest of the treatment for an abscess until healed.  Another growth of flesh may appear in the uterus, and it should be cut in this manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor.  A cancerous tumor which is in the uterus should not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red boils that appear on the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the veins' openings, causing a constant flow of copious blood.  If hemorrhoids progress, they become warts.  Their types and signs have been mentioned in the classification, and we describe them here.
What is curable and what is not.  I say that if the hemorrhoids and warts are deep within the uterus and do not appear to the touch, then there is no treatment with iron.  Those which are on the mouth of the uterus, where they can be felt, are those that can be treated.
The woman should enter a cool room, then the warts should be scraped with a lancet or a rough cloth and cut off at their roots.  Then, if blood flows from one of the blood-cutting instruments, it should be stopped without causing a puncture.
Such as henna, shian, and frankincense, etc. Then, elevate her legs against the wall for an hour, then lower them into cool water. If the bleeding persists, have her sit in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, and astringent herbs, etc. Then, take barley flour, mix it with honey and vinegar.
Apply this to her back. If the bleeding stops, then stop. Otherwise, apply leeches to the lower back and breasts, without restrictions.  Then take wool, dip it in the juice of rue, or the juice of comfrey, or the juice of the branches of privet, with astringent drink, and apply it to the area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until healed. As for the red sore, it resembles the heads of boils, rough in appearance. It should be cut off as much as has appeared, just as with the warts, and treated until healed.
The Seventy-fourth Chapter.
On treating abscesses in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may appear in the uterus, such as cancer, stony tumors, ulcers, wasting diseases, devouring diseases, polyps, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors.  All of these diseases and their types and signs have been described in the classification.
Therefore, in this article, we must discuss the hot tumor that appears in the uterus, if it is one of the tumors that have accumulated for a duration. How is it treated with iron?  Observe if, in its initial stages, the tumor's pain is severe, with throbbing, inflammation, and fever.
And if you see it red, if touched, do not rush to treat it. Treat it with what aids maturation, until these symptoms subside.  Then, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats and lean back.
And draw her legs toward the lower abdomen, spreading her thighs, and placing her arms under her knees, securing them with an appropriate bandage.
Then, the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument to open the cervix, the image of which will be presented later.  If you wish to use this instrument, you must estimate the depth of the woman's uterus, so that what enters from the instrument does not exceed the depth of the uterus.
If the instrument is deeper than the depth, this will harm the patient.  Therefore, applicators should be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the instrument from entering the depth of the uterus entirely. The spiral that runs in the instrument must be on the upper side.
While you hold the instrument, the attendant rotates the spiral until the cervix opens.  If a boil appears and is soft and thin when touched by hand, it should be incised at its head with a broad scalpel. When all the matter has been discharged.
A soft wick dipped in rose oil or green oil with some astringent should be placed in the wound. The wick should extend outside the incision in the cervix.  Outside, on the uterus and pubis, place pure wool dipped in boiled baker's water.
Then, treat it after two days with the ointment suitable for it until it heals.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then water boiled with soss or long zarawand root.  Then return to the ointment.  If the boil is hidden inside the uterus.
Iron should be avoided in its treatment; it should be treated as described in the classification.
Chapter seventy-five.
On teaching midwives how to treat living fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural form.
The midwife must first know the natural form of childbirth.  Signs include the woman drawing downwards, longing to smell the air, and finding the postpartum state easier to bear.
A rapid delivery indicates a natural birth, with the baby emerging headfirst, and the placenta either coming out with it or hanging from the navel.  If you see these signs, you should press her abdomen to hasten the delivery of the baby.
If it emerges headfirst, the placenta comes out with it, and it is cleaned well of those impurities.  Any birth other than this is unnatural and undesirable, as the baby might be born feet first or with arms and legs emerging before the head.
A single arm or leg, or the head and an arm, or a twisted position, or perhaps the baby emerging on its back—these are all undesirable forms.  The midwife should be skillful, gentle, with all these things, and careful to avoid mistakes.
I am explaining each form and how to address each.  If the baby emerges headfirst, the natural way, and the woman's labor is strong and difficult, and you see her strength weakening, have her sit on a chair and instruct other women to support her.
Bind her uterus with boiled licorice and moist ointments.  Then the midwife should insert a small scalpel between her fingers, and cut the placenta, or cut it with her fingernail, to drain its moisture.  Then press the woman's abdomen until the baby is expelled.
If it does not descend, the woman should be injected with licorice saliva mixed with shirg's fat.  After the injection, tell her to cough, and use a reed to make her sneeze. Hold her mouth and nose.  The baby will come out soon. If the baby's arms emerge first, gently push them back slowly.
If the baby fails to descend, place the woman on a stool, raising her legs.  Then shake the stool on the ground, while the woman grips to prevent falling.  If the hands do not enter and the baby is dead, cut them off and pull out the rest of the baby.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them out gently; he will come out.  If the baby emerges on its feet, push its feet upward, then turn the baby a little at a time until it is straight, and then pull one leg slightly.
If they emerge to the thighs, tell her to cough and make her sneeze with a reed.  It will come out.  If it does not come out by these methods, gently rotate the baby back into its natural position, and it will then come out smoothly. If it still refuses to come out by all these methods...
Take saliva of the *khatmi* plant, licorice saliva, and *shirg* fat, and dissolved gum.  Pound all of them well in a mortar.  Then, apply this mixture to the woman's vagina and lower abdomen.  Then, make her sit in lukewarm water until it reaches the thighs.
If you see that the lower part has softened, make her a compress of myrrh and have her endure it. If she holds it for an hour, have her sit on a chair, then make her sneeze, and block her mouth and nose, and gently press her lower abdomen.  The baby will come out immediately.  The baby emerges on its knees and hands.
If it emerges in this manner, gently insert the hands a little at a time.  Then, the woman lies on her back, with her buttocks hanging down while lying on a bed and pressing her hands together.  Then gently press above her abdomen. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so that her feet are elevated. If the baby does not emerge, take both her legs and move them vigorously.  Then press above the thighs gently until the baby moves upwards, then the midwife inserts her hand and gently positions the baby.
Tell the woman to strain until the baby emerges. The baby emerges sideways, with one arm hanging down.  Gently reposition the arm. If you can't reposition it, have the woman stand up and walk. If she can't walk, have her lie down.
Shake her legs vigorously, then use the poultice described from saliva. If the delivery is obstructed, have her sit on a chair, then work on repositioning the arm and putting the baby in the natural position. Make the woman sneeze and tell her to strain until it emerges.
The baby emerges on its back, arms outstretched, and its face towards the mother's back. The midwife should grasp the baby's hand and gently turn and straighten it.  Once straightened, apply the fat and saliva we have described to the baby's forehead.
Then tell her to move gently. Once she does this, have her sit on a chair and tell her to lean to her right side. Then tell her to strain and make her sneeze with the forceps.  The baby will emerge, upright on its side.
If it descends into the cervix in this manner with the placenta, cut the placenta with your fingernail until it is free of moisture.  If it is attached to the baby's face or neck, cut it from the umbilical cord to prevent suffocation and death. Then push the baby inside until it is in the correct position.
Tell the woman to strain repeatedly, and it will emerge easily.  For the birth of twins or multiple fetuses, know that twins frequently are born, and sometimes three or four are born and survive; however, this is rare.  As for five, this is unnatural and they will never survive.
And they are surrounded by a single placenta, however numerous, and they are separated by a membranous partition attached to the umbilical cord of each.  They may emerge like a single baby, naturally and easily, or emerge in a difficult manner, as described.
If some of them are difficult to deliver and present the unnatural forms we have mentioned, treat them as we have described.  Let the midwife be gentle and do everything with care and patience, and use any means available to achieve safety.
It is possible for one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or more than ten fetuses to form in the womb, and it has been verified that one woman miscarried seven and another fifteen, all of them conceived.
By the estimation of the Exalted and Knowing, all of this should be known to the midwife, for she may encounter such a case sometime and should know how to deal with it appropriately.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
In the delivery of a dead fetus.
If, having treated the dead fetus with the methods of treatment described in the section, it does not emerge and you are forced to intervene manually, then you should consider the woman's condition.  If she is healthy and has no symptoms that cause concern for her well-being, the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
Her head should be tilted downward and her legs raised, and an attendant should hold her securely from both sides, or bind her to the bed, so that her body does not shift when the fetus is drawn out.
Then, the mouth of her uterus is moistened with the moist ointments, along with the saliva of the [specified] herb, fenugreek, and linseed. Then, the midwife anoints her hand with these ointments and salivas, and gently inserts it into the uterus. She then seeks a place to insert the hooks, targeting the appropriate locations on the fetus.
If the fetus is positioned to descend head-first, insert the hooks into its eyes, or the back of its head, or mouth, or palate, or under its chin, or collarbone, or the areas near the ribs, or beneath the clavicles.
And if its descent is on its feet, then at the pubic region and the middle ribs.  The hook should be held in the right hand, its curve placed between the fingers of the left hand.  The hand should be inserted gently and a hook driven into one of the aforementioned locations.
Until it reaches an empty space, then another or a third hook is inserted opposite it, if needed, to ensure an equal pull without it tilting to one side.  The pulling should be even and not just in a straight line, but also moving in all directions.
To facilitate its removal, as is done with a tooth when extracting it.  During this process, the pulling should be relaxed if it gets stuck in one place. The midwife should then anoint some of her fingers with ointment and insert them to rotate any part that's stuck.  If part of the fetus emerges, the hooks should be moved to other locations.
She should do this a little higher until the whole fetus is removed. If a hand emerges before the rest and cannot be pushed back due to its being compressed, it should be wrapped in a cloth and pulled out until the whole hand is extracted and then severed.
Either at the wrist joint or the elbow.  This should also be done for the other hand and the legs. If the fetal head is large and experiences pressure during delivery or has accumulated water in its head, a pointed, slender instrument should be inserted between the fingers.
Using it, the head is opened to release the water, or it is compressed with the instrument called a *mashdakh*, whose image is shown with the images of other instruments in the following chapter.
Likewise, if the fetus has an unusually large head, the skull should be cut open or compressed with the *mashdakh*, as mentioned before.  Then the bones should be removed with forceps. If the head emerges and gets stuck at the collarbone, it should be cut open to allow the moisture in the chest to drain.
Then the chest will close.  If the chest does not close and cannot be extracted, cut as much as possible. If the lower abdomen or belly is swollen or contains tumors, it should be punctured to allow the moisture to drain.
If the fetus is positioned on its feet, pulling it out and guiding it toward the cervix is easy. If it is stuck at the abdomen or chest, it should be pulled with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and the abdomen or chest should be cut open to allow the contents to drain.
If all other organs are removed and the head returns and is stuck, the midwife should insert her left hand. If the cervix is open, she should insert her hand into the cervix, grasp the head, pull it with her fingers toward the cervix, then insert a hook or two and pull it out with them.
If the cervix has adhered to a hot, inflamed tumor, one should not force it. Instead, apply moist, oily substances, use bandages, and sit in relaxing, moist water.
If the fetus is on its side, if possible, use the methods described for a living fetus.  If not possible, cut and remove it.  Absolutely no part of the placenta should be left inside. Once you are finished with the treatment, use the treatments mentioned for hot tumors that arise in the uterus.
If bleeding occurs, she should sit in constricting water and use other treatments.  I have witnessed a woman who became pregnant, then the fetus died inside her, and subsequently she became pregnant again.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a tumor appeared in her groin, swelling until it opened and discharged pus.  I was called to treat her, and treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close.  I then applied some potent, drawing ointments to it.
A bone then emerged from the area, and after some days, another bone came out. I was amazed by this, as the abdomen is not a place where bone is found.  I concluded that they were bones from the dead fetus. I searched the wound and removed many bones from the fetus's skull.
I continued this process until I removed many bones. The woman was in the best of health and lived like that for a long time, with a slight discharge of pus from the area. I have presented this unusual case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the skilled physician attempts to achieve with his hands in treatment.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
It contains images of the instruments needed for removing the fetus.
Image of a spiral used to dilate the cervix.
This is the binder, with which books are bound together. It has two spirals at the ends of the wooden planks, but this spiral should be gentler than the binder and should be either ebony or boxwood.  The width of each plank should be two finger-widths.
Their edges should be about a finger's width, and their length should be one and a half spans. In the middle of the planks, there are two rods of the same wood, firmly attached to them. Their length should be half a span, or slightly more, and their width about two fingers' width, or slightly more. These two rods.
They are the ones that enter the cervix to dilate it when turning the spirals; an image of another, similar instrument, gentler and lighter.
Made of ebony or boxwood, shaped like tongs, but with two rods at the end, as you see. Each rod is about a span long and two fingers wide.  If you want to dilate the cervix with it...
Then have the woman lie on a bed, legs spread apart, and insert these two rods, tightly, into the cervix.  You hold the end of the instrument between her legs, and then open your hand with the instrument, as you would with tongs, to whatever degree you want to dilate the cervix. So that the midwife can do as desired.  An image of another spiral, mentioned by the ancients.
