Chapter seventy-eight.
In the removal of the placenta.
If the placenta remains after childbirth, it is necessary to instruct the woman to hold herself still, then make her sneeze with a [wooden] implement, and grip your hand firmly on her mouth and nostrils.  If it comes out this way, well and good. Otherwise, take a measure.
Pierce a hole in the cover, place in it herbs that open the cervix, such as rue, mint, dill, chamomile, wormwood, sage, and valerian – all of these medicines, or some of them;  immerse them in water and place over a fire.  Then place a silver tube on the hole of the pot’s cover, and the other end in the woman’s cervix.
Hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus, then have her sneeze, as we said.  If the placenta comes out quickly, well and good.  If it remains after this treatment, and does not come out, order the midwife to dip her left hand in the fat of a she-camel or the saliva of the sea-hawk.
Then she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the placenta. If she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out slowly, little by little. If it is stuck deep within the uterus, insert the hand as we described until, if the placenta is found, pulling it out slowly, little by little, gently.
So that the uterus does not fall during strong pulling, but it is necessary to gently move it to the sides, right and left, and then increase the amount of pulling. Then it will respond and be freed from the adhesion.  If the cervix is closed, we have described the treatment with sneezing and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, beware of force when re-pulling, but it is necessary to tie what has come out of it to the woman's thigh, then inject her with the four-part ointment.  For it will putrefy her after some days.
It will dissolve and come out, but if it putrefies, a foul odor will rise from it to the stomach and head, and that will harm the patient. Therefore, a suitable decoction must be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients have tried the decoction of the incense and dried figs, as a picture of the instrument with which a woman is fumigated when menses and placenta are retained, and the like.
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or made of copper.  The narrow end is placed at the vulva, and the wide end over the fire.  The incense is placed on the embers; it is held until the incense is gone and then replaced with more.
Chapter Seventy-nine.
In the treatment of the unperforated anus.
Many boys are born with unperforated anuses, which may be blocked by a thin membrane.  The midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger, or else cut it with a sharp scalpel, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then apply wool soaked in wine and oil, and treat with ointments until healed.  If you fear it will close again, insert a lead tube into the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
The anus may also be blocked by an abscess or a tumor.  In this case, the abscess should be incised, and then treated with the remedies mentioned, and with cauterization.
Chapter Eighty.
On treating warts that appear on the lower parts of the body.
Warts that occur on the lower parts are a complication and thickening that happens near the anus, either externally or in the space on one side.  There may be one or more warts.
If this thickening persists, it may open and discharge a whitish watery fluid or thin pus.  Some of these warts may penetrate into the rectum and intestines.  If they are non-penetrating, they may be identified by what is expelled from them in the stool and wind when using the laxative.
Possibly worms may also be expelled from them.  Some warts, if located in the space and penetrating into the bladder or urethra, may be found. Some may penetrate into the hip joint or tailbone.  The penetrating wart towards the anus can be recognized, as well as those that do not penetrate.
Insert your index finger into the anus and introduce a thin probe of copper or iron into the wart. If there is no deviation within the wart, then if there is a deviation, insert a thin lead probe, or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair on your finger. If you don't feel it at all, and nothing like stool, wind, or worms, as we mentioned, comes out of the opening, know that it is not penetrating, so proceed with treatment.
If the wart penetrates the bladder or urethra, the sign is the discharge of urine from it, and the inability of the affected area to heal with medications.  If it penetrates the hip joint or tailbone, the sign is the probe reaching there.
If there is no deviation to the bone, and pain is present in the affected area, and pus constantly issues from it, and if the treatment is ineffective and does not heal with ointment, then all these penetrating warts will never heal.
Treatment for these is a futile effort for those ignorant physicians who attempt it.  For those not penetrating and not chronic, healing is hoped for through the application of iron, as I describe and have tested.  This involves placing the patient on their back before you.
Raise his legs upward and his thighs angled toward his abdomen. Then, insert a copper or lead probe into the wart, if it deviates, so you will know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the buttocks, you should insert your index finger into the buttocks.
If you feel the probe has penetrated through your finger, exposed and without feeling any tissue or flesh between your finger and the probe, then be certain it is penetrating. Do not waste effort on it, for it will not heal, as we have said.
They say it heals in some rare cases.  A treatment that may be effective is to heat a thin cauterizing iron, according to the size of the wart, as previously mentioned.  Insert it hot into the wart until it reaches the area near the buttocks, then repeat this two or three times.
So that you know that all excess, solidified flesh, resembling a bird's feather tube, has been burned. Then treat it with a thread soaked in oil until the burned flesh comes out, and then treat it with healing ointments if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with other treatments.  If you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger in the buttock, and there is a thick layer of flesh or membrane between the probe and the finger, and you see the wart just below the skin's surface, then cut the skin from the beginning of the wart. While making the incision, keep the probe within the wart, until the incision reaches where the probe tip ends. Remove and discard the probe. Then, clean away the solidified flesh, which resembles a bird's feather tube, leaving nothing behind.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If bleeding overwhelms you and prevents you from completing the excision of that flesh, the best and quickest remedy is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine.  Cauterization by fire combines two beneficial effects: burning the excess flesh.
Stop the bleeding and dry the moisture. Then treat it with a thread soaked in oil or pulverized sulfur mixed with oil, until the area becomes suppurative and the burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that promote the growth of solid flesh.
These are the medicines that contract and dry. If it is cured, it is healed, and there is no need to return. If the wart extends deep into the buttock and away from the body surface, insert your finger into the buttock and search with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it there is a barrier of peritoneum or flesh, and it is close to piercing through, then the only action is one of hope and expectancy.  This is done by employing one of three methods: either cauterizing it, as we have said.
Or, cut it open until you reach its bottom, so you can apply the thread and treatment from close proximity.  Do not extend the cut too far, lest you sever the muscle surrounding the buttock, causing involuntary bowel movement.  Then, treat it as previously described.  Perhaps it will be healed, as we said.
Or, pierce that barrier and puncture it, after cutting it to the vicinity of the buttock, as we said, either with a probe or another sharply pointed instrument.  Then cleanse all the accumulated flesh in the cut to the extent possible.
Then, close the entire wound, including the cut and the upper opening, until it is sealed, leaving the wart open within the buttock. This will be less burdensome for the patient.  The wart may be healed in this manner.
If, when inserting the probe into the wart and it is on one side of the buttock, near the surface of the body, along with the skin and the circumference of the buttock, then take a probe with a perforated tip like a cobbler's awl, in this manner:.
Thread five or more strands of yarn through it. Then, insert the probe with the thread into the wart until you reach its bottom.  If it is protruding within the buttock margin from the inside, near it.
Pull the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the buttock and bringing out the end of the thread. Join both ends and tie them tightly. Leave it for a day or two, tightening the thread firmly whenever it cuts into the flesh, until the flesh between the ends of the threads breaks and falls out.  Then treat the wound until it heals and is completely recovered.
If the opening of the wart is not protruding, make it protrude as best you can, but not if it is deep within, for you should not do this to the muscle for fear of cutting it. Then, do to it as we mentioned above until it heals.
The shape of the pointed lancet used to pierce warts should have a very sharp curve on one side and the other side should not be sharp so as not to cut anything unnecessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-one.
Concerning hemorrhoids that bleed, their incision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are within the anus, resembling red swellings or like grapes, and come in small and large sizes, and blood constantly flows from them.
They can be single or multiple, and some are found outside the anus, at its edges. However, those located outside the anus are often less moist; a yellowish liquid or a little blood drips from them constantly, and they are the color of the body.
The treatment for those inside the anus is to instruct the patient to defecate and strain until the anus protrudes and the polyps are visible.  Then quickly grasp and remove them with forceps, or with your fingernail, cutting them at their base, if the forceps do not adhere due to their moisture and laxity.
Then grip it with a rough cloth or a piece of sharpened linen or pull it out with your fingers, then cut it, and afterward sprinkle some sharp powders over the cut to act as a cauterization, or cauterize it as previously described in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat it with other remedies until it heals. If the anus does not protrude, inject the patient with an enema containing a little sting to wash out what is inside the anus and cause it to quickly protrude when the patient strains.  As for polyps outside the anus, their treatment is easy and straightforward.
To remove it, grasp it with your fingernail or forceps, and cut it.  Then treat it with the methods mentioned until it heals.  For those who dislike cutting with metal, it is better to use a needle. Take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the polyp upward and pierce it with the needle at its base from the opposite side. Tie the ends of the thread below the needle, which is positioned across, and pull the polyp firmly. Then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this with all the polyps, leaving one unpierced.
To allow the excess blood to drain, place a cloth soaked in rose oil or myrrh on the anus.  Have the patient rest, and leave it until the polyps fall off.  If the polyps fall off, treat them with ointments and other remedies until they heal.
As for treating fissures, they often arise from dryness, and a blockage of natural processes. If they are chronic and no medicine works, it is necessary to scrape them with a scalpel or your fingernail until they become moist and the upper crust, which prevents them from healing, is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more forcefully than before until it bleeds and peels. Then treat it, and it will heal.
Eighty-second Chapter.
On the treatment of ingrown nails, both ingrown and not ingrown, dry warts, and the ant (a type of skin lesion).
A nail is a rounded, skin-colored swelling resembling a nail head, occurring throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and toes, and causing pain when walking.
It is necessary to cut around the nail, then grasp it with a scalpel or a hook and pull it out from its root.  Then treat it. Or, if desired, cauterize it as previously described in the relevant chapter.  As for dry warts, each is a small, rough, hardened, rounded protuberance on the surface of the body.
They often appear on the hands of children.  Treatment involves stretching the wart, cutting it, or puncturing it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls off.  Or, if desired, cauterize it with fire or a strong medicine, as previously described.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts may be removed with the instrument I mentioned in the treatment of the ant.  The ant is also a small, hardened, thick protuberance on the surface of the body, extending deep into it.  When cold affects it, a sensation similar to the sting of ants is felt.
It occurs throughout the body, most often on the hands.  Treatment involves taking a tube made of strong goose or eagle feather and placing it around the ant, ensuring the tube encircles it completely. Then, rotate your hand with the tube until it breaks the skin around the ant.
And then push it deep into the flesh, and the ant will be pulled out from its root.  Or, if desired, you can do this with a copper or iron tube in this way:
The top of the tube should be solid and twisted, so it is easier for the fingers to control and turn it.  Or, if you prefer, cauterize the ant as previously described in the section on cauterizing warts.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Images of instruments used for injections in cases of rectal problems, diarrhea, and colic.
The injector can be made of silver, porcelain, or hollowed or hammered copper.  Small and large sizes of this instrument can be made for different users. Those used to treat small children, or those with tight or painful rectums, should be very slender.  Image of a large injector:.
The upper funnel, where the syringe is attached, is wide in this manner.  It has a partition where the syringe is attached, as you see.  The lower end that enters the rectum is very smooth and solid. On one side are two small holes, and on the other side is a single hole.
The size of the hole should be equal to or slightly thicker than a reed.  The syringe, in which medications are placed, made of animal bladder or sheep's intestine, should be shaped like a small spoon, with a diameter of a span.
It is pierced with many holes, as it turns, and there should be a distance of one finger's width between each hole.  Then, a strong thread of doubled yarn (of ten threads or so) is inserted into those holes and used to gather the syringe together like a spoon. When medication is placed inside, tighten the head of the injector between the stones firmly, then inject.  This is an image of the circular syringe and the hole.
The method of injection is to fill the intestine or bladder with the medication, warmed to a degree tolerated by the patient.  Beware that it is not too hot or too cold.  Then, the patient lies on their back with their legs raised.
A mat or blanket is placed beneath. Then, the tip of the injector is lubricated with ointment, egg white, or licorice saliva, or similar.  Then, it is gently inserted into the rectum.  The syringe's attendant grasps the syringe with both hands and squeezes it firmly until all the contents of the injector are emptied into the intestines.
Then the injector is removed, and the patient lowers their legs to the wall. If the medication is impelled to exit, the patient should hold it as long as possible.  If he sleeps with it all night, it will be more effective in the treatment.  If injected for diarrhea and expulsion is hastened, repeat the enema until recovery.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, the injector should be very gentle, smoother than the injectors used for children.  This is an image of a gentle injector, with its skin stretched tight.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
In the treatment of surgical wounds.
I have previously mentioned in the classification of the treatment of similar burns, and now I am describing here their treatment by hand and medication in full detail.  I say that wounds differ according to the thing causing the wound and according to the location of the wound.
The things that cause wounds are numerous, such as a blow from a stone, a cut from a sword or knife, a thrust from a spear or stick or arrow, and similar things.
As for wounds according to locations on the body, they are like a wound on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and similar organs.
And I will describe the treatment of some wounds to make them a standard and a rule for all other wounds, and I begin with the simple wounds of the head, especially as the compound ones will be mentioned in the first chapter of the third book of this book.
So I say that if a simple wound has occurred on the head, and it is not a bone fracture, I examine it. If it is from a blow with a stone or similar, and only the skin is bruised, and the wound is large, and I fear the occurrence of a burning swelling, then immediately lance the wound to release the pus. Do not delay this, and draw blood from him to the extent of his strength, if nothing prevents the bloodletting, and especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is scant.  Let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the injured area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drinking, and thick foods. If anything prevents bloodletting, then let him reduce his food intake, avoid meat and drinks, and apply a cloth soaked in rose oil, alone or with a drink that has a binding effect, to the wound if a burning swelling occurs.
And if you fear the burning swelling, apply to the wound, if it is fresh and has not been altered by the detrimental air, which I will describe to you shortly, and if it has been altered by the air, apply some ointments to it.
Until the pus is discharged, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and caused by a sword cut or similar, and its edges do not come together naturally, bring them together with sutures, as described in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin has exposed the bone and is hanging, and its attachment is only to a small flap, then cut it off. Then treat the wound with medicines that will build strong flesh to replace the skin.  Tell the patient to eat foods that have strength, such as heads of animals and similar items.
If a foul odor develops in the skin and it is not attached to the bone, cut away all the affected part and cauterize it. Then treat it. If in these wounds, an artery or vein is bleeding and the bleeding does not stop with the medications, search for the artery.
If you strike it and it does not stop bleeding, sever it with a scalpel and tie it off.  If the methods mentioned are not effective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops.  If these wounds are small and superficial, their treatment is easy and simple; just sprinkle this powder on them while they are still bleeding.
Before the air changes it, the recipe for the powder is: one part frankincense, two parts saffron, and three parts quicklime or unquenched lime.  Grind them all together, sift, and fill the wound with it.  Apply firm pressure so that it adheres well.
It will clot with the blood. Then, bind it from above with bandages and leave it without disturbing it as long as it does not swell.  The medicine will not be removed until it heals.  Quicklime alone can achieve this if you do not have frankincense and saffron.
This action can also be done with quicklime in many large wounds, if its application and binding are properly executed, and if the wounds are fresh and bleeding, and have not yet been affected by the air. However, if the wound has already undergone some change, especially during the summer,
Applying this powder is not effective in this case. Treat it as described earlier: apply some ripening ointments or a paste made from barley flour, water, and honey to encourage pus formation. Then treat it with further appropriate measures until healed.
If a minor fracture occurs in the bone, pull it with a splint.  The treatment of skull fractures is discussed in the section on neck injuries in the next section. If a wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment from a simple head wound.
If a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treatments that cause strong constriction, such as alum, verdigris, and the like, because they will injure and spasm the nerve.
Absolutely nothing cold should be applied to the wound, because the essence of the nerve is cold, and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs.  Use gentle remedies like moistened rose-water, repeatedly washed with fresh water, or diluted with oil or rose and zinc ointment.
Also wash with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner, and use moist remedies.  If the wound is large, use sutures or bring the edges together with strips, then treat until healed.
If the wound has a recess and pus has collected at the bottom, drain it from the lowest point.  If an artery has been severed and blood is flowing from the wound, tie it off, or cauterize it, if necessary.
If part or all of the hyoid bone has been severed, but the blood vessels are intact, bring together the skin edges with sutures on the hyoid cartilage. Do not touch the hyoid bone, but smooth it and return it to its natural shape, then tie it firmly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with astringent and drying ointments until healed. If the wound has affected the bone, probe it. If there are bone fragments, remove as many as possible. If not possible, leave it until the wound becomes necrotic and heals, making removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the powder we described. If the bone in the wound becomes necrotic and forms a fistula, treat it as described in the section on chest and shoulder surgery.
If the wound is from a spear or knife, and you see a recess, examine it. If air comes out when the patient breathes, know that it is a fatal wound.  If there is no recess, and the wound is fresh with blood, do not immediately apply the powder, nor tighten it, lest the blood be trapped in the recess.
For it will return to the heart and kill the patient. Instead, apply a drawing ointment twice daily. If no ointment is available, place a used cotton swab in the wound mouth to absorb the moisture that comes out, and let the patient sleep with the wound facing upward.
To drain the accumulated material. If three days or more have passed, and the patient shows no spasms, bad palpitations, or shortness of breath, and their general condition is good, know that the wound is sound. Treat it with sutures and other remedies until healed.
If it is difficult to see and the wound discharge is continuous, know that it has become a fistula. Treat it according to that section.  The patient should lie on their back with the wound exposed, to drain any collected material.  If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it with the stitching previously described.
If it is large, or with powder if small, if it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, examine the wound and immediately remove those fragments if they are loose. If not loose, leave them until.
The wound will fester, then they will be easier to remove.  As for other wounds occurring in other parts of the body, their treatment is as previously described.
Eighty-fifth Chapter.
Concerning wounds of the abdomen, the expulsion of the intestines, and their suturing.
A wound that appears on the abdomen may be large, small, or medium.  A large wound may expose one or several intestines, making their reintroduction and suturing more difficult and challenging.
A small wound, too, may present difficulty in another way, making its closure more challenging. If the intestines are not promptly returned to their position, they swell and thicken, making their return difficult.  For this reason, the most favorable wound is the medium-sized one, as it does not present the same difficulty in returning the intestines as do the other two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen has four aspects. Two sutures are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and other bodily wounds. The other two sutures are specific, applying to all wounds, but particularly suited to suturing the abdomen.
I will describe them to you one by one, with explanation. If the wound is small and something of the intestines has come out, and its return is difficult, this is due to one of two reasons: either the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestines have been puffed up due to the cold of the air.
If this is the case, it is necessary to warm it by dipping a sponge or wet cloth in lukewarm water, alone or possibly boiled with saffron, frankincense, and myrrh and applying it to the intestines to dissolve the puffiness.
A drink with a constricting quality may also achieve this, being better than water alone for dissolving the puffiness. If you see that the puffiness has subsided, moisten the intestines with water in which hyssop or cumin has been boiled, as this will facilitate their entry with greater ease.
If, after this treatment, its return remains impossible, make a small incision in the wound using the instrument for piercing nostrils. The first treatment, if successful, is better than incision.  One only resorts to incision as a last resort, when all other attempts have failed.  This is an illustration of the instrument:.
Its one bent side is limited, and the other side is unlimited.  The thin end is not as slender as a scalpel, but is slightly blunt. It is an instrument resembling a small awl, as you see. If the wound widens and the intestine enters, it should be returned to its natural position.
Its specific location, if achievable, is best. However, if the wound is wide and in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs elevated higher than their head. If it is in the upper abdomen, the head and chest should be elevated higher than the lower body.
Similarly, if the wound is in one side of the abdomen, always keep the side with the incision higher than the other.  This should be applied to major and minor surgical procedures.
For minor procedures, place it as best you can. Then, have a helpful assistant hold the entire wound with both hands, bringing the edges together, and then gradually expose portions of it to the surgeon performing the stitching.
This is the general method of single-stitch closure. Take a needle or several needles, appropriate to the size of the wound. Leave a space equal to the thickness of the little finger at the edge of the wound. Then, insert one needle, without thread, into the edges of the skin. With the edges of the peritoneum beneath the skin, from the inside, so that it penetrates from that side.  The edges of the skin and peritoneum are brought together, forming four layers.  Then, a doubled thread is tightly wound around the needle on both sides until the edges of the wound are securely joined.
Then leave a space equal to the thickness of a finger and insert another needle. Then, thread it as you did with the first needle. Continue in this manner with as many needles as needed to completely close the wound. The needles should be of medium thickness.
Because needles that are too thin will quickly cut through the flesh, and thick needles are difficult to insert into the skin, therefore the needles should be of medium thickness.  And the needle should not be inserted into the skin edge too deeply.
So that the flesh does not tear quickly and the wound does not open before healing.  Also, do not space the stitches too widely so that the wound cannot heal. Then, cut the ends of the needles so they do not injure the patient during sleep.  Make protective coverings of linen material on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
And leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of stitching with needles is best for smaller surgeries, as it may only require one or two needles, or so.
As for the method of stitching for a general wound, gather all four edges—that is, the two skin edges and the two peritoneum edges—in one stitching with a needle containing a thread of moderate thickness.  Then, after the needle has traversed these four edges, return the needle from the same side it entered.
So that the thread is interwoven at the top of the wound, making the stitch like the way sacks are sewn shut.  Leave a space between each stitch the size of a small fingertip. This stitch is known by everyone.
With this stitch, a surgical procedure was performed on a man who had a knife wound in his abdomen. The surgical incision was more than a span long, and approximately two spans of the small intestine had been exposed. The wound was located in the middle of the abdomen.
After the intestines had been exteriorized for twenty-four hours, the wound was closed.  It healed in about fifteen days, and he was treated until he recovered.  He subsequently lived for many years and remained fully functional.  Doctors had previously declared him incurable.
Surprisingly, I did not use any ointment, as I was in a location with no medications available. Instead, I applied used cotton to the wound twice daily and kept it moist with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the special types of stitches, I mention it according to Galen's text: Begin the stitching at the skin, inserting the needle from outside to inside.  Once the needle penetrates the skin and the muscle extending straight along the entire length of the abdomen...
Leave the peritoneum edge on the side where you inserted the needle.  Then, insert the needle into the other edge of the peritoneum from inside to outside.  If you insert it, re-insert it again into this same edge of the peritoneum from outside to inside.
Leave the peritoneum edge on this side and insert the needle into the other edge from inside to outside.  Simultaneously, as you insert it into the peritoneum, reach the peritoneum edge on its side, until the entire thing is penetrated.
Then, begin again from this same side, tracing along the peritoneum edge on the opposite side. Bring the needle out of the skin near it, then return it to that skin and trace the peritoneum edge on the opposite side with this edge of the peritoneum.
Bring it out of the skin on its side. Do this again and again, until the entire surgical wound is sewn in that manner.  The overall description of this stitching is like a furrier's stitching of furs.
By sewing the peritoneum once from one side with the skin edge and leaving it once from the other side until it is empty.  The second type of specialized stitching, which Galen also mentioned, is this in his words.
It is as if those practitioners sew it by bringing each part to its matching counterpart, naturally; thus they join the peritoneum edge to its other edge, and the peritoneum edge to its other edge, in the way I describe to you.
The needle should be inserted into the edge of the peritoneum nearest to you, from the outside, penetrating inward, and leaving the edges of the peritoneum. Then the needle is returned, penetrating from outside to inside at both edges of the peritoneum, and then returned again.
And penetrate it from inside to outside at the edge of the peritoneum on the other side. This type of stitching is better than the common, easy stitching previously mentioned. This is Galen's statement. Some have said that the abdominal wound may be stitched using two needles. This is done by inserting a single thread into both, beginning by inserting the needle from your side, penetrating to the other side, and inserting the other needle from the opposite side, penetrating from your side, according to the method of the leatherworkers' stitching.
Know that a perforation in the middle of the abdomen is more difficult to stitch than in other abdominal locations.  As for treating the wound, its treatment is like that of other wounds.  If you find the wound fresh with its blood, before the air changes it, and return the intestines to their place, stitch them, and make them firm, then apply the medicated dressings.
If the wound has been affected by the air, apply some of the available ointments until it suppurates and the stitches are shed, and the peritoneum and the peritoneum are joined together.  Then treat it like you would treat other wounds until it is healed.
If you do not have medications, apply powdered cotton from the start of the suppuration.  Change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals.  You generally do not need any other treatment for uncomplicated wounds, if you fear the wound might cause pain to the main organs.
Therefore, you should dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpits. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines, which often occurs, irrigate him with a lukewarm, black astringent drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestines and has become a penetrating wound into its cavity.  Know that the thicker portions of the intestines heal more easily, and the thinner portions heal more difficultly.
As for the jejunum, it does not accept healing from any wound that occurs within it at all, due to the abundance and thickness of its blood vessels, its thinness, and its proximity to the nerves.  However, if what has emerged from the wound is the mesentery and you have grasped it fresh, return it according to how you would return the intestines themselves.
If it has been a while and it has turned green or black, you should tie it with thread above the blackened area to prevent bleeding, as there are blood vessels and arteries in the mesentery. Then cut what is below that ligament.
You should leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, outside it, so it is easier for you to pull it and remove it when the mesentery falls off and the wound becomes suppurative.  Description of a wound that appears in the intestine.  Now, if a perforation appears in the intestine and it is small...
Sometimes, in some people, it can be repaired.  I have seen a person who had a wound in their abdomen from a spear thrust, the wound being on the right side of the stomach. The wound festered, and feces and air were coming out.  I treated it, though I did not expect it to heal. I continued to treat it until it healed and the area closed.  Seeing the area had closed, I feared that a mishap might occur in his abdomen.  However, absolutely no such mishap occurred.  He remained in the best possible condition. Indeed, he ate, drank, engaged in sexual intercourse, went to the bath, and exercised in his service.  Some experienced individuals have stated that if a wound occurs in the intestines and is small, it should be treated in this manner.
Large ants are to be taken and the edges of the wound are brought together.  Then, one ant, with its mouth open, is placed on the wound edges.  When the ant grasps and closes its mouth, its head is cut off; this will adhere and not separate.  Another ant is then placed near the first.
Continue doing this with several ants, according to the size of the wound.  Then close and stitch the wound.  Those heads remain adhered to the intestine until the intestine heals and is well, and no harm occurs to the patient. The intestine can also be sutured with fine thread.
To extract the animal's adhesive thread from the thread after it enters the needle, take the end of the thread from the animal adhesive, unravel it, then tie a thin, spun linen thread to its end, and finally thread that into the needle. The animal's adhesive thread is used to suture the intestines, and then returned to the abdomen. This method of suturing with ants and the animal adhesive is only a matter of hope and expectation.
However, if the rupture is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, there is no remedy and no cure at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
On the treatment of the common cold and nasal discharge.
Know that any wound or tumor that becomes chronic, develops into an ulcer, and does not heal, constantly discharging pus without cessation, is generally called a fistula (nasoor) in any part of the body, and we call it a cold (zakaam), or fistula.
It is, in fact, a hard, dense, white mass with no pain, possessing a cavity like the hollow of a bird's feather.
For this reason, some called it a feather.  Sometimes it is moist, constantly discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture may cease.  This moisture may be abundant, scanty, thick, or thin.
I have previously stated in the classification of surgical conditions that any ulcer that does not heal and does not form new flesh, is due to one of nine causes. One of these is either a deficiency or poor quality of blood in the body, or there may be dense flesh within or along its edges preventing the growth of good flesh. Or, it may be due to excessive harm and dirt; or, the ulcer itself is putrid, and the material that discharges from it is poor in quality; or, the treatment is inappropriate; or, there is a widespread disease in the region; or, it is specific to that particular location.
Or, it may be due to excessive harm and dirt; or, the ulcer itself is putrid, and the material that discharges from it is poor in quality; or, the treatment is inappropriate; or, there is a widespread disease in the region; or, it is specific to that particular location. As was observed in the city of Saragossa, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is slow, naturally, or it may be due to the presence of one or several bones.  All of these causes, their signs, and their treatments with remedies, I have outlined in the classification.
Here, I need to inform you of the treatment of abscesses and colds that occur through manual practice.  Know that abscesses can appear in all parts of the body, some leading to large veins, arteries, or nerves.
Or to the peritoneum, or the intestines, or the bladder, or a rib, or a vertebra of the spine, or a joint of the many-jointed limbs such as the hand and foot, and some lead close to a major organ, and the like.
Those abscesses of this type are among the difficult-to-heal illnesses, resistant to treatment, and their management involves great effort and ignorance. If the abscess does not reach the locations mentioned and one hopes for its treatment.
If you want to ascertain this, take a probe of copper or iron. If the abscess is straight, probe it with that. If the abscess has a curve, probe it with a thin lead probe, for lead is pliable.
If it enters and curves towards the curve, if the abscess has many openings that you cannot discern with the probe, inject one opening with moisture, for the moisture injected will flow towards the other openings.
Then, investigate thoroughly in any way possible to determine if there is a bone, a nerve, or if the abscess is deep or shallow, or if it is a single abscess with multiple openings.  Understand all of this to the best of your ability through examining the patient's pain when your hand touches the area.
And similar to this are the signs and causes that produce the tumor. If you ascertain all of this accurately, then proceed to treatment with confidence, by observing if...
If the abscess is superficial and close to the surface, or in a healthy area far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the places I have described, then open the abscess as previously described and remove the hardened material, corrupted flesh, and excess flesh from within.
And treat any flesh or warts that grow in its opening until it heals.  If the abscess is deep and located in a straight line, you should cut it open as deeply as possible and then clean it of all corrupted flesh.
Then use a twisted applicator soaked in potent medicine and insert it into the depths of the abscess that you can reach with the instrument; do this several times until the potent medicine consumes all remaining corruption within the abscess cavity.
Then apply ointments that promote healthy tissue growth until it heals. If it does not heal with this, cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the abscess is bone, and you are certain of this, probe and open it as I have instructed you, unless something prevents you from doing so.
If it is a vein, nerve, or major organ, as we have said, if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness, then scrape it until the corruption disappears, then treat it with what will heal it until it heals, and if it does not heal, and the pus persists as it was before.
Know that you have not reached complete removal of all corruption.  Therefore, expose everything affected again, thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with all your effort, and then apply the remedy.  If it heals, then well and good.  If not, know that the corruption is deep and hidden.  Expose all affected areas once more.
Thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with all your effort. If the corrupted bone is small and you can pull it out, pull it out with the finest forceps suitable for that purpose.
If there are many bones, thoroughly remove them all; do not leave anything. If any ligaments or tissues obstruct, and their severance poses no danger, cut those ligaments and tissues, and extract the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones at this time, apply something to fester the wound, and leave it for some days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones fester.  Protect the wound; do not allow the incision you made during your work to close.
Instead, if you fear that, place on it cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian balm, or one of the green balms.  When those ligaments fester and the bones are loose, their removal and extraction will be easy.
If it is a single large bone, like a leg bone or thigh bone, and only its surface has decayed, scrape it thoroughly until the blackness and decay are gone, then close the wound.  If a large portion has decayed and the decay has reached the bone marrow, it must be spread out.
Cut it all the way to where the decay ends, then treat it until it heals.  I will tell you about a cold that affected a young man's leg to serve as an example and aid in your treatment.  This man was young.
Around thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to something moving from within his body until the material reached the leg and swelled into a large tumor.  There was no external cause.  Then time passed with the errors of the physicians until the swelling opened.
A large quantity of material flowed from it, and his treatment was poor, until the leg festered and became full of many openings, all emitting pus and bodily moisture.  A group of physicians treated him for approximately two years, but none of them were skillful in the art of surgery.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg, and material flowed from those openings in a great torrent. The man's body had wasted away, and his complexion had turned yellow. I inserted the probe into one of those openings, and it reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
I found that some of them connected to others from all sides of the leg.  I then incised one of those openings until I exposed some of the bone. I found it decayed, eaten away, blackened, putrid, and perforated.  The hole reached the marrow. I reported what I had discovered.
I removed the corrupted bone, and I believe that there was no other corruption in the bone than what I had cut and exposed. I believe I had extirpated it.  Then I applied healing remedies to the wound for a long time, but it did not heal.  Then I again exposed the bone, further than the first time.
I found the corruption connected to the bone. I exposed whatever corruption I could see as well.  I tried to close it, but it did not heal or unite. I exposed it again. I continued to cut the bone piece by piece, and tried to close it, but it did not heal, until I cut off about a span of bone.
I removed it with its marrow, and then closed it with remedies. It healed quickly and recovered.  This repetition of work and incision was necessary due to the patient's weakness and susceptibility, and my fear that he would die.  Because he would repeatedly faint due to excessive vomiting, he recovered completely.
And solid flesh grew in place of the bone. His condition improved in his body, his strength returned, and he behaved normally.  He was not harmed by any difficulty while walking; if bone in any part of the body were broken.
Therefore, it is necessary to expose it in this manner: take a bandage and tie it to the end of the protruding bone.  Have someone pull it upwards. Then make another bandage of thicker wool than the first.
Then tie it to the flesh beneath the bone, pulling the end to draw the flesh downward.  As you expose the flesh in the area you want to expose, so the saw does not injure it. Place a plank or board under the bone from below, securely.
Because if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the diseased bone.  The incision should be slightly above the affected area, so there is no decay in the bone cavity that does not appear on the surface, forcing you to make another incision.
If the bone is diseased but not protruding, and instead is connected but decayed within or in part, expose the flesh from all sides.  Then place the plank below, and then make the incision from the first side, where the decay is.
When you are finished with the incision, make another from the other side.  Let the incision be a little distance from the decay, as we have said.  If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and make an incision into the bone where it connects on the other side.
If the decay is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but removal. If the decay is in the finger or toe, treatment is very difficult. However, you should scrape away the decay however it appears to you, and remove and cleanse it by any means possible.
By any means that is possible for you, provided no nerve or artery is encountered.  Know that the saws and knives used to cut these bones vary according to the position, structure, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and porosity of the bones. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare a tool suitable for each type of work.

Chapter eighty-seven.
In cutting off limbs and spreading bones.
Limbs may become rotten either from an external cause or an internal one. If you treat this decay with medicinal remedies and the treatment is not successful, and you see the decay spreading within the member, nothing stops it.
Then it is necessary to cut off that member to where the decay has reached so that the patient may be saved from death or from a great affliction which is greater than losing the member. A sign that this has afflicted someone is that the member becomes black, so you think that fire has burned it.
Or it may rot after becoming black, until that rot spreads to the parts adjacent to that member and affects the entire body.  Therefore, act quickly to cut it off.  Likewise, if the cause of the decay is the sting of some venomous creatures, such as the sea scorpion, snake, or centipede, and the like.
If the decay or the bite is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger, and do not delay the spread of the decay to the hand. Likewise, if it has reached the hand, cut it off from the base of the forearm, and do not delay the spread of the decay until it reaches the elbow of the arm.
Likewise, if it has reached the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow joint itself. If the decay has spread and you see it reaching towards the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for that will result in the patient's death.  Use other treatments as best you can.
Likewise, do the same with the leg. If decay has reached the toe, cut off the toe at one of the phalanges. If it has reached the foot, cut off the entire leg. If it ascends to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If the decay has reached above the knee, there is no remedy except to abandon it and allow the patient to succumb to death.  The method for cutting or dissecting a limb is to tie a bandage below the location you want to cut and another bandage above the location.
One attendant stretches the lower bandage downward, and another attendant stretches the upper bandage upward. You then expose the flesh between the bandages with a broad scalpel until all the flesh is uncovered.  Then cut or dissect.  And you should place linen cloths on all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct place, causing the patient excessive pain and a hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, quickly cauterize the area or apply some sharp hemostatic agents, then return to your treatment until you are finished.
Then bandage the wounded limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals.  I will tell you about a similar case that occurred to a man in his leg:  a black discoloration with a burning sensation like fire.
This decay first appeared in his finger, and then spread to the entire limb. The man, upon seeing the decay spreading through the limb with the intense pain and burning, had it amputated at the joint, and he recovered.
After a long time, this same decay appeared again in his index finger.  He came to me, and I applied the remedies I had on hand to the affected area after cleansing his body.  The decay did not stop and continued to spread to the other finger.
Until the decay spread through the hand, and he urged me to amputate his hand.  I refused, hoping to stop the decay through treatment, and also fearing that amputation would cause his death, as he was near death.  When he lost hope in me, he left for his home.
I later heard that he eventually amputated his entire hand and recovered. I have recounted this story to be a guide and assistance in cases like this illness and as a model for their treatment.
Eighty-eighth Chapter.
On the treatment of abscesses and how to inject medications into them.
If a fleshy tumor forms in any part of the body and persists for a long time, accumulating matter, then bursts or opens, releasing all the accumulated matter, and the area remains empty, like a vessel.
The skin over it is like a tattered cloth, thin and not severely damaged, not affecting bone, nerve, or ligament.  For this reason, it deserves to be called an abscess, not a fistula, unless the duration is so long that the damage affects one of these structures.
In that case, it is called a fistula or ulcer.  The treatment is to cut away all that skin, as it circles around, especially if it has become thin and like a tattered cloth, and you are certain that it is not adhering to the area due to its damage.
If you suspect the skin is still adhering, and the damage has not reached that extent, and there is a layer of flesh in the skin, treat it with injection.  Observe the abscess; if it is large, and the pus emanating from it has a foul odor, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
Which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris, in equal parts, mixed in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens and becomes as thick as honey, then take what you need of it, dilute it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess.  Tie off the opening and leave the medicine in it for two hours.
Then remove it by pressing; do this for several days until the abscess is cleaned and the bad odor is gone.  You may inject with ash water if you do not have this medicine; this means taking ash from the wood of the vine or ash from oak wood and pouring water over it.
Then strain it and inject it until you are sure that the abscess has been cleansed. If the patient is not responding to Egyptian medicine or ash water, inject with water and honey mixed with a little powdered verdigris.
Or inject with honey and a drink mixed together, because honey cleanses and washes, and a drink adheres to the abscess, especially if the drink has a property of being binding and drying.  If you do this several times and are sure the abscess has been healed, then inject what will encourage flesh to grow there.
For example, take some palm ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and a binding drink, or inject with other medicines and ointments, the properties of which we have explained in the article on ointments. If the opening of the abscess is narrow and will not accommodate the syringe.
If it is narrow, widen it slightly with an iron tool, or place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or Sirac ointment in it until it widens. Also, if the opening is wide, bring its edges together by stitching and leave open only enough room for the syringe to enter without more.
Also, if the opening from which pus is dripping is elevated, cut it at the lowest point to allow the pus to drain downward, because if pus is collected deep inside the abscess, it will prevent flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut the abscess opening downwards as desired, then apply pressure to the affected area so that the pus flows out easily as is convenient, and do not allow any pus to remain inside at all.
As for the adhesive medicines that are applied to the abscess from the outside, and how to apply them, take one of the adhesive ointments that is suitable for your purpose and spread it over a linen cloth, making the cloth large enough to cover the entire abscess. Then, with scissors, cut a hole slightly larger than the opening of the abscess.  Spread some soft ointment on another cloth, the size of the hole, and apply it to the opening, keeping it a distance from the abscess opening so that the pus can drain out easily.  Do not remove the larger cloth until later.
For several days, the one on the abscess opening should be removed at all times so that the accumulated pus can drain out, and to know whether the pus that is draining is copious, scant, ripe, or unripe.
Moreover, examine the abscess site itself: does the patient feel pain there, or not; is there swelling, or not?  If your treatment continues in this way and you see the material that drains is scant at the abscess opening,
Know that the abscess has adhered, or is about to adhere.  Then treat it tomorrow or on the third day.  When you have treated the wound, change the cloths and renew the ointment.  If, after a long time, thin pus comes out of the abscess, do not despair of its adhesion.
For his healing is near, because some people have slow healing of flesh in their wounds, and others have the opposite; if, after many days, unripe pus comes out of the abscess, know that it has not adhered.  If the abscess adhesion is slow,
Then increase the drying medications, and let the medications be moist in consistency and dry in strength, like the palm ointment, if more colocynth has been added.  Among the most effective remedies is to use palm ointment.
It is treated with rose oil, and the moderate, thick herbal extract is sprinkled on it, and then you knead it together and use it.  A similar procedure can be performed if the palm ointment is unavailable; honey can be heated until it becomes thick and used; or you can take myrrh, aloes, and kender, grind them, and sprinkle them on the honey while it is heated. Then apply it to a cloth and bind it to the abscess, or apply the heated honey until it thickens, sprinkle the medicines on it, place cloths over it, and bind it.
Similar remedies can include long-stalked zeraun, the rhizome of the asmanjūn lily, kresnā flour, and rue, used singly or together, ground, sifted, and sprinkled on the honey described.  If the abscess persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
If it has affected the bones, or other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it with the treatment for fistulas, as previously described.
Eighty-ninth chapter.
On treating the inflamed and injured nail, amputating excess finger, and separating fused fingers.
Dāḥis is excessive flesh that grows under the fingernail of the hand or foot, and possibly on other fingers. If left untreated, it will grow, swelling into a painful boil.
Or it may become corrupted and fester, eating away the base of the nail, perhaps even destroying the entire nail.  The corruption may reach the bone, giving off a foul odor, causing the end of the finger to widen and turn dark;  if treatment with the methods described in the classification is unsuccessful,
then one must cut away all the excess growth from the nail with a knife, and afterwards cauterize the wound. Cauterization is very beneficial in this case.  But if the bone and the nail are healthy,
and if the corner of the nail had pressed the flesh inwards, causing pain and irritation, then a thin pledget should be placed beneath the corner of the nail that is irritating the flesh, lifting it upward, and the flesh should be carefully cut away.
Apply caustic, burning medicines to the remaining flesh until it is all gone, then treat with ointments until it heals.  If the corruption has affected the bone, it must be cut out and removed, for the condition will never heal as long as there is corrupted bone present.
If you see decay and corruption spreading through the finger, amputate it at one of the phalanges, as previously mentioned, and then treat it until it heals.  If the nail has been struck or wounded and there is intense pain, one must first lance the affected area.
Then, using a sharp scalpel, split the nail with an oblique cut from top to bottom, taking care not to reach the flesh underneath the nail.  If you do, you will cause intense pain in the affected area and it will be a cause of excessive flesh growth at the site. Then treat the area with something to relieve the pain.
As for the extra finger that sometimes grows on people's hands, it may be entirely flesh, or it may contain bone, or even a nail, and some of it may grow at the base of a finger joint.
Or it may grow on some of the finger phalanges.  Those that grow on the phalanges of the finger do not move; those that grow at the finger joint might move.  Any that is purely flesh can be easily cut by severing it at its base with a wide scalpel.
However, if it grows at the base of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting it.  If it grows on the finger at one of the phalanges, one must first cut the flesh in a circular manner around the bone, then use one of those appropriate saws to expose the bone.
Then treat it until it heals.  As for the fusion that sometimes occurs between fingers, this frequently happens and can be a congenital condition or result from a wound, burn, or similar injury.  In such cases, the fusion must be separated.
So that the fingers return to their natural state.  Then place between them a linen or cloth soaked in rose oil so it does not quickly fuse, and separate them, or place a thin lead plate between them until the area heals properly.
Likewise, if fusion occurs between some fingers and the palm, separate the fusion in a way that best restores the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
In the cutting of the veins.
Veins are twisted, thick blood vessels filled with black, dark-colored phlegm, found in most parts of the body, and most commonly in the legs, particularly in those who travel frequently, are porters, and are laborers.  First, one should use frequent and vigorous rubbing of the body to remove the black phlegm.
Then, lance the affected area with a lancet.  Treatment with iron can be done in two ways: one is to cut and remove the black blood; the other is to sever the vein and remove it entirely.  For incision, first warm the leg with warm water.
Until the thick, murky blood dissolves.  Then, wrap the patient's leg from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage.  Then, make a wide incision in the vein in one place, or two places, or three.
Then, extract the black blood with your hand, from the bottom of the leg upwards and from the top downwards, until you remove an amount of blood that you consider sufficient, or as much as the patient's strength allows.  Then, tie it off and instruct him to avoid foods that produce black phlegm, and repeat the vomiting and the bloodletting.
When the veins become filled and this harms the patient,  as for severing, it should be done in this manner: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair, then put him in a bath and warm the leg with hot water until it turns red and sweats, or engage him in vigorous exercise.
If a bath is not available to warm the limb, then make a lengthwise incision in the skin over the vein, either near the knee or at the heel.  Then, open the skin with hooks and dissect the vein from all sides until it is visible to the touch.
When it appears, you will see it red and smooth.  Once it is freed from the skin, it will also appear like a tendon. Then, insert a probe beneath it until it is elevated and extends beyond the skin.  Hang it with a smooth, blunt hook. Then, make another incision close to that one, about the width of three fingers.
Then, dissect the skin off the vein until it is exposed.  Then, elevate it with the probe as you did before and hang it with another hook, as you did initially. Then make another incision, or several incisions, if necessary.
Then sever it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel.  Then pull and dissect it until it comes out of the second incision.  Then pull it to the incision above it and do this until you pull it from the third incision above all the others, until it is all out, and then cut it.
If pulling and dissecting do not work, insert a needle with a strong doubled thread, tie it, and pull. Insert the probe underneath and twist your hand in all directions until it comes out, and make sure it doesn't break, for if it breaks, it will be very difficult to dissect it.
This will cause harm to the patient.  If you have dissected it completely, apply wool soaked in a drink and rose oil or oil to the surgical site and treat it until it heals. If the vein is knotted, twisted, and has curves in various directions...
And if it is not straight, as we said, you should make an incision on every curve and twist in it.  Then hang it with hooks until you have dissected it entirely.  Be careful when making the incisions not to cut or injure the vein, for this will make it difficult for you to dissect it, and so conserve your efforts.
Image of the incision used for dissecting the vein.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not have a hook like other hooks, nor should its tip be sharp, so as not to injure the vein.  It should be thick and smooth in its bend, because if it is thin it will cut the vein with its thinness.  Rather, it should have the advantage of thickness, as we said.
Chapter Ninety-one.
In the vein of the civilian.
This vein arises in the legs in hot countries such as the Hijaz and the Arab lands, and in hot, sparsely fertile lands.  It may also arise in other parts of the body besides the legs, arising from an ulceration occurring beneath the skin.
As it occurs within the bodies of snakes and worms, and the ulceration of the gourd and the worm that breeds between the skin and the flesh. A sign of the beginning of this vein's appearance is a severe burning sensation in the leg, followed by the swelling of the affected area, and then the vein begins to emerge from the site of the swelling.
As if the root of a plant or an animal.  When its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one to two dirhams, tied, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it grows too large, cut off a portion and wrap the remainder. Do not cut it from the root before it has all emerged, for if you cut it, it will shrink and enter the flesh, causing a tumor, ulceration, and a foul sore in the area.
Therefore, it should be gently drawn out little by little until the entire vein is outside the body and nothing remains inside.  In some individuals, this vein may be as long as five or ten cubits.
I have been told that it emerged from a man's body as much as twenty cubits. If it breaks during treatment, insert a reed into the hole, and press it firmly against the body for a long time until all the material inside is released. Then, try to heal the area with remedies for several days.
Then treat it with the remedies for tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint or in the leg itself, causing many openings and branches emerging from each opening. Treat it as described in the classification and as previously mentioned.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
Concerning the ulcer on the worm that develops under the jaw, known as the cattle disease.
This disease is called "cattle disease" in some of our countries, because it often affects cattle. It is a single small worm that develops between the skin and flesh, crawling throughout the body, both up and down.
Its movement from one part of the body to another can be felt, until it penetrates the skin, and emerges, forming a swelling from the putrefaction of certain humors, as occurs in worms, snakes, and gourd seeds in the abdomen.
Its danger lies in the fact that if it crawls through the body and ascends to the head and reaches the eye, it might break through it and exit, causing the eye to be lost.  This often occurs.  Therefore, if you wish to treat it and remove it, it can only be done when it is crawling and is perceptible to the touch.
Therefore, you should tie firmly with a bandage above and below it, then cut open over it and remove it. If it has burrowed into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply a cauterization with fire to the area until it is burned.  Its greatest potential harm is to the eye, as we have said.
If you see it has reached the head, near the eye, tie firmly under it on the forehead, then cut open over it and remove it.  The patient should maintain a cleansing of their body with laxatives for putrid humors, and be cautious of foods that create putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-three.
On the incision of the disease known as the 'naفر'.
This disease, called the "naفر" in our country, is a pain that appears in some organs and then moves from one organ to another.  I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a woman in distress in some rural area.  She bared her arm, and I saw a slight swelling in the arm's vein.  After an hour, I saw that swelling creeping up her arm along with the ulna, as a worm might climb, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as mercury could.
If it moved from one place to another, the pain would leave that spot and settle in the shoulder. Then, after an hour, it would spread throughout the body, even to the other arm.  She told me that it circulated her whole body, as I observed.
I was amazed by its rapid movement from one organ to another.  Before, I had only heard of this disease moving from one organ to another; but I had never seen it with my own eyes like I did in this woman.
I attributed this only to the fact that the woman was from the desert, lean of body, and with exposed veins.  From this, the moving pain became noticeable to the senses.  It should not appear in the same way in those of a refined lifestyle with moist, concealed veins and bodies.
If you wish to treat it, and the sufferer feels the pain, if it manifests itself visually as we described, then apply pressure above and below it quickly, and make incisions there, to allow the trapped wind/pain to come out.
If you do not see it, treat it with shaking the body and remedies that disperse wind, such as rotten bean and scabies bean, and similar medicines.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On extracting arrows.
Arrows vary according to their types and the locations where they strike the body.  As for their variation in type, some are large, some small; some are hollow glass, some solid glass; some have three angles, some four.
Some have tongues, some have splinters.  As for those that vary according to the organs they strike, there are two types: either the organs are hollow major organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. If an arrow strikes one of these organs, and signs of death, which I will describe to you later, appear, you should avoid extracting the arrow, for death will usually befall the owner. If these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow is not concealed deep within the organ, remove it and treat the wound.  And signs of the brain if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane covering the brain, include severe headache, fainting, and dizziness.
If these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow is not concealed deep within the organ, remove it and treat the wound.  And signs of the brain if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane covering the brain, include severe headache, fainting, and dizziness. Redness in the eyes and tongue, convulsions, confusion of mind, vomiting, and possibly bleeding from the nostrils or ears.  Speech may cease, the voice may be lost, and a white, porridge-like moisture may emerge from the wound, accompanied by a watery discharge resembling the juice of meat.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and extracting the arrow, if it has not already been removed.  And as for the signs of an arrow striking the heart, if it is near the left breast, and you feel it as if it has lodged in something solid, not hollow.
There may also be a pulsating motion from the arrow, and black blood may flow from the wound, followed by coldness in the limbs, cold sweat, and fainting.  Know then that death is inevitable.  And a sign of an arrow wounding the lung is a frothy discharge of blood from the wound.
The vessels near the neck swell, the patient's complexion changes, and he breathes heavily, seeking cool air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it will be near the small ribs, and the breathing will be heavy, with severe pain.
He sighs and all the muscles of the shoulders move. If the arrow strikes the liver, intense pain follows, and blood resembling the liver in color flows from the wound. If the arrow strikes the stomach, undigested food may emerge from the wound.
And his condition is evident.  If the arrow strikes the abdomen, and there is a rupture, with bowel or feces coming from the wound, there is no hope for treatment or removal of the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder and urine emerges from the wound.
If something protrudes from the wound and the patient's pain intensifies, know that he is doomed.  As for other organs, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they are more likely to survive.
Unless the arrow struck an artery or nerve, and was not poisoned, I tell you of some things I have observed regarding these arrows to help you in your treatment.  An arrow struck a man in the root of his nose, near his eye.
I extracted it from the other side, under the earlobe, and he recovered without harm to his eye.  I also extracted another arrow for a Jew that had lodged in the earlobe of his eye, under the lower eyelid.  The arrow was largely concealed, only its small, wooden tip remained visible.
It was a large arrow from a composite bow, with a smooth, square iron tip, and no ears.  The Jew recovered without any further eye problems.  I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.  This arrow was Arab.
It had two ears, and I split it between the uvula. It had lodged deep in his throat, so I carefully removed it.  The Christian recovered and was healed.  I extracted another arrow from a man's abdomen; we judged that he would die from it.
After thirty days or so, with no change in his condition, I split the arrow and carefully extracted it. He recovered without further incident. I saw a man with an arrow in his back; the wound had closed over it.
Seven years later, the arrow emerged from the base of his thigh.  I saw a woman with an arrow in her abdomen; the wound had healed, and the arrow remained.  Her condition did not change, and she experienced no difficulty in her normal activities.
I saw another man with an arrow in his face; the wound had closed, and he experienced little pain.  Many such cases exist.  I extracted an arrow from the nose of a sultan's commander; it had lodged slightly to the right.
The arrowhead was completely hidden.  I was called to treat him three days after the arrow's penetration.  I found the arrow wound very small.  I probed it with a thin instrument and felt nothing.  He was experiencing a prickling sensation and pain under his ear, on the right side.
I hoped that the prickling was from the arrowhead, so I applied a dressing with a drawing and ripening effect, hoping that the area would swell and the arrow's location would become apparent so that I could extract it.  No sign developed to indicate the arrow's presence in that spot.
I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened.  The wound healed over. The patient despaired of extracting the arrow for a while until he felt it one day inside his nose. He told me.  I then applied a strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it. I felt the fine tip of the arrow that was embedded in the wood.  Then I further enlarged the wound with the strong medicine until the arrowhead became visible.  This took approximately four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I had access to it with forceps, I pulled and moved it, but it did not come out.  I continued to manipulate it with various instruments until one day I grasped it firmly with specialized forceps, as their image is presented at the end of the chapter.
The physicians had judged that his nasal cartilage could not be repaired, but I repaired it.  The wound healed, and the patient recovered completely, experiencing no further discomfort.  I am telling you how to remove some arrows so you may use this as an example and guide for those that I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped through description, nor encompassed by a book.  Instead, the skilled craftsman draws comparisons from the few to the many, from what is present to what is absent, and infers a new action and new instrument in the face of unusual cases.
If I descend from this craft, I say that arrows are extracted from the organs they have entered in two ways: either by pulling from the point of entry, or from the opposite side, which is the same as removing from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding into soft tissue, in which case it should be pulled out; if it does not come out at the time of the injury, you should leave it for several days until the surrounding flesh decays, making its removal easier.  The same applies if it is embedded in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it for several days and try pulling and moving it daily; it will then come out. If, after several days, it still does not come out, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone from each side with a delicate drill to widen the space for the arrow, and then pull it out.
If the arrow is embedded in the skull, and has penetrated one of the brain cavities, and some of the symptoms I have described have appeared, then refrain from pulling out the arrow. Leave it for several days until its condition improves.
Then, proceed to pull out the arrow. If it is deeply embedded and difficult to remove, use the drills around the arrow as described previously, and then treat the wound until it heals.  If the arrow is hidden in a part of the body, If it is hidden from sight, probe for it with a sound, and if you locate it, pull it out with instruments suitable for the task.  If you cannot do so due to the narrow wound and the arrow's deep penetration, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way,
cut around it until the wound widens, and you can remove the arrow.  If it has two points, grasp them. Remove the flesh trapped between them from every direction with every possible means. If you are unable to remove the flesh, break the points and twist them until it is released.
When attempting to remove an arrow from any location, use your hand with forceps on all sides to extract it.  Be extremely gentle so the arrow does not break, making extraction difficult.  If you cannot remove it in your time,
leave it for a few days until the surrounding flesh has become putrid.  Then try again, as it will be easier then. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments described in the relevant section.  Take care to avoid cutting a vein, nerve, or tendon, and use your ingenuity.
Use every means possible to remove the arrow gently, carefully, and firmly, as described.  When pulling the arrow, the patient's position should be the same as when the arrow entered, which is most suitable.
If this is not possible, use any method you can.  As for an arrow emerging from the opposite side, either something will have protruded outward, or you will feel the arrowhead close to the skin's surface and see it protruding.
Cut the surrounding tissue to the extent that the forceps can fit inside. Then pull it out; it will be easier to remove. If it is lodged in bone, twist your hand in a circular motion so the arrow cuts into the bone, widening the space for itself, and then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days and try again until it comes out. If the arrow's shaft is still in place, push it out. If the shaft has fallen off and you want to use force, insert a hollow instrument into the arrowhead's shaft to guide it into the arrow's base and then push it out with the instrument.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with an instrument that fits into the hollow. This will make it easier to remove. If the arrow is poisoned, you should remove all the poison from the affected flesh, if possible, then treat it with suitable remedies.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or side, and is close enough to be felt with a probe, and you can make an incision, make the incision, being careful not to cut a nerve or blood vessel, and remove the arrow.
Then suture the wound if necessary, and treat it until it heals.  Image of the forceps used to remove arrows:
Its tips are like the beak of a bird, crafted to be like a drill. If it grips an arrow or anything, it won't release.  There are various sizes made, large, small, and medium, all depending on the size of the arrow and the width of the wound.

Image of the solid cannon.

Chapter Ninety-Five.
In the opening of veins.
The veins that customarily have been opened in the body are thirty.  Of these, sixteen are in the head; the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the *ḥussīsīn*; and the two arteries visible on the temples; and the two veins at the sockets of the eyes, known as the *nāẓirīn*.
