And the vein that stands up in the middle of the forehead, and the vein in the tip of the nose, and the two veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip, and these veins known as the *jayārak*, and the two veins beneath the tongue.
And as for the veins that are opened in the arm and hand, they are five veins. One of them is the *qayfāl*, which is on the outer side, and the common people call it the vein of the head.  And the *ʾaḵal*, which is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the *bāsaliq* and a branch from the *qayfāl*, and the common people call it the vein of the body.
And the *bāsaliq*, which is located on the inner side, and is also called the *ʾibṭī*, and the common people call it the vein of the abdomen and the cord of the arm, which is located on the elbow, which is where it attaches, which is the one that appears clearly above the thumb, and the *ʾasīlim*.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, with two branches.  In the leg and foot, there are three veins: one under the bend of the knee, on the outer side; the second, the *ṣāfn*, is located at the heel, on the inner side; and the vein of the women, located at the heel, on the outer side.
And in the other leg, there are three veins like them.  As for the two veins behind the ears, their opening is beneficial for chronic colds, migraines, shingles, and chronic, malignant head sores, and their method of opening will be described.
This is done by shaving the patient's head, rubbing its back, in the location of the veins, with a rough cloth thoroughly.  Then the patient's neck is bound with a turban, until the veins appear, located behind the ears, in the lower parts of the head.  The veins are then felt with one's finger; where the pulsation is felt under the finger, the area is marked with ink.
Then, take a scalpel, known as the *naṣl*, and insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then, lift the vein and skin upward and sever the vein with the skin, making a complete cut, the length of which should be the length of two fingers clenched together.
Or similar, and remove the amount of blood desired.  Then bind them with bandages and leave them until healed.  Their incision and cauterization were previously discussed in the beginning of the book.  As for the two arteries in the temples, their incision is beneficial for chronic migraines, severe headaches, persistent eye inflammation, and severe, persistent discharge into the eyes.
Their method of incision, as I will describe to you, is that the patient's neck is bound with a turban, until the veins become clearly visible, and their pulse is apparent under your finger. At that point, mark the location with ink.  Then, lift the skin over the vein from above with your index finger.
Insert the *naṣl* scalpel from below, lift the vein upward, and sever it as you did with the other veins.  Remove the blood to the desired extent. Then, release the patient's neckcloth and place your finger on the vein for an hour.
Then place a cotton pad and bandage upon it, and bind it firmly from above. Leave it until healed.  Their incision, cauterization, and lancing were previously mentioned in the beginning of the book.  As for lancing the forehead vein, it is beneficial after lancing the temporal vein for chronic facial ailments such as ringworm, sores, and persistent redness.
Its method of incision, as I describe to you, is that the patient's neck is bound with a turban until the vein appears.  Then, take the instrument called a *fāʾis*, and this is its image:.
Place the pointed spike at the head of the *fāʾis* on the same vein and strike from above with a comb or similar object. Allow the blood to flow as desired.  Then, release the patient's neckcloth and bind it until healed.
A wide scalpel may be used, but it should not be sharp-edged like other scalpels. Instead, it should be slightly wide-edged.  Incise it in a twisting motion, because the bone is close by.  A thin scalpel may break there.
As for the veins in the eye sockets, they are beneficial for eye ailments like scabies, redness, and blemishes, and facial diseases.  Their method of incision is that the patient should tighten their neck with a turban, and then you should incise them while standing over their head.  The incision should be slightly oblique and lengthwise with a small, somewhat wide scalpel.
If the area has little flesh, a thin-edged scalpel may break.  Then, take the amount of blood needed, place a bandage over them, and tie them up for one night.  Then, release the bindings.  As for incision of the nasal vein, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
Severe headaches and facial ailments like red bumps that appear on the nose, especially if chronic, are also treated by this method.  The procedure involves tightening the patient's neck, then holding their nose with your left hand, and using a long, thin scalpel.
Insert it into the center of the nostril itself, between the nasal passages, in a straight line.  The vein is not apparent to touch there, so blood will emerge immediately.  You should press the scalpel in slightly, draw off the required blood, and then tie it off for a night, as it will clot quickly.
As for the jugular veins, incision is beneficial for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and melancholy diseases that appear on the skin's surface, such as black vitiligo, scabies, bad ulcers, and cankers.  The method for their incision is that the patient should tighten their neck under them with a bandage, and the practitioner should stand over the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair, then the vein is incised lengthwise, with a slightly wide incision.  Then, the moderate amount of blood, or as much as needed, is drawn.  The same is done to the other vein, then the bandage is released.
The two veins are tightened moderately so that the patient does not suffocate, and left until the next day. The wound will heal.  As for the veins of the jaw, incision after incision of the jugular vein is beneficial for mouth ulcers, gum decay, bad ulcers, chapped lips, and bad ulcers.
Those that are in the nose and around it. The method of their incision is that you have the patient sit in front of you, tighten their neck with a turban, and then turn their lips outward. Look for the two veins, one on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, and identify them by their blackness.
Also, around them are thin, black veins, which are to be cut off cleanly. If it is unclear to you and you do not know which to take, then proceed to cut the largest and clearest one.  Do the same for the veins on the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, incision after incision of the jugular vein is beneficial for the obstructions in the throat caused by sore throat and mouth diseases. The method of their incision is to have the patient sit before you, facing the sun, and raise their tongue.  Look under the tongue on one side for a vein and on the other side for a vein. Their color is toward black.
Incise them and be cautious; do not cut them deeply, for beneath them are arteries.  Excessive bleeding from those arteries might occur.  The three veins that are incised at the elbow are those customarily incised in all people.  Their incision can be done in two ways.
Either with a wide, herbal lancet, or olive-shaped lancet, to the point of tenderness; or with a knife-shaped lancet,  a lancet that is sharpened (and this is its form). The form of the wide herbal lancet is:
It is wide, as you see, to be suitable for opening hollow, full, protruding, visible, thick veins that contain thick, dark blood.  And this is the form of the olive-shaped lancet:
This lancet is narrower and more pointed, suitable for opening thin veins containing thin, yellowish blood. This is the form of the sharpened lancet.
This sharpened lancet, suitable for incision, comes in various widths and thicknesses, depending on the size of the veins.  It can also be used to discern other conditions; it is well-known among practitioners.
As for the jugular vein, which is one of these three veins, its benefit from being lanced is that it draws blood from ailments in the throat and neck area, including those near the chest and abdomen. The lancet-operator must be cautious when lancing it and should be aware of the underlying artery. If the incision goes astray and the lancet pierces the artery, it will cut the artery.
This will cause blood to spill, and therefore the lancing should not be done with a pointed lancet but with an incision made by a sharpened lancet.  If the jugular vein is not clearly visible, it should be avoided, and the practitioner should turn to another vein or look for a branch of it.
Alternatively, lance the arm's vein, as it is clear, and use the sharpened lancet, as we have said. If you wish to lance the jugular vein directly, then before tightening the arm, carefully feel the location to locate the pulse and then mark it with ink.
Then the arm is tied, and the vein is lanced with a deflected incision using the sharpened lancet, as we have said.  Carefully ensure that the strike is away from the artery's location. When you see swelling at the marked spot after tightening the ligature, that swelling is the expansion of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood during the lancing springing forth like a child's urine, and it is thin, red blood, know that it is from the artery.  Immediately, place your finger on it for a long time, and then remove your finger. If the bleeding stops, it often does stop. Then tighten the arm.
Leave it alone, and warn the patient about neglecting it.  Let it remain on the neck and do not move it for days until it heals.  If the bleeding does not stop and you are not able to provide a remedy, then sever the artery if it is visible to you; for its ends will contract, and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell, split it, and use one half, firmly binding it to the affected vein using a bandage and splints until the next day.  If the bleeding stops, do so. Otherwise, treat it as previously described, using sharp cauterizing instruments for the bleeding.  Severing the blood vessels is not difficult in most cases.
Because the wound is small and the ligature is effective on the arm, know this.  As for the black vein, the benefit of lancing it is to draw blood from the top of the head and lower body, because it is composed of a branch from the basilic and a branch from the cephalic, as we have said.
The lancet operator should be careful when lancing.  Below the vein are nerves.  If the lancet is driven in too deeply and strikes a nerve, numbness will result, difficult to observe, and possibly not heal at all.  This nerve is often palpable.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin, not discernible, then you should lance it with a tearing motion and avoid the nerve to the best of your ability. If the vein is between two nerves, lance the vein lengthwise.  As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of lancing it is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye ailments.  If you wish to lance this vein in particular, it is better to use a pointed or a broad lancet, because it is the safest of all veins, as there is neither artery nor nerve beneath it.
However, when lancing, you should avoid striking the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek the soft spot.  If the vein is not struck on the first attempt, repeating the lancing procedure is not harmful. However, some individuals may experience swelling if not lanced on the first try.
But that swelling will do no harm.  Regarding the method of lancing, its symptoms, and what should be done to rectify them, first know that lancing is used solely to preserve health.
It may also be used for diseases.  To preserve health and maintain it, preventing illness, lancing should be done on one of the three veins in the elbow—the cephalic, the basilic, and the median cubital—in early spring, when signs of fullness appear.
Lancing should be done on a Sunday or Tuesday, after three hours of the day have passed.  As for lancing used for illnesses, there is no specific time limit; it should be done when needed, whether night or day.
At any hour, at any time.  Boys should not be lanced until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should elderly men over sixty.  If someone intends to perform lancing for any reason...
He should cleanse his bowels before lancing with a gentle enema if there is much phlegm retained, lest foul matter from the intestines be drawn into the veins during lancing, harming the vital organs.  A feverish person, a drunkard, or an intoxicated person should not be lanced until these effects have subsided.
Also, avoid lancing after dysentery, vomiting, childbirth, excessive intercourse, exertion, exercise, late nights, fasting, and any physical or mental activity that weakens the body.  Then consider thinning the humors if the blood is thick.
Using foods, drinks, and medications, if possible.  He should then take a bath, if nothing prevents him, or engage in some exercise, to thin the blood.  Lancing should be done in the middle of the day, as we mentioned.  He should try to clear his mind of all negative thoughts that day.
Such as anxieties, anger, and fear, and all bodily afflictions such as excessive fatigue and exertion, intercourse, and similar things.  Let the objects that people are accustomed to use be present in his gathering, such as perfumes, herbs, amusements, and the like, each person to the extent he can.
Then the lancet-wielding physician sits on a higher cushion than the patient.  Then he extends the arm and rubs it with his hand two or three times. Then he tightens the bandage with the ligature, twisting it two or three times, with the tightening being moderate.
Because if the tightening is excessive, it prevents the flow of blood, or if it's too loose, it also prevents the flow of blood.  Then, after tightening, the patient should rub both of his hands together.
Until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch.  Then the surgeon should wipe the lancet with a small amount of old oil, specifically.  Then he should place his left index finger on the same vein, slightly below the place he wants to lance, so the vein is not pierced and is avoided. For some veins are like tendons, which retract during the lancing, and some are filled with air. If the lancet is placed on them, they recede beneath the lancet and deceive the surgeon, and the lancet does not open the vein. If it does open it, it is only a narrow opening.
Therefore, the surgeon should be cautious and deliberate in all these matters.  Then he lowers the lancet; if it opens the vein on that first attempt, well and good.  Otherwise, he should try again slightly below or above that spot; quickly, if the area has not swollen.
If the patient swells or suffers, leave it for a day or two. Do not tighten the bandage, as it may cause a hot swelling. He should not enter a bath. Then, he may have the lancing repeated if he wishes. If the lancet has been inserted and the opening is small, and the blood flow is thin.
And fearing that the desired amount of blood will not be released, reposition the lancet in the same hole gently and steadily; enlarge the opening slightly. Do this quickly before the area swells, because in many people a small opening can cause swelling.
If you see that it has swollen, do not return to it at all. It will not help. Apply some clarified oil to the area. It facilitates blood flow and is better in this location than plain oil or other ointments.
Use clarified oil in all your venesections when blood flow is difficult.  This remedy, the *Fariq*, and the *Shakhznaya*, if applied to the area, can also thin and dissolve thick blood.
If a large swelling occurs at the venesection site, which often happens, especially for someone who has not been lanced before or whose vein opening was small, then quickly apply a sponge soaked in warm salt water to the swelling and compress it for an hour.  It will dissolve.
This should be done after the blood has completely flowed from the vein itself or from another vein.  If a few days later, there remains some discoloration (black or green), it does not harm. If desired, apply some resolved (dissolved) opium and myrrh.
Or some of the juice of *Fawdeng* and the like.  A swelling and protuberance often occur when lancing the Basilic vein.  Apply your hand to it.  If you find it pulsates when you press it, this is a bad protuberance, so be careful not to apply any of the mentioned remedies.
For it may bleed arterial blood.  However, bandage it with something astringent to harden the area, then treat it with other remedies until it heals.  One should draw blood from each person proportionally to their strength and the prevailing color of their blood.
If the blood is dark, let it flow until it turns red.  Likewise, if you see it as thick, let it flow until it becomes thin.  Likewise, if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness subsides.  It is appropriate for someone who is strong and full and needs to draw blood all at once.
If strong, widen the vein incision and make the lancet wide. If weak, do the opposite.  The blood should be drawn in several stages, and the incision should be narrow.  The best instrument for lancing a vein is one that is curved, slanted, or a slit, not a puncture.
This type of lancing is free from bleeding and cutting nerves.  It is better and safer than lancing across or lengthwise.  If someone faints during lancing, one should feed them something like bread soaked in pomegranate juice or spiced sherbet before the procedure.
If he is feverish and has blood drawn three or four times, if his temperament is cold, let him eat bread soaked in myrrh drink or honey drink scented with herbs or a fragrant, herbal drink beforehand.
If fainting occurs during lancing and it is due to excessive blood loss, then he should be given meat broth and a light, fragrant drink.  Incense should be used, the chest should be gently sprayed with it, and all other treatments mentioned in the section on fainting should be employed.
That is from vomiting.  And as for someone wishing to loosen their arm and further release blood, someone whose lancing is for excessive vomiting and whose strength is weak, should have the blood released little by little according to their strength over consecutive days.
And as for someone wanting to loosen their arm and release blood a second time, if their body is strong, let them do so after seven or nine hours from the first lancing.  And as for someone wanting to draw blood from their body to the opposite side to which it has leaned, let them be loosened on the second or third day.
And if the body has much heated and intensified blood, causing fever, then blood should be drawn in one dose, a large amount, and the opening widened until fainting occurs, after having checked all the conditions of bloodletting. And that you place your hand on his pulse while the blood flows, lest death occur instead of fainting, for this often happens if the bloodletter is ignorant and negligence occurs.  This should not be done if you intend to loosen the arm and release blood a second time.
If the vein opening closes and blood flow is difficult, do not forcefully prod or twist it, as this is very harmful. Instead, either leave it until it can be lanced again or use a scalpel blade to remove any clotted blood from the vein opening.
Or apply something salty dissolved in water, or a powerful antidote (like faruq or shakhzanaia), and gently prod it until blood flows. If the vein has swollen, leave it.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If the need arises to release more blood, then either lancet above that spot, or lancet the other arm, or another vein.
As for lancing the arm vein, it is used instead of the temporal artery and basilic vein if those are unavailable or hidden, because it is a compound of them both.  The method for lancing it is that the patient should put their hand in warm water until the forearm reddens and the vein becomes clearly visible. Then, a moderate bandage is applied slightly above it.
Then, lance the vein slightly off center, neither horizontally nor vertically, and make the opening wide.  Make your incision slightly above the wrist joint. If blood flow is difficult, re-immerse the arm in hot water and allow the blood to flow into the water.
Until you have the amount you need. If it is summertime, you may dispense with re-immersion in hot water.  Re-immersion in hot water is most often used in winter.  Lancing this vein is the safest of all veins. Because there is no major vein or nerve beneath it.  As for lancing the ulnar vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method is to tie the wrist with a bandage or your hand, after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no major vein or nerve beneath it.  As for lancing the ulnar vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method is to tie the wrist with a bandage or your hand, after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells. The vein will be very apparent to the touch. Then lance it slightly off-center.  Even if you cut through it completely, this will not harm him.  Take care; do not use your instrument excessively.  Underneath is the nerve of the fingers, and the area is exposed without muscle. Then return the arm to the hot water.
Leave it to drain. If you do not return it to hot water, the blood will clot in the opening of the vein and cease flowing. When you have extracted the desired amount of blood, apply a mixture of ointment and salt to the vein to prevent quick clotting.
The same should be done with every narrow branch.  As for the benefit of lancing the vein on the left hand, it is useful for spleen ailments.  Do the same in lancing it as you did in the second case.  As for lancing the saphenous vein, its benefit is for illnesses in the lower body, such as uterus ailments.
And menstrual retention, kidney ailments, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar illnesses. The method for lancing it is to immerse the leg in hot water and apply massage until the veins become visible. Then tie above the joint of the leg with a bandage. The vein is located near the heel, visible, like the thumb.
From it, many branches extend throughout the leg. Lance it in the widest branch, or at the heel where it joins, which is better and safer. If you lance it on the side of the leg, be cautious of the nerves beneath on the side of the leg.
Make your incision by angling, as if you were aiming for amputation.  Let the lancet be sharp.  If the blood flow is difficult, return the leg to hot water and allow the blood to drain out. If the lancing is unsuccessful the first time,
Then lance slightly higher; the area is safe and nothing to fear if you take care not to damage the nerve, as we said.  Do the same with the saphenous vein on the other leg.  As for the saphenous vein, its location is, as we said, at the heel, on the outer side.
Its use is for hip pain, if it arises from hot blood.  The method of lancing is to have the patient enter a bath and, quickly, to tie the leg from the hip to above the heel with a long, thin bandage, using four fingers. Only then will it appear.
If it appears, lance it, in any case, but preferably with an angled incision.  Alternatively, amputate or cut it.  The location is safe. In most people it is very hidden, so if you do not find it...
If it does not appear at all to the touch, lance some of its branches; these appear on the back of the foot, near the little and ring fingers.  Be careful not to damage the nerves and remove the amount of blood you desire.
Then loosen the bandage, place a compress on the lanced area, and bandage the spot.  It will quickly heal.
Chapter Ninety-six.
On cupping and how to use it.
Cups can be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass.  Cupping can be done in two ways: one with a mark and the removal of blood, and the other without a mark.
This cupping without a mark is done in two ways.
And the cups of the "akhda'ayn" (which are the sides of the neck on both sides), and cups of the chin, located beneath the lower jaw of the mouth, and cups of the shoulders, and cups of the coccyx on the tailbone, and cups of the elbows, which are the middles of the arms, and cups of the legs, and cups of the ankles.
Cupping draws blood from the thin veins dispersed in the flesh.  Therefore, it does not deplete strength like leeches.  Cupping, whether with or without fire, should not be used for any illness that is caused by fullness until the entire body is emptied.
If cupping is needed, whether for illness or custom, we use it at any time, at the beginning, end, or middle of the month.
For some people, when blood increases to the point that cupping is needed, they experience heaviness and headaches in their heads.  Others experience fullness, redness in their face, head, and neck.  Some experience itching in their face, side, and darkness and soreness in their eyes.  Others itch the area where the cups were placed.
Some laugh excessively, others taste blood in their mouths, their gums swell, and they spit up blood.  Some sleep a lot, and others see blood, redness, dead bodies, and wounds in their dreams.  Whenever we see any of this,
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order cupping after two or three hours have passed of the day.  The benefit of a single cup is to help with heaviness in the head and water dripping into the eyes.
However, it is necessary that this be done after the body has been emptied.  This cupping can be a replacement for venesection of the temples.  Caution should be taken that those with a cold head or a cold should not use it, as it will harm them greatly.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly or those with cold diseases in their heads, and those who become accustomed to it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, the cupper should lower the cup slightly downwards, for fear of causing forgetfulness.  As for cupping the shoulder, it is a replacement for venesection of the temples and the neck.
Therefore, it is helpful for asthma, shortness of breath, a split respiratory system, coughs, and fullness.  The cupping of the shoulder should be raised slightly, because if it is positioned lower, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it is beneficial for headaches, eye inflammation, migraines, choking, toothache, and is a replacement for venesection of the neck.  The cupper should be instructed not to deepen the insertion of the instrument, lest a vein be cut and bleeding occur.
As for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for sores in the mouth, gum disease, and similar mouth ailments.  It may replace venesection of the veins in the lips.  As for cupping the shoulders, it is beneficial for palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
As for cupping the hollows of the forearms, it is beneficial for what the venesection of the three veins—the neck, temples, and groin—benefits.  Because it draws blood from all the fine veins in the flesh, and draws those fine veins from other thicker veins, until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be instructed not to go too deep with the instrument, because the area is devoid of flesh and has nerves and arteries underneath.  As for a single cupping on the coccyx, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids and sores of the lower region.
The cupper should be instructed to use a large, brass cupping cup, as the area requires strong suction, and glass cupping vessels might break.  A great deal of pressure is required.  As for cupping the legs, it reduces fullness noticeably.
Because it draws blood from the entire body, and is beneficial for chronic pain in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder.  It stimulates menstruation and is beneficial for boils and abscesses.  It acts as a substitute for venesection of the neck and groin, but it greatly exhausts the body and fainting often occurs in most people. Cupping the groin is similar in benefit to cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping is to first place an empty cupping vessel and apply moderate suction.  Do not prolong the application of the cupping vessels, but rather apply and remove them quickly to allow the humours to flow evenly to the area.  Repeat this process until the area becomes red and open.
When the redness of blood appears, then apply pressure and resume suction gradually.  Then, consider the condition of the body. In those with delicate, porous flesh, a single pressure should suffice, lest the area become ulcerated.
The cupper should widen the pressure slightly and deepen it;  the suction should be gentle and with a slight movement. If the blood is thick, it should be pressed twice; the first time to open a path for the thin, watery blood, and the second time to fully remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very thick, the pressure should be repeated a third time to achieve the desired result.  In short, if we wish to remove a small amount of blood, one pressure is sufficient; if we want to remove a large amount, we should apply more pressure, especially if the blood is thick.
Therefore, a deep pressure should be applied, but the moderate depth of the pressure should only penetrate the skin.  What ointments and waters should be used when applying cupping, and what should the cupper avoid?  For those with thick, hard, dry skin and tight pores...
The places where cupping is to be applied should be rubbed with softening, opening, dissolving ointments.  In summer, for example,  ointments of  honey, violet, sweet almond, or pumpkin seed are suitable.
In winter, ointments of narcissus, lily, chamomile, or similar are suitable. If the blood is thick and cold, use ointments such as marjoram, dill, benzoin, or rue, and similar.
If the cupper has skin with open pores and soft flesh, then the use of ointment should be avoided.  These individuals should wash their cupping vessels after the procedure with rose water, cool water, elderberry water, pumpkin water, or similar waters.  As for those whose blood is very moist...
They should wash their cupping vessels with vinegar or rue and sumac water, and similar.  As for those with thick blood, they should wash their vessels with old wine, marjoram water, rue or chamomile decoctions, and similar.  Cupping should be avoided during and after bathing.
Rather, it should be used an hour or two after leaving the bath.  One should not sleep after cupping.  This is what the cupper and the blood-letter should consider before and after cupping.
First, it should be considered whether the cupper or blood-letter is bilious, with a predominance of heat and inflammation in their blood.  They should consume cooling foods such as pomegranate, endive, vinegar, lettuce, rosewater, and similar.  Their food should include fowl, lamb, lean meats, and similar.
If their temperament is cold, they should be given honeyed drinks, mead, or basil-seed rosewater.  They should consume moderately aged, aromatic wine, somewhere between old and new.  A limited diet is recommended, consisting of fowl, pigeons, sparrows, and doves.  The amount of drink should be more than the amount of food on the day of cupping and blood-letting.
In some cases, for some individuals, it may be appropriate to administer antidote, musk medicine, or shiltha before cupping or blood-letting, or afterwards, to strengthen the major organs and thin the blood.  However, it should not be given to those who are feverish.
As for cupping that does not require specific conditions, these are the cuppings applied to the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney area, and groin.  Because these organs cannot tolerate conditions, the purpose is either to draw blood from one organ to another.
For example, placing a cupping glass on the breasts in cases of epistaxis, or to remove a cold vapor that has collected in an organ, such as placing a cupping glass on the abdomen and navel.  This loosens the organ, warms it, and alleviates pain by dissolving that vapor.
Cupping may be applied to the kidneys if obstructions or stones are present, as its strong suction might open the blockage or dislodge the stones.  Similarly, it may be used on the liver and spleen when vapors are disturbed within them. These cupping procedures can also be used empty, by suction alone.
Cupping may be used with fire, or filled with lukewarm water for conditions like boils.  The cupping glass, preferably a large one, is filled with either hot water alone or water in which certain suitable herbs have been boiled.
Then, filled, it is placed on the affected area, held, and removed, repeated as needed. This is the method of using cupping with fire.
Its mouth opening is the width of two open fingers, as depicted, and its depth is about half a span.  A small hole, just large enough for a needle to fit, is made near the middle of one side of the cupping glass. It is crafted from thick-edged Chinese or brass.
It is smooth, flat, and polished so that it does not harm the organ when placed upon it.  A cross-shaped piece of brass or iron is in the middle to hold the burning candle. This cupping device can be made larger or smaller than described, depending on the ailment.
The user's skill is important; cupping for children and thin individuals is different from cupping for strong, robust adults.  The method for applying the cupping device with fire to the affected area involves taking a tightly-wound linen wick or a small pitch candle and placing it in the center of the hardened rod at the cupping device's center.
The fire should rise upwards toward the bottom of the cupping device, so that the patient's body is not burned.  Then, it is placed on the affected area. A finger is placed on the hole we mentioned, so that when the cupping device is held, the finger does not need to be removed.
Steam will emerge from that hole, and the cupping device will be released at the location. Then the wick is adjusted as needed.  A cupping device used for water-based sores does not have a hardened rod or a hole.
Instead, it is used by filling it with water and placing it directly onto the affected area. This is its appearance/image.
The larger the cupping device, the better, as it can hold more water.
Chapter Ninety-seven.
On the application of leeches.
Leech application is primarily used on parts of the body where cupping cannot be applied, either due to their small size, such as the lips and gums, or because the area lacks substantial flesh, like the fingers and nose.
To use them, select leeches from pure, uncontaminated fresh water, then keep them in fresh water for a day and a night to ensure they are hungry and have no food in their bellies. Then the body is first evacuated by bloodletting or cupping, then the affected area is cleansed.
Until it reddens, then the leech is placed upon it.  If it fills up and falls off, that is effective.  If the area can be cupped, this is more beneficial.  Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar, then copious water, and massage and squeeze it.  If, after the leeches have fallen off, bleeding continues as a trickle, then dampen a linen cloth in cold water.
Place it over the area until the bleeding stops. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered alum, or astringent, or similar coagulants, until the bleeding ceases, or apply peeled halves of chickpea.
Leave it until the chickpea adheres to the area, and the bleeding will stop.  If further leeches are needed, avoid using the same leeches if possible. If the leeches refuse to attach, wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or, pierce the area with a needle until some blood emerges, then apply the leech.  If it feels blood and attaches to the spot, if you want it to detach, sprinkle some powdered dates, salt or ash on it and it will fall off.
The Third Chapter.
Of fractures.
This chapter, too, is essential to the practice of medicine, concerning the setting of fractures and dislocations of bones.
Know, my children, that many ignorant physicians and laypeople claim to understand this chapter, having never examined the writings of the ancients or read a single word from them.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become extinct in our land, and I have never encountered a skilled practitioner of it. My understanding of it, however, came from extensive reading of the works of the ancients and diligent study to grasp their meaning.  Then, I devoted my entire life to experience and practice.
I have presented to you in this chapter everything my knowledge encompasses. After presenting and clarifying it for you, I have refined it, removing unnecessary verbiage, condensing it to the utmost brevity, and explaining it thoroughly.  I have also depicted numerous examples of the instruments used in this art.
This is an added clarification, as I have done in the previous two chapters.  There is no power except through God, the Exalted, the Great.
The First Chapter.
This chapter contains a summary of the essentials of bone fracture treatment.  Before we begin describing individual fractured and dislocated limbs, it is necessary to discuss general principles and sections in this chapter. This will first necessitate your understanding and grasping of its truth, you and any others eager to learn this noble craft.
So I say that whenever a fracture, dislocation, or injury from a fall occurs, one should first hasten to reduce the fracture or dislocation, or both. Unless prevented by factors like weakness or if the person involved is a child or very old person, or if the time is extremely hot or cold.
Then, his diet should be limited to cold legumes, poultry, and venison.  Drinks, fatty meats, overeating, and all foods that fill the veins with blood should be avoided, until the swelling subsides and the risk of fluid accumulation at the site is minimized.
Then, the patient should return to his original diet, as was his custom.  If the fractured bone begins to knit, the patient should consume foods that are nourishing, thick, and strong, with a viscous quality, such as lentils, rice, heads of vegetables, and beef tripe. Eggs, soft fish, thick drinks, and similar foods.  With this regimen, the fracture will knit faster and better.  Know that fractured bones in strong men and the elderly will not always unite to their original state.
Due to the dryness and hardness of their bones, the joining of bones may not be as complete as in the supple bones of young children. However, nature produces a substance resembling glue, thick and adhesive, on all sides of the fractured bone, attaching and holding the parts together.
Connecting them until they achieve the same strength and security as before, so that nothing hinders their functions.
For this reason, the patient's diet should consist of foods that are strong, viscous, and thick, as we have mentioned.  Know that fractures vary depending on the affected body part, as a fracture of the leg bone differs from a fracture of the head bone, and a fracture of the chest differs from a fracture of the spine.
And so too are all the other parts different from one another. We will discuss each type of fracture in detail in its own section. The type of bone fracture may also vary within itself.
Because a fracture may be a simple break without splinters, or it may be along the length of the bone with splinters and detached or non-detached fragments.  The fracture may also involve a wound and tearing of the skin, or it may be a slight crack.
Each type has a specific method for its treatment, as will be explained in detail in its place.  A sign of a fractured bone is its deformity, its prominence, its visibility, and its creaking sound when touched with the hand.
If there is no visible deformity, no creaking sound, and no pain or discomfort when examining the bone, and the patient does not experience much pain, there is no fracture; it could be a sprain, a mild fracture, or a slight crack.  Therefore, it should not be moved, prodded, or manipulated at all.
Instead, apply the appropriate remedies to be discussed later, then apply a gentle bandage.  Know that if the bone is fractured and compressed with two forces without splintering, then each part has moved away from its counterpart.
You should immediately attempt to realign and adjust it before a hot swelling develops. If a hot swelling occurs, leave it for several days until it subsides, then adjust it gently and using any possible method.
Know that realigning and adjusting a bone is easier than one with splinters. Then, it will be bandaged as described later. If the bone has splinters, the fractured limb (whether an arm or leg) must be stretched from both ends.
Either by your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with both ropes and hands.  Position the limb on a level surface in its natural shape.  Once the fractured bone parts have been extended, carefully and gently return the protruding parts to their proper positions.
Strive to avoid causing pain or discomfort to the patient through your actions.  Do your best to reunite the two bone fragments in the best possible position.  At that time, you should touch and feel them with your hands. If you find anything out of place, adjust and correct it to the best of your ability.
Avoid excessive stretching and forceful prodding, as many ignorant practitioners do.  Often, they cause a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb through their actions, as I have witnessed repeatedly.
After the realignment, careful adjustment, and bandaging, keep the limb still and quiet. Warn the patient not to move it during wakefulness, sleep, turning over, restlessness, bowel movements, or any other activity.  The patient should ensure that the limb's position prevents pain.
If, while in that position, the patient feels pain or discomfort in the limb, he should change the position to one that does not cause pain.  He should also ensure that this new position is straight and even.
So that the limb does not become crooked if it is set.  As for how to bandage a broken limb, as I describe to you, know that broken limbs vary in size, shape, and condition.  Small ones, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and similar parts...
The bandages for small limbs should be soft and gentle, while for larger ones, like the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be strong and firm.  A wide bandage is necessary for a large limb, holding it securely and evenly on all sides, free of any irregularities.
Then, after completing the realignment, apply the appropriate plaster. Using a soft bandage, cover the fracture site.  Then, wrap the bandage three or four times around the fracture itself, depending on the limb's size.  Slightly tighten the bandage, and then continue it upward from the fracture site.
Tighten the bandage less than you did on the fracture site.  Then, gradually loosen the bandage as you move away from the fracture.  Gradually work to realign the limb until it is properly positioned.  Then, use another bandage to wrap the fracture site.
Wrap the bandage several times, then continue down the fracture site.  Maintain the tension and looseness of the wrapping as described in the first, upper wrap.  Place soft padding or bandages between the layers to correct any misalignment of the fracture.
If there is misalignment, do not add padding. Otherwise, apply another bandage. Then, apply firm splints to these layers, if there is no swelling or inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or inflammation, apply something to reduce the swelling and dispel the puffiness. Leave it for a few days, then apply the splints.  The splints should be made from wide, properly shaped reeds or from ebony wood.
Made of pine, palm fronds, reeds, or similar readily available materials, each splint should be of this exact size and shape.
However, the splint placed directly on the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others. The splint's length should be appropriate to the size of the fractured limb.  Shape of the splint:
Then, bind the splints with another bandage, in the same way as the first.  Tie firmly on top with strong thread, increasing the tightness over the fracture site. The less tightness should be used the further from the fracture.
The threads should be of medium thickness and not too thin, preferably made from moist linen.  For threads that are thick, like those made by ignorant people from twisted linen strips, is a serious mistake.
Because the tightness is then uncontrolled.  Thin threads are also unsuitable, as the desired tightness cannot be achieved.  There should be at least a finger's width between the splints.
If the patient is irritated by the edges of the splints after tightening in the correct places, place soft padding or fluffy wool underneath so that nothing irritates him.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and a skin perforation, we will discuss that separately.
Know that not every fractured limb needs to be splinted from the first day.  If the limb is large, splints should not be applied until five or seven days, or longer, depending on your confidence that inflammatory swelling will not occur.
This is a description of the bandages used by the ancients to treat fractures, dislocations, and sprains.  It is a simple, concise bandage that sets fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is neither excessively hot nor cold.
Take the flour dust, the fine particles clinging to the millstones during operation, and knead it as it is, without sifting it with egg white.  Make the dough neither thick nor thin, then bake it.
Another bandage for fractures, dislocations, and sprains: Take ten dirhams each of mastic, ladanum, acacia, frankincense, myrrh, and saffron; five dirhams each of gum arabic and licorice; and twenty dirhams each of gum arabic/Roman clay and Armenian clay. Grind all the ingredients together and mix them with gum arabic water, or with egg white if the patient's temperament is hot.  Using this bandage, which is well-combined, quickly sets fractured bones and is suitable for most people due to its balanced nature.
Another bandage, also beneficial for fractures and sprains: take ten dirhams each of myrrh, mastic, and white hellebore; five dirhams each of gum arabic and licorice; six dirhams of acacia; and twenty dirhams of Armenian clay. Grind all thoroughly, sift, and knead with water or egg white, then apply.
A bandage for dislocated joints and bones, relieving pain, and treating bone splintering and fractures: Take prepared wool, dip it in cooked vinegar and oil, and apply it to the affected area.  This bandage does not possess the strength to set a fracture.
However, it is excellent for relieving hot swelling and pain. Another bandage for setting a fractured bone: take unripe fig leaves and wild poppy leaves, grind them together, and apply to the wound in a wet state. Another, brief bandage to use when a bone is fractured.
To resolve the remaining swelling, take portions of hellebore root, chamomile, violet, and quince flour, grind them all together, and knead them with plaster if the limb is not inflamed.  If it is inflamed, knead with coriander water or water, and apply.
Another stronger resolving bandage, used when a hard swelling occurs after a bone fracture: take a portion of each of the following: hellebore root, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, violet, and chamomile.
Grind them all together and knead with vinegar water, or fresh water, or plaster; this depends on the limb's heat and the calming of its heat.  As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that this should be applied to a fractured limb during setting.
Except for the plaster made from wax and oil only, and he described it as being intermediate in thickness between thick and thin.  As for Galen, he believed that things containing dryness and some heat, such as myrrh, gentian, and frankincense, and similar things should be placed on the fractured limb during setting.
A bandage recipe for weakness and pain: Take mugwort, chickpeas, human hair (or bird feathers), hellebore, and salt, all in equal parts.  Grind, sift, knead, and apply as a bandage.  As for the appropriate amount of ligament that should remain...
Then, it should be removed.  See if the patient experiences no pain, no rubbing, nor movement of the fractured bone from its place.  Do not remove it for many days.  However, if there is severe rubbing, disturbing pain, or swelling in the area, [remove the bandage].
Quickly remove it at once; do not delay.  Remove the bandage.  Then take a soft cloth or a damp sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area until the itching and pain subside.  Then allow the limb to rest for an hour.
Then apply softened wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, and bind it for the night.  To soothe the hot swelling and ease the swelling and pain of the limb.  Then, reapply gentle pressure and a light bandage, but not as tight as the initial bandage.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and swelling have completely disappeared, and a bandage and pressure are needed, reapply as you did initially.  If nothing mentioned above occurs in the limb.
Do not remove it until after three, four, five, or seven days.  Sometimes it may be left for twenty days; all depending on the condition of the limb as we have said, until the fracture is firm and the flesh has begun to heal around it.
Then, in that case, increase the pressure even more than your initial pressure, and also increase the patient's nourishment as previously mentioned.  If you observe that the fracture site is dried up and wasted more than necessary, know that nourishment is not reaching it.
Then, bathe it with lukewarm water each time you remove it, and do so every three days.  Ease the pressure slightly.  By this action, nourishment will reach the limb and it will heal quickly. As for what the ignorant among the setters of broken limbs do, of breaking the limb again...
Because if it is not set properly the first time, and is set crookedly, that is an error on their part and a great deception.  Even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it.  I have seen no trace of this from any of them at all. The correct approach is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
Concerning fractures occurring in the head.
Fractures of the head are numerous, their forms varied, and their causes diverse. Some fractures result from sword blows, and the bone may be broken all the way to the peritoneum beneath the bone. As happens with wood when it is struck.
And this type of fracture is therefore called a 'struck' fracture.  Alternatively, the sword's cut may have broken only part of the bone, and its edge alone was fractured, without the cut reaching through completely; this type is called an outright fracture. The wound caused by these two types of fractures may be large or small.
Some fractures are caused by a blow with a stone or a fall on a stone or the like, resulting in a contusion or a bruise.  This fracture may either penetrate and approach the membrane beneath the bone, or else be limited to the surface of the bone.
And the wound of these fractures too may be either large or small. Some fractures are hidden within the bone, subtle, like a slight fissure.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture.  A fracture of this type may occur due to a fall, or a blow from a stone or similar object, forcing a portion of the bone inward, causing a depression in the area, similar to how a copper object might be dented by a blow, with a part of it being driven inward.
This is most common in the spongy bone of the head, such as in children's heads. All of these fracture types may have splintered and un-splintered fragments. We will describe the treatment of all of these in their respective places, and we will identify all these fracture types by examining them.
By probing them and removing any putrefied flesh from them.  The hairline fracture is recognized by examining the bone, and rubbing and staining it with ink; the fracture will appear black.  As for the treatment of the fracture, first consider the patient's symptoms.
If you observe obvious signs of danger, such as vomiting bile, stretching, loss of awareness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, bulging and redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms, then do not approach the patient or treat him, for death is certain in most cases with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that do not cause great concern, and hope for his recovery, then proceed to treat him.  This is if the injured person comes to you soon after the injury, during the winter months.
You should endeavor to remove the bone before the fourteenth day, in any case.  If it is during the summer months, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane beneath the bone deteriorates and the symptoms we described appear.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is crushing and injury, you must cut away the crushed and injured part as I describe to you.  This is by shaving the head of the injured patient.
And expose the bone in whatever way possible, and according to the shape of the wound and what is causing discomfort to the patient. If during the exposure of the bone, you encounter bleeding or a hot swelling, then treat it appropriately.
This means to fill the area with cloths soaked in rosewater and ointment, and clean the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding is controlled.  Then, begin to turn and remove the bone, which can be done in one of two ways.
One method is to cut the bone with a narrow, precise incision, and this is its illustration.
Then, you use another incision wider than the previous one, and this is its illustration.
Then use yet another incision, wider still than the second.  You should have a set of different incisions, some wider than others, some shorter than others, all with extremely sharp edges, made of Indian steel or good quality steel.
Use gentle pressure when striking the incision to avoid jarring the head and causing further harm. If the bone is strong and hard, it is necessary to drill around it before using the incisions with the drills they called "non-dipping drills.
Because they do not penetrate the bone of the skull beyond, for the drill has a rounded edge beneath its sharp head, similar to a ring or small circle, preventing it from sinking and going beyond the thickness of the bone.
You should have many such drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that for each skull, you have a drill whose sharp point is appropriate in length and shortness to the thickness of that skull.  This is an illustration of three types. Of drills, large, medium, and small.
As for how to drill around the fractured bone, place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated, then move the drill to another location, and space each drill hole by the thickness of a reed or similar.
Then cut between each two holes with the chisels, doing so with the utmost gentleness, as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with another instrument you have prepared for this.
Such as bone saws and fine forceps.  You must be very careful that the drill or chisel touches no part of the peritoneum. If you have smoothed the bone, freed it from the peritoneum, if it was attached, and extracted it, then you must clean it.
And smooth any roughness remaining on the bone with another instrument similar to a chisel, though it must be finer and gentler than other chisels.  If any small pieces or fragments of bone remain, remove them gently with the instruments available.
Then treat the wound afterward with the compresses and ointments mentioned.  The other approach to the procedure is very straightforward, free from danger. Galen mentioned and praised it greatly. This is what he said. First, you should begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and prominent.  Once you have exposed that part, you should place the tip of this lens-shaped chisel beneath it.  This is its image:.
The lens-shaped part of it should be smooth, so it does not cut anything.  The sharp part of it, on its long sides, will be as you see it, so the lens-shaped part rests against the peritoneum, and the sharp edge of the chisel against the bone. Then strike the chisel from one side.
With a small hammer, so that you gently sever all of the bone as it turns.  And you are safe from the membrane; no frightening accident will occur, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he is asleep.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in certain places, scrape it off with the tip of the lens-shaped chisel itself, and remove it gently.  It will come off without harm or fear.
If the fracture of the bone did not penetrate to the membrane, and the broken bone's surface remained rough and with splinters, then you should scrape off those roughnesses and remove those splinters using small tools of various sizes.  This way you can use the most appropriate one for each place, according to what the work indicates.
The fractured bone itself and its shape; you should use the widest of these tools for scraping first, then use a narrower one after, and continue doing this, in order to arrive at the use of the narrowest and thinnest of them all.
For all the smaller, hair-like fractures and gentle breaks, you must apply a treatment to each one according to what will bring about its repair.  This is something not hidden from anyone with even the slightest training in this craft and who has considered what we have written and explained about major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, you should take a linen cloth sized to the wound, soak it in rose-oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, folded or triangular-shaped. Soak it in rose-water and rose-oil, and place it on the first cloth.  Do this as gently as possible so as not to burden the peritoneum.  Then use a wide bandage over it, and do not tighten it except to the degree that it holds the wound only.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two until the hot swelling subsides.  Then remove it and apply afterwards the medicines that are known for their drying qualities, such as the roots of lilies, the flour of the kresna, the powders of kandar and zarawand, and the like.  Make these into poultices.
Dust the wound with them as they are dry. In general, use a medicine that cleanses without stinging.  Do your utmost while treating the wound to ensure that it is clean, free of grease, dirt, or pus, and that no pus collects within it at all.
Because if pus collects on the meninges, it corrupts and rots them, causing a great illness to the patient, and this may occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If a blackness appears on its surface, then if the patient displays the symptoms we have mentioned, know that he is doomed without fail.  And if the blackness occurred because of a medicine applied to it, and the potency of that medicine was such as to produce that blackness.
You should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, blend them well, and apply them to a cloth. Then place it on the peritoneum and treat it with the necessary remedies until it heals.
Chapter Three.
In setting a fractured nose.
Know that only the upper halves of the nose, or one of them, can break, since they are bones. The lower part is cartilaginous and does not break, but rather is subject to contusions, deviations, and flattening.
If one of its halves is fractured, you should insert the little finger into the nostril and straighten the fracture from the inside with your index finger and thumb from the outside, so that you restore the nose to its natural shape.  Do this gently, ensuring that your actions do not cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then it should be straightened with a blunt instrument. If the fracture is in both sides, do the same.  He should begin the reduction on the first day following the fracture.
If possible, and otherwise after seven or ten days when the inflammation has subsided, you should insert a thread of linen into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two threads if the fracture is on both sides.
The thread should be thick enough to fill the nostril.  Some early physicians recommend soaking the thread in ghee and changing it daily.  However, I do not think this is necessary, but instead the thread should be soaked in beaten egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the thread in place until the bone has set and the cartilage has solidified.  After the thread is in place, you can insert goose quill tubes wrapped in soft linen into the nostril. This will provide a stronger hold for the fracture and prevent the patient from having difficulty breathing.
This is not essential.  If you wish, use the threads, or if you prefer, perform the procedure using the threads.  If inflammation develops in the nose during your procedure, bandage the nose with a piece of linen cloth or cotton dipped in vinegar, rose oil, or a diachylon ointment.
If no inflammation develops, you should bandage the outside of the nose with a mixture of semolina, finely ground kandar (a type of resin), and beaten egg white, then place a soft compress on top. Do not bind the nose at all, especially if the nasal bones have been fractured only slightly.
Or if it has fragmented, you should dissect the fragments, remove them with a suitable instrument, then suture the gap and treat it with ointments appropriate for healing and suppuration. If a wound occurs inside the nose, treat it with thread and use lead tubes. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On setting fractured lower jawbones.
If the lower jaw is fractured but not accompanied by an open wound, and if the fracture is only from the outside, and not fractured in two and not concave inward, then its identification is easy.
If the fracture is on the right side, one should insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth.  Likewise, if the fracture is on the left side, insert the index finger of the right hand and gently lift the fracture bump from the inside outward.
With your other hand on the outside of the bone, you control its alignment.  If the jaw fracture has split into two, then traction should be applied from both sides to straighten it.  If it has affected the teeth, they should be gently separated.
Or, if any part is likely to separate, tie it with a gold, silver, or linen thread.  Then, apply a splint to the fractured jaw, then place a folded cloth over the splint and finally a large, firm bandage or a piece of leather equal in length to the jaw. Then tie it securely above, as best you can to hold it together, so it doesn't move.  Instruct the patient to rest and be quiet.  Give the patient soft food.  If you suspect a change in shape, loosen it on the third day.
Then tie it securely above, as best you can to hold it together, so it doesn't move.  Instruct the patient to rest and be quiet.  Give the patient soft food.  If you suspect a change in shape, loosen it on the third day. Then, fix the bandage with flour dust mixed with egg white or semolina, after removing the splint.  Apply a soft compress to the bandage.  As long as the bandage stays on and the bone's condition doesn't change, leave it in place.
Do not remove it until the fracture heals and strengthens.  The fracture often strengthens in three weeks. If a hot swelling appears during that time, use the methods we mentioned previously to calm it until it subsides.
If the fracture is accompanied by an open wound, and a piece or pieces of bone have separated, gently remove the pieces with an appropriate tool. If the wound is narrow, widen it with forceps as needed.
Then, if you have removed all the pieces of bone, suture the wound if it is wide.  Otherwise, apply one of the suitable ointments. And treat the wound until it heals.
Fifth Chapter.
On the treatment of a fractured collarbone.
The collarbone is most often fractured from the front, at the end of the shoulder blade.  The fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it breaks and splinters in two without creating fragments, which is the easiest to treat;
or the fracture may create fragments, which is more difficult to treat; or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound.  If the fracture is without a wound, have two assistants; one to hold the upper arm adjacent to the fractured collarbone, and the other to extend the neck to the opposite side.
Then adjust the fracture with your fingers until it is properly aligned, without any bumps or irregularities.  If further extension is necessary, a ball of linen or wool should be placed under the patient's armpit.
Adjust the bone as needed, extending and lifting the collarbone, and pressing the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned. If you cannot pull the end of the collarbone outwards because it has sunk inwards, the patient should lie on their back.
A medium-sized cushion is placed under the patient's shoulder. The assistant presses down on the shoulder to raise the sunken portion of the collarbone.  Then the fracture is corrected and aligned with the fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the collarbone has broken off and is moving, you should gently separate it.  If the fragment is embedded in the bone, you should use one of the instruments prepared for this purpose to carefully remove it. After the instrument that protects the pleura, which is made of wood or iron, is placed under the collarbone, this is its image/form.
It resembles a spoon, without any curves. Its width should be adjusted to the size of the bone, and its length according to the required work.  It should have two ends, one wider and the other narrower, as you see.
If the wound you created to remove the bone fragment is wide and you fear inflammation, stitch the edges of the wound together. If the wound is small, or you fear serious inflammation, treat the wound with sutures and bandages according to the size of the wound.
If there is a hot inflammation, soak the bandages in rose oil and vinegar or a drink, and apply them.  As for a fracture, if there is no wound or incision, apply a bandage made of mill dust mixed with egg white to the bone. Place a soft splint on it, then place a ball under his armpit if needed. Then take a very long bandage, about a span wide or so.  Apply a folded bandage over the splint and dressing. Then take a splint made of thin wood. Its width should be three fingers and the same in length. Then wrap it in a cloth, then place that cloth with the splint in the part of the bandage over the fracture. Then tie the bandage around the fracture as you would with a turban. Tie it to his neck and under his healthy armpit and under his injured armpit, and repeatedly tighten it on each side.  As you see, the tightening secures the fracture firmly, which is clear to you.
The whole point is that the splint should not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day.  Whenever the ligaments relax and you see the splint has moved, correct it and tighten the bandage.  Then, have the patient sleep on their back, and place a small pillow under their armpit at night.
To raise his arm away from his side, so that the clavicle fracture rises with the shoulder blade, or tie his arm to his neck. Do not loosen the bandage unless a wound or swelling occurs at the site within twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see reason to do so. Maintain the tension and leave it until the clavicle fracture is healed and the bond is strong, and it becomes even stronger over twenty-eight days. It may be in some people in less than that.
Chapter Six.
On the reduction of a fractured shoulder.  The shoulder rarely fractures in its wide portion, but rather its edges.  Thus, if a part of it breaks or fractures are in the middle, it is known by touch.  Then, according to the shape of the fracture, apply a splint.
Align it and restore it to its natural shape by all possible means. Then, apply mill dust, egg white, and soft linen on the area, and place a folded cloth pad on top. Finally, place a wide splint made of a plank over it.
Make the splint as wide as the entire shoulder or slightly wider. If there is a depression under the splint in any part of the shoulder, smooth the depression with soft linen until the splint is level, then bind a long turban over it.
Secure the splint firmly so that it does not shift from its position.  Examine the bandage daily, and tighten it whenever it loosens.  Realign the splint if it moves.  The patient should lie on his sound side, and the shoulder will be healed in twenty days.
For one or twenty-five days. Once this period is complete, remove the bandage, confident that it will not harm the bone;  for it is one of the bones that one does not fear shattering or displacement. If a fragment of bone protrudes and is piercing the skin, make an incision over it and remove it. Do what I mentioned for a fractured collarbone concerning the treatment of any inflammation that may occur.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of fractured ribs.
The chest may fracture in the middle, though this is rare.  Its extremities are more prone to fracture and splintering.  If the middle breaks, symptoms include a downward tilt, severe pain, and difficulty breathing, along with coughing.
Blood may also be coughed up, and the fracture in the bone becomes evident, easily discernible to the touch.  To treat it, the patient lies on their back, a cushion placed between their shoulder blades.  Then, the patient's sides are pressed together, and the ribs are brought together with the hands, from both sides, carefully aligned on all possible surfaces.
And hold it until the bone returns to its proper shape.  Then, apply a bandage and splint, placing on top a splint made of thin board of poplar, willow, or similar lightweight material, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then carefully tie it to the fractured bone to prevent displacement.  Wind the bandage around the back in a circular motion several times and tighten it firmly.  Then, check the bandage frequently, tightening it whenever it loosens.
And if the injury necessitates its removal due to a wound, pain, or swelling at the site, then promptly remove it, and take off the bandage.  Correct any issue that has arisen with appropriate treatment; then, reapply the bandage if deemed necessary. Apply to it and maintain the compression until healed.
Chapter Eight.
In the setting of fractured ribs, know that fractures occur in the thick parts of the ribs near the spine, and their anterior extremities;  they are prone to injury because they are cartilaginous.  This knowledge is readily apparent to the touch when examining with the fingers.
Treat the fracture by aligning the broken pieces with the fingers until the shape is corrected.  Then, bandage and compress the fractured bone with a splint if necessary.  If the ribs are fractured inward, the patient will experience severe pain.
And the pain is like the pain that occurs in those with a hernia, because the bone pierces the diaphragm and also causes difficulty breathing, coughing, and profuse bleeding. This is a difficult condition to treat, and the ancients used many methods to address it.
Some said that the patient should consume foods that produce flatulence and wind to inflate the abdomen and push the fractured bone outward.  We discourage this, lest it exacerbate the development of inflammation if it has not yet occurred; and if inflammation has already begun, it will worsen and confirm its presence.
Some said that a cupping device should be applied to the area, and then strongly suctioned.  This is more logical, but one fears that the cupping device might pull substances to the area due to its weakness.  Others said that the area should be covered with wool dipped in hot oil.
The space between the ribs becomes filled in, so that the ligament is flat if wrapped around the circumference.  Then the patient is treated for the hernia, with diet and medicine.  If the patient is severely strained, they cannot endure it. The bone was piercing the diaphragm painfully, and we feared for the patient.  Therefore, it is necessary to cut open the area, expose the fractured rib, then place the instrument that protects the peritoneum, as previously described, underneath it and gently remove the bone.
Then, if the wound is large, we stitch the edges together and treat it with ointments until it heals.  If inflammation occurs during this process, immediately apply rose-oil-soaked dressings to the affected area and treat the patient internally to reduce the swelling.
He should lie on the side on which he sleeps more easily until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
On the setting of spinal and neck fractures.
If the neck bones are fractured, this is rare; more often, they are bruised.  The same applies to the vertebrae of the spine.  If this occurs to someone and you want to know whether they will recover or not... If you observe that his hands are relaxed, numb, and lifeless, and he cannot move, extend, or grasp them; if, when you pinch or prick them with a needle, he feels no sensation or pain, know that in most cases he will not recover; he is doomed.
But if he can move them and feel the pinching and pricking, know that the marrow of the bone has been spared, and the patient will recover with treatment.  If a spinal fracture presents similarly, and you want to know whether recovery is possible...
Examine his legs.  If you find that they are relaxed, and the same condition as the hands has occurred, and then, when he lies on his back, wind and feces are expelled involuntarily, and when he lies on his stomach, urine is expelled involuntarily... And if, when he lies on his back, he is unable to urinate, know that he is doomed, and do not bother with treatment. If none of these things occur, the matter is less serious.  Treatment for what has happened should aim at relieving the inflammatory swelling.
By placing rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks, on the affected vertebra three times a day, until the inflammatory swelling subsides.  Then apply one of the strengthening, dry dressings to the area and bind it with a bandage.  Instruct him to rest and remain still.
He should sleep only on the side that does not cause pain until he recovers. If a splinter or something detached from the bone has occurred during the injury, the skin should be incised and the detached bone extracted. Then, if the wound is large, the edges should be brought together with sutures.  Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals.  If the last bone of the coccyx, the tailbone, is broken, the little finger of the left hand should be inserted into the rectum.
The broken bone should be aligned with the other hand as much as possible.  Then apply a bandage and splint, if necessary, and bind it.
If you feel a fractured splinter in it, incise it and remove it. Treat the wound with the previous treatment until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
The bones of the thighs rarely fracture. If they do fracture, the fracture is usually a splintering at the ends, a longitudinal crack, and inward displacement.  The affected person experiences pain, stabbing, and numbness in the leg on the fractured side.
To set it, run your hand over it to determine the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at the ends, straighten the ends to the best of your ability, matching the natural shape.
If the fracture is longitudinal, or has shifted inward, lay the patient on his stomach until you can set the fracture.  Once you have straightened it, apply bandages, and then a splint of wood or leather, securing it firmly.
Do not fear that the fracture will shift or that the splint will loosen.  Fill the hollows of the hips with padding until the splint is evenly fitted.  Instruct the patient to sleep on his back or his healthy side, if a hot swelling appears.
Refrain from manipulating or setting it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply whatever remedies are appropriate, as previously mentioned.  Then, resume setting and securing it properly.  If fragments or splinters appear in the bone, do not remove them.
Do not touch it internally, but straighten it externally, as we have said. Leave it secured until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting a fractured humerus.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, set it using one of two methods. One method is to take a smooth, slightly curved stick, medium thickness, in this manner:
Tie straps to its ends, then hang it from a high point. The patient sits in a chair, then places the broken arm on the stick so that the armpit lies against the middle curve of the stick. Then hang a heavy object from above it.
Or a servant can pull it downwards, and then the physician aligns the fracture with his hands until the fracture is restored to its proper position; and the other method is that the patient lies on his back, and the arm is suspended from the neck by a bandage.
Then order two servants; one to hold the part above the fracture in his hands, and the other to hold the part below it; and each of them to pull in their direction. If you want a stronger pull, tie a bandage below the fracture and another above it, and have each servant pull towards their respective sides.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, the bandage should be placed under the armpit, and the other under the fracture towards the elbow. Likewise, if the fracture is near the elbow, the bandage should be placed in that location and on the elbow itself.
Then the fracture is aligned gently, without force, until it is properly aligned and fits well.  Then it is tightened, unless a hot swelling appears. If a hot swelling does appear, leave tightening until the seventh day.  Apply moistened wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides.  Then tighten it.  The method of tightening is to place the dressing on the fracture, then place a folded piece of new cloth over the dressing.  Then bring the arm back to the upper arm, and place the hand open on the shoulder.
Hold the cloths and tightening on the upper arm and forearm, so that the arm acts as a splint, if nothing prevents you from doing so and there has been no change in the broken bone. If you fear a change in the bone, use splints.
The splint placed on the fracture itself should be wider and stronger than the other splints.  A space of one finger's width should be between each splint.  The length of the splints should be according to the fracture, with an additional three finger-widths on each side.
Then tighten the splints with the tightening described in the first section.
It is that your tightening should be more intense at the site of the fracture.
The further away from the fracture, the less the tightening.  If you see the placement of the splints and tightening, as we mentioned, during the setting of the limb, do so.
