If you fear a hot swelling, leave the tightening and splints until the seventh day, as we said.  Then examine the ligaments every three days, lest irritation or swelling occur in the area, or food be prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightening.  All this is rectified as mentioned.
If you are confident that none of this will occur, do not loosen the ligaments until many days have passed.  The patient should lie on their back, their hand on their abdomen.  A padded arm sling filled with wool should be placed under the arm.
Examine at all times, night or day, lest the fractured limb's shape change or the ligament relax.  Correct all of this with your effort.  Make the patient's diet as we previously described, first gentle, until the bone strengthens.
Then the diet should be made thicker.  The arm and leg usually strengthen in forty days.  At that time, the ligaments should be loosened, and baths should be used, and treated with ointments suitable for that, if the fracture is extensive and complicated.
Do not loosen the ligaments until fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve.
In the setting of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the radii. One is small, and it is the one next to the thumb. The other is large, and it is located under the smaller one, below. It may happen that only the larger radius is broken, or only the smaller one, or both may be broken.
If the upper small radius is broken, its setting is easy and its healing is quicker. If the lower radius is broken, the fracture is worse and healing is more difficult. The worst case is when both bones are broken.  If the broken bone is the upper small radius...
Therefore, the physician must, when setting it, make the traction gentle and slight to align it. If the larger radius is fractured, the traction must be stronger. If both radii are fractured, the traction must be very strong indeed.
The hand should be positioned during the setting and traction, stretched out on a cushion. The thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers. The pinky finger should be lower than the other fingers. The patient should be seated upright.
The cushion should be at the same height so that the patient does not have to strain.
Then, an attendant will support the arm from below, either by hand or with a bandage, and another attendant will support it from above.  Then the physician will align the bone to the best possible position. If the fracture has splinters, they should be adjusted.
Adjust each splinter back into its place using your best effort. If a detached splinter appears, piercing the skin, and you have no hope of setting it, then cut around it and remove it as previously described.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, a separate chapter addresses it.
If, at the beginning of the setting, a hot swelling appears, apply a compress made from rose-oil-infused beeswax.  The compress should be medium thickness, and apply gentle pressure until the swelling subsides.
Remove the compress and apply a bandage made of mill dust and egg white.  Then apply the splints.  The splint covering the fracture itself should be wider and stronger.  Know that, in most cases, six splints are needed for the arm.
The fracture may be in one ulna or in both ulnas.  Then, make your traction stronger at the fracture site.  As you move the traction upward or downward, gradually lessen the tension, as previously mentioned in the beginning of this chapter.
The bandages wrapped around the fracture should be soft and damp, not overly stiff.  The thread used for traction should be linen, of medium thickness as described.  Inspect the limb and ligaments after several days.
If a problem arises, such as irritation on the limb, warm water should be applied to the affected area until the irritation subsides. The limb should be left untensioned for a night to rest, then traction should be resumed.
If the traction has become slack and the bone has shifted, or similar problems have occurred, correct everything to the best of your ability.  Also, if the limb is not receiving enough nourishment due to excessive traction, it should be relaxed slightly and left for a few days until nourishment returns, then the traction should be tightened again.
If none of the previously mentioned problems arise, the patient should not be released until twenty days or so.  Then, hang the patient's arm from their neck, keeping the arm straight.  They should avoid strenuous movements and sleep on their back.
Know that this arm fracture is set within thirty days, or thirty-two days, and perhaps even twenty-eight days, all depending on the individual's constitution and strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of fractures of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpals and phalanges of the fingers rarely fracture, but are often injured.  When a fracture or injury occurs to the hand...
The patient should sit in a position of rest, with a chair in front of him at a proper height.
Then, the patient places his hand outstretched on the chair.  The attendant should then straighten the fractured bones, and the physician should adjust them until a proper alignment is achieved.  At this point, a dressing and splint should be applied, unless a hot swelling has developed, and then a brace should be applied over the area.
Wrap it in a soft cloth.  If the fracture is towards the palm of the hand, make a ball shape from cloth.  Instruct the patient to grasp it with their injured hand.  Then tie it with a long cloth.
The splint should be made of soft leather, so the skin can heal with the flexing of the entire hand, and it should be tightened as needed. If the fracture is outward, then the splint should be placed on top and another one beneath the hand to keep the hand open and upright.
Then, the bandage should be applied as the hand is rotated, and the fingers should be fastened together with a bandage.  If the fracture is to one of the fingers, if it is the thumb, adjust it properly, and then bandage it with the hand. Or, if desired, make a small separate splint to straighten the fracture.
Do not move it. If the fracture is in any of the other fingers, such as the middle, index, ring, or little finger, straighten them and bandage them to the adjacent, healthy finger, or bandage them all together.  This is best.  Or, place a small, upright splint on them.
As we stated for the thumb, examine carefully after setting the fracture and after the hot swelling subsides.  Treat any problems that arise according to the descriptions given earlier.
Chapter Fourteen.
On setting a fractured thigh.
The thighbone often fractures and is easily felt, as it shifts back and forth.  It is set by placing a bandage above the fracture and another below, with the patient lying on their face.
Then each attendant pulls the bandage towards their side, keeping it straight, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone.  However, if it is close to the hip joint, a soft bandage, either of wool or similar material, should be used.
At the hip joint, towards the pubic area, the pull should be upwards, and the other bandage downwards, below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee, and the pull should be downwards.  Then the physician should straighten the bone with both hands until it returns to its normal shape.
The bone will heal well.  At that point, the bandage and traction should be maintained unless a hot swelling develops on the limb.  If swelling occurs, leave it for a few days until the hot swelling subsides, and then return to treatment.
For traction, a wide, strong bandage should be wrapped around the fracture twice or three times, with excess material left over.  Then, the leg should be twisted so that the heel is near the hip joint. A long thread should be inserted between the thigh and leg near the lower knee area, with the thread ends extending from both sides above.
Then, wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.  Then, place splints over the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and create a single splint over the shin bone. Fill the gap between the thigh and leg with soft padding so the traction is even.
Then, begin with strong traction from the middle, over the fracture site, three or four times.  The further the bandage is from the fracture site, the less strong and looser the traction should be.
Then, take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg and tie them to the adjacent splints above.  Then, bring the thread ends down to the ankle and use them to secure the other ends of the splints.
So that the bandage does not slip out of position.  Then, maintain the traction as long as there is no pain, swelling, or inflammation in the limb. If any of this happens, immediately loosen the bandage and correct any problem, as we have described repeatedly.
If there is a protruding fragment of bone, it should be smoothed if possible. Otherwise, it should be cut and removed. Treat the wound as previously described until healed.  This thigh fracture may be treated without also splinting the leg.
As we mentioned regarding the humerus and arm,  however, treating it this way will not cause the patient lameness.  But if it is treated in isolation, without the leg being joined, the patient will always be lame.
Know that a fracture of the thigh takes fifty days, or slightly more or less, depending on the variations in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
In fractures of the patella.
The patella rarely fractures, but is often bruised. If a fracture does occur, it is either a split or a fragmentation of its parts, and this may occur with or without a wound.
One must rely on observation in all this, and set the fracture by aligning the separated parts with the fingers until they are brought together and united, as accurately as possible with gentleness and precision. Then, apply a bandage and a circular splint if necessary.
And apply a further bandage consistent with this, and then treat all situations as described for other fractures, such as inflammation, and the like, by addressing each symptom with an appropriate remedy until healing.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the setting of a fractured shin.
The shin has two bones, one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the ulna.  They are subject to the same types of fractures as the arm bones. Therefore, its setting is like that of the arm, the procedure being the same. If both bones are fractured, the leg will turn in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg will turn forward; if the thicker bone is fractured, and this occurs from below, this is evident to you.
Therefore, use traction and realignment, and bind the splints accordingly, with the same procedure; however, if the fracture of the leg is severe with many fragments, the traction should be less and gentler, and take care in setting it.
There is an additional step in treating the leg compared to the arm.  Once you have realigned the splints and completed all your work, take two pieces of pinewood—used for leveling rooms, placed between the gaps in planks, or similar, from palm leaves or similar material.
Choose from them a piece of moderate thickness, not thin, and let its length be the length of the leg from the knee down. Then wrap two layers of cloth around each one lengthwise. Place one on the leg, and the other on the opposite side.
Let it be from the knee to the bottom of the foot. Then tie the two pieces of wood in three places, at the ends and the middle.  With this restraint, the leg will not tilt left or right, and will be well-supported.  A groove of wood may also be used along the length of the leg.
Place it [the groove/restraint] to prevent movement, especially when the fracture is accompanied by a wound.  Check the leg daily and watch carefully for swelling or puffiness, or any other sign. If anything of that sort occurs, treat it accordingly until it heals.  The leg bone will set within thirty days or thereabouts.
Chapter Seventeen.
In the fracture of the bones of the leg and fingers.
The heel bone is rarely fractured.  The bones of the leg may be fractured, and the fingers are also rarely fractured, but rather are mostly bruised.
If a fracture of the leg bones occurs, and you see that some of the bones are overlapping, have the patient stand on the ground with their foot upright as if walking, then place your foot on the raised bones and press them down until they are level.
Until they return to their proper positions. Then take the bandage and splint, place a small, flat board with two ends under the sole of the foot. Securely bind the foot with the bandages and any other materials you require.
After three or four days, release the bandage.  You will find the bones level, whether they were fractured or dislocated.  The procedure is as described.  If some of the fingers are fractured, manipulate and align them, as described for the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the fractured finger, lengthwise and slightly wider than the finger itself.  Place the board I previously described under the foot, and secure it tightly.  If two, three, or more bones are fractured.
Make a splint for each finger, sized appropriately and wrapped in soft cloth.  Secure the board under the foot, ensuring each end of the board extends beyond the sole of the foot for good support.
You should not forget to treat all the fractures as described, addressing each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
Fractures of the woman's pubic bone and the man's pubic bone.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, have her sit down, leaning her body back slightly, with support from behind.  Then, have the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it is full and forms a ball shape within her vulva.
Then, the woman should move and slightly raise her pelvis, causing the cotton to come out until it is like a ball at the entrance of the vulva, and the fracture of the bone is reduced.  Then, a pad is placed on her back. When she wishes to urinate, gently remove the cotton so she can urinate.
And reposition it in the same manner as it was initially placed, then return to the original pad.  Do this for seven days, or similar, and it will heal.  If you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a reed tube around its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva.
Then, blow forcefully into the tube to inflate the bladder within the vulva, which will reduce the fracture.  Then pack it with cotton and maintain the described care for several days until healed.  As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, this method is used for its reduction.
Its application is as described for the hip bone.  The correct procedure for these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, is clear to you, especially if you have some experience and understand this book of mine well, for the fracture itself usually shows you the way.
Follow the correct procedure for its reduction and strengthening.  As for the penis of a man, if it is fractured, take a goose's windpipe, insert the penis into it, then place a bandage of cloth over it and bandage it.  Leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the reduction of bone fractures if there is a wound.
If someone presents with a fracture and a wound, especially if the bone is large like the femur or humerus, or similar, one should promptly perform phlebotomy if the conditions for venesection, as previously described, are met.
If the wound is bleeding, one should promptly cauterize it by applying powdered alum if nothing else is available.  Then proceed to set the fracture on that same day; do not delay.
If no inflammation occurs, if inflammation does occur, then delay the setting until the ninth day, until the inflammation subsides. Do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, as it will expose it to undesirable symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin, exposed, then you should gently attempt to reposition and align it with your hands and a slight extension.  If you cannot reposition and align it with your hands, reposition it using this instrument, which is made of iron, seven or eight fingers in length. And its width should match the wound.
Therefore, the physician should take three or four of these, as needed for treatment in each type of fracture.  They should be round, with a slight thickness so that they do not bend when prodded during use.
It should have a sharp point, a bend at the tip, thicker at the top and gradually thinner towards the bottom.  This is its image/shape:.
It is called a "perem" in Greek, meaning a small lever.  The sharp, bent tip should be placed on the protruding bone end and used to push it back.  Once the bone has returned and is somewhat aligned, use the tool to align the fractured bone ends.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grip it properly, the end of that bone should be cut so that the instrument can hold it. If the bone cannot be brought back as described, cut it using a similar instrument from the ones we have mentioned.
Or saw it open with one of the saws, as best you can.  Then remove any remaining rough edges or thin layers from the bone.  If, after aligning the bone, the patient experiences severe, harmful pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can realign it to its natural position, do so, as you will greatly benefit the patient.  Once the bone is completely set, soak a cloth in a black astringent drink, especially if it is summer, and do not place a bandage or anything oily on the wound.
So that mold and decay do not occur.  Then use splints when you are finished setting the bone. Leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole in the bandages around the wound with scissors.
Be extremely careful not to tighten the wound with the fracture.  Often, unskilled physicians do this, causing their patients either death, or ulcers or nasal discharge. Let your tightening be gentle and relaxed, unlike the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we described, and the patient experiences distressing pain in the area, you should not apply splints.  Instead, make bandages of strong cloth for the splint area and bind them tightly.
If, after a day or two, you see pus forming in the wound, remove the cloth soaked in the drink that you placed on it. Then use the dressings and ointments that are customary for treating wounds, such as the four-fold ointment, and so on. You should also loosen the bandage.
Examine the wound every evening and morning until it heals and is cured.  The limb should be positioned so that pus drains downward easily.  If the wound takes many days and does not close, or the pus does not cease, know that there are small fragments of bone.
Therefore, you should probe the wound with a probe. Remove any detached fragments. If any fragments remain unseparated, and are irritating the limb and causing pain, cut and remove them by any means possible.
If the wound develops a cold, or eating away, or another type of decay or putrefaction, you should counter each such symptom with similar treatment as described in its appropriate section.
If a large, prominent bone such as the femur or humerus, or similar large bone, is fractured, you should not attempt to pull or remove it. For often death results from this.  Leave it until it becomes putrid; perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see an area for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating the complications that arise after some fractures.
This complication often arises after the fracture heals, especially if it is near the joints.  It spoils the appearance of the limb and may prevent the limb from performing its natural function.
If the complication is recent, use astringent remedies such as dates, frankincense, myrrh, gum arabic, and acacia—taking some or all of these, and mixing them with an astringent drink, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply this [mixture] to the complication with care, and bind it firmly. Leave the bandage for many days, then remove it and repeat [the application] until the complication subsides, God willing, or apply a tight lead plate to it.
Lead has the property of removing all that has accumulated in the tissues. If the complication has become hard, severe, and necessity dictates its removal, then cut it from above and remove or scrape off the excess tissue until it is gone. Treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In treating a fracture, if the limb, after being set, remains thin, unlike its original condition.
If a fractured bone is set and the limb remains thin and weak afterward, this is due to many reasons. One reason is either excessive loosening and tying of the ligaments improperly, or excessive tightening of the ligaments so that nourishment cannot reach the limb; or excessive and excessive manipulation; or excessive movements at inappropriate times; or lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
Treatment for this involves nourishing the patient and enriching his body so that blood increases in it, using a bath, bringing joy and happiness to him, and similar measures.  Then apply pitch to the limb to draw much nourishment into it, or continuously manipulate it with lukewarm water. So that nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In treating fractured bones that have been rejoined crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
When a limb that has been set straight after healing shows crookedness, or a bulge in the broken bone, or a knot, and the limb's appearance is thus marred. However, if the limb is not prevented from its natural function, one should not accept the claim of those who assert that they can break the limb from the head; many ignorant physicians and setters of bones in our country do this.
This action is highly reprehensible, leading to considerable risk, potentially causing harm.  But if the crookedness and knotting are recent, it is appropriate to apply water in which relaxing herbs such as plantain leaves and root, and King's crown, and similar, have been boiled.
And apply relaxing dressings, such as well-made diachylon, or take the sap of the plantain root and mix it with chicken fat and sheep's fat and apply as a dressing, or take rich figs, grind them with pigeon droppings, and other similar medicines called resolvents.
And the knotting will dissolve with continuous gentle massage with the hands, moving the limb in every direction at all times.  However, if the bending is long-standing, severe, and has become hardened, and necessity dictates its treatment with iron.
Then it is necessary to split it open from above, to release the connection of the bone, and to cut away any excess knotting or bone with thin cuts.  Care and effort must be used in this process, then treat the wound as described previously until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is a dislocation of a joint from its position, hindering movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing the afflicted severe pain and suffering.  Therefore, if someone suffers a dislocated jaw, one must immediately attempt to relocate it.
Do not delay at all; if delayed, the affected area will swell and form a mass, making relocation of the jaw difficult. Therefore, one should not delay, nor move nor stretch it while it is swollen, for it frequently causes painful spasms and suffering to the afflicted.
However, if this occurs, one should first bleed the afflicted person, then wait until the swelling subsides somewhat.  Then, apply warm water and ointment to the limb.  Then, gently relocate the jaw and treat each limb as described in its appropriate section.
The chapters on the jaw are also arranged according to what was previously discussed concerning fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower part.
Chapter twenty-four.
On treating the lower jaw.
The jaws dislocate rarely.  Their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they slightly shift from their position and relax slightly, or they completely dislocate and relax completely. The jaw, dislocated to the chest, causes the patient to drool and be unable to hold their saliva or close their jaw.  The tongue is unable to articulate speech.  If the dislocation is slight, it will often return to its position easily.
However, if the dislocation is complete, the jaw must be quickly repositioned and not delayed.  A servant should hold the patient's head, and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the patient's mouth if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
Or, if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, both thumbs should be inserted, and the other fingers of the hand should be used to align the jaw from the outside.  The physician instructs the patient to relax and release the jaw to move in every direction, and the physician aligns and pushes the jaw until it returns to its position.
If repositioning is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved, use warm water and ointments to make repositioning easier. Do not delay the repositioning, as stated before.  Once the jaws return to their proper position and the patient's mouth is closed and the jaws are not relaxed, then place linen bandages with a prepared mix of wax and rose ointment over them.
Then gently bind with a relaxed bandage. The patient should sleep on their back, with their head positioned between two pillows to prevent movement to the right or left, and should not be asked to chew anything.  His food should be soft and easily digestible.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is healed, let him eat whatever he desires.  Use gentleness, and do not force him to open his mouth for eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is completely healed.
If repositioning the jaws is difficult, and if they do not return to their correct positions at some point, fever, persistent headaches, and perhaps abdominal discomfort and repeated vomiting may occur.  If you observe this, know that it is a serious condition.
Often, one who experiences this condition dies within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On repositioning the collarbone and shoulder joint.
The collarbone, being attached to the chest on the inner side, does not usually dislocate inward.  However, it may dislocate outward, which is discernible by touch. To reduce the dislocation, the patient should lie on their back with their arms extended.
Then, press the area firmly with your hand, and it will return to its place.  After that, apply a bandage and splints, securing them tightly.  The part of the collarbone that extends to the shoulder and connects with it rarely dislocates. If it does, it should be repositioned as described above, to the extent possible.
Then apply a bandage, splints, and tight wraps.  Instruct the patient to remain still and quiet until healed.  This same treatment is used to reposition the shoulder joint if it is also displaced.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On reducing the dislocation of the shoulder.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one, towards the armpit and downward; two, towards the chest; and three, rarely, upwards and away from the shoulder.
It does not dislocate behind the shoulder blade nor in front of the nerve.  Most often, it dislocates downward toward the armpit, especially in those with little flesh, as it enters and exits quickly in such cases.
However, in those with much flesh, it is otherwise; that is, it exits and enters with difficulty.  Sometimes, a person may experience an injury or fall causing the shoulder to swell and become a hot, painful mass, which may be mistaken for a dislocation.  It is necessary to examine this thoroughly to ascertain the true state.
Then, in treating it, if the dislocation is downward towards the armpit, by comparing the dislocated shoulder with the healthy one, you will find a clear difference.  You will find a distortion in the head of the dislocated shoulder.  And beneath the armpit, the head of the dislocated shoulder feels like an egg upon palpation.
The afflicted person cannot raise their hand to their ear, nor move it through any range of motion.  Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, this will be evident to the touch, not hidden.
This dislocation may be easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is young. To reduce it, have an assistant raise the arm upwards. Then, place your thumbs under the armpit and forcibly lift the joint upwards to its proper place, while the assistant raises and extends the arm upward.
Then, lower it downwards, and it will return quickly. If it does not return by the method described above, and the dislocation has been present for several days, then the patient should bathe in hot water and use poultices that soften and relax, such as cooking the root of hyssop, fenugreek, and the herb crown of thorns in water and applying it.
Then, the patient lies on their back, and a ball of wool of moderate firmness is placed under their armpit.  Then, the physician places his heel on the ball, and takes hold of the patient's hand, extending it downwards.
And pushes the ball with his foot, and it will return to its place. Or, if you prefer, you may reduce it in this way: have a man taller than the patient stand on the side, and have him place his shoulder under the patient's armpit, lifting the armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another assistant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards their abdomen.  If the patient is light, then something else should be suspended from them to weigh them down, and then the dislocation will return immediately.  The dislocation may also be reduced in another way.
Another method is to place a long, round-headed piece of wood on the ground, like the pestle head of a mortar, neither thick nor thin.  Then, a soft cloth is placed on the top of the wood, and the patient is placed under the armpit, standing along the length of the wood.
Then, the patient extends their hand downwards from the other side, and also stretches their body forcefully from the other side.  Then the joint will quickly return to its place. If the dislocation is difficult to reduce by all the methods mentioned, then use this treatment: take a piece of wood about two cubits long. Its width should be about four fingers, and its thickness about two fingers.  It should have a round head, to easily enter the armpit in this manner:
Then, a soft cloth is tied to the round head to prevent the wood from hurting the patient.  The wood is then placed under the armpit, and the entire arm and forearm are extended along the wood downwards, and the wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then, the arm is placed on a transverse beam.  The hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. The joint will then enter its place immediately. If it has entered in any way possible, a moderate-sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then, the prepared bandage, made of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white, is placed over the entire shoulder, wrapping around from above.  The ball is then tightly tied under the armpit, and the bandage is wrapped around it from above. The hand is suspended from his neck.
He should not move his hand for seven days.  The patient's diet should be kept light to strengthen the limb, which will hasten healing.  Then, after seven, or five days, the bandage is removed, and he is encouraged to move the limb. If it remains stable and does not relax, he is considered healed.
If the joint frequently dislocates due to moisture or another ailment, cauterization with three cauterizing agents should be used, as previously described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done, and the bandage is removed after seven days, and the joint still does not stabilize,
If the bandage and tightening are repeated multiple times and the joint still does not stabilize, dislocating and relaxing, and it cannot be raised upwards, know that the nerve at the top of the shoulder has been severed, stretched, or relaxed.  In that case, know that the joint will never stabilize in its proper position.
For the jaw, which is situated near the chest and breast, and behind it, reposition it by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns to its place.  All other methods of tightening and treatment should be used until healing occurs.  If, after healing, there is stiffness in the limb and slowness in movement.
The patient should use a bath repeatedly until the stiffness softens and returns to its original state.
The twenty-seventh chapter.
On the treatment of the elbow joint.
The elbow joint is difficult to dislocate, and also difficult to relocate.  It dislocates in all directions, particularly forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you, as it is visible and palpable, whatever the form of the dislocation.
If you compare the dislocated elbow to the healthy one, its dislocation will be clearly evident due to the depression of the joint.  The arm cannot be bent, nor can the shoulder be touched.  You should act quickly to relocate the dislocation before a hot swelling develops.
If a hot swelling develops, it will be difficult to relocate it, and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backward.  This is the worst type of dislocation, the most painful, and often leads to death.
If it is possible to relocate it, a servant should extend his hand with both hands and outstretched arm, and the physician's hand should be positioned above and below the elbow, pushing the joint back into place with both thumbs or the base of his hands.
If the dislocation is forward, the arm may be bent once until the base of the hand strikes the corresponding shoulder. If the dislocation does not return, then employ very strong, forceful extension—two servants should extend the arm.
Two attendants also hold the patient to prevent displacement during extension.  Then the arm is rotated in each direction after wrapping a folded cloth or wide bandage around it.  If the physician works with both hands on the joint, applying ointment to smooth the joint's movement will make dislocating it easier.
Then the joint is pushed firmly until it returns.  After it returns, a bandage should be applied, containing a compress and drying agent with egg white, and the arm should be tightly bound and suspended from the patient's neck for several days.
Then it is released.  If the joint is fixed in place and the ligament has been released from it, leave it alone.  If you see that the joint is not firm, reapply the bandage and bandage and leave it for several days until it becomes firm, then release it. If any swelling occurs after the joint has returned and been released,
If movement is difficult, use moist heat in a bath, gentle massage and pressing until it softens, or place a fattened ram's horn on the joint, wrap it, and leave it for a day and a night, then remove it and put him in a bath.  If he sweats, massage the joint moderately.
Then repeat the horn application one, two, and three times with bathing until it softens. If you wish to use a dampened and thinned cow's hide with ghee, and bind it tightly, repeat this several times, as it will soften and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often dislocates and its reduction is easier than other joints, but it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the moment of dislocation, before the area becomes swollen or a hot swelling appears.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board and have an attendant extend the arm. The physician should place their hand on the prominence of the joint and push until it returns, however, it is necessary to observe if the dislocation has shifted to the palm of the hand.
Let the patient place the outside of their hand on the board during extension and reduction. If the dislocation is prominent on the outside of the hand, then let the inside of their hand be on the board, so the physician's hand rests on the same prominence of the joint. If it returns immediately, then do so, otherwise apply a bandage that soothes the swelling.
Leave it alone; do not attempt it again.  For it cannot be tolerated, and you cannot reduce it after days have passed, except that the joint will remain crooked and will not harm the patient, unless the hand weakens and cannot grasp anything.
At that point, you will know that the nerve is severed or injured, and there is no remedy except to apply cautery.  Perhaps it will help, perhaps it will not.  When the wrist is reduced, apply the bandage we described and then bind it tightly, leaving it for five days.
Then it is untied and the hand is exercised. If its movement is difficult and there is some stiffness, soften it with heated water and massage repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction.  When a finger dislocates to the palm's exterior or interior, extend the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumb until it returns to its place. Then tie the head of the finger and support it toward the direction to which it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it is straight on that day. If it is at night, tie it as described, and continue to loosen it during the day and exercise it with movement, and tie it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
And do the same if it dislocates to the palm's interior, tying it towards the same direction and performing your initial procedure until it heals.  Do likewise for any other direction of dislocation.
Chapter Thirty.
In treating the dislocation of the spine's vertebrae.
When a vertebra of the spine or neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, for death rapidly approaches the afflicted person.
A sign of this is that the afflicted person's stool is expelled involuntarily; they cannot control it.  Often, some of their limbs—either their legs, or their arms, or one of them—become relaxed.  If only one vertebra is displaced, it often shifts.
Its displacement can occur in four directions. That which moves backward is called a hump.  If the hump has developed from childhood, there is no treatment for it, nor any cure.  However, if it resulted from a fall, blow, or similar cause...
The ancients described various treatments with lengthy explanations, much of which is of little benefit.  I have condensed this into a few key points, making it sufficient to encompass most of their approaches, clarifying the meaning and depicting the mechanism differently from their descriptions and explanations.
I say that a hump occurring in the front of the chest, and likewise those occurring to the sides, are incurable.  Only those occurring in the back are treated, as I will describe.  This involves placing the patient on their face.
On a level platform near a wall, spread a damp mat underneath to prevent injury to the chest.  Then place an upright plank, embedded in a hole in the ground, near the head end of the platform, and another plank near the feet at the other end of the platform.
A servant holds one plank, not fastened in the hole, and another servant holds the other plank in the same way. Then, a soft, strong bandage is wrapped around the patient's chest and under the armpits.  The end of the bandage is extended to the plank near the head and tied to it.
Then, another bandage is tightened over the hips, knees, and ankles.  All the bandages are gathered and tied to the other plank at the feet.  Each servant then pulls the plank to their side using the bandage, and the planks remain in their embedded positions.
However, the planks are not fastened, as we have said.  The physician places his hands firmly on the hump until it is straightened, or he places a board upon it and leans on the board with his legs until it straightens.  If this treatment is not successful, take a board about three cubits long.
A hole is made in the wall, near the patient, to accommodate the end of the board.  Then the middle of the board is placed on the hump, and the physician places his legs on the other end and pulls firmly to compress the hump.
It will return to its place.  If you wish, you can use a hand-twisted screw, by driving two planks, each one cubit long, into the ground at the patient's head and another two planks at the patient's feet, at the end of the shop.
Let there be a span between each plank. A hole is made in each plank for the screw to pass through.  All the planks are firmly fastened to the ground so they do not move.  A twisted stick, which is the screw, is inserted into the holes of the two planks, and the bandage is twisted in the holes.
At the end of the screw is a hole in which a stick a span long is fastened.  Similar arrangements are made in the other two planks.  Then the bandage tied to the patient's chest is tightened around the screw at his head, and the bandages tied to his legs are tightened around the screw at his feet.
Then a servant stands by each screw, twisting the screw with the twisting tool. The physician adjusts the hump as previously described. This is the image of the screw, the shop, and the patient.
Then, after the fracture returns to its place and the area is level, the dried dressing should be applied, using egg white, and then a bandage.  A splint made of a board, three fingers wide or so, and the length of the hump, should be placed over the bandage.
And over it some good beads, then bind with the appropriate bandage. The patient should use soft food until healed. If any protuberance remains in the area at the end of the healing, then the medications that loosen and soften should be used, along with the splint we described, for a long time.
A lead plate may also be used.  A protuberance might appear at the end of the back beads, and this may be mistaken for dislocation.  However, it could be an extra bone that has protruded; do not use this treatment for it, as death may result.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and are not subject to the slight displacement and displacement that occur in other joints. The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inward, outward, forward, and backward.  It dislocates inward most often, and rarely forward or backward.  A sign of an inward dislocation is that if you place the sound leg of the afflicted person beside the affected leg, it will be longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the sound one, and the affected person will not be able to bend their leg at the hip. The area just below the hip will be swollen and noticeably inflamed, showing that the head of the thigh bone has moved there.
The signs of a dislocation outward will be the opposite of these signs.  A sign of a forward dislocation is that the leg is fully extended, but cannot be bent without pain, being in the knee.  If the person tries to walk, they cannot go forward.
Urine retention and swelling of the hip occur, and when walking, the person's weight is on the heel.  A sign of a backward dislocation is that the knee is not extended, and the person cannot bend the knee before bending the hip, and the leg is also shorter than the other.
The hip is relaxed, and the head of the thigh bone is clearly visible at the area of the waist.  However, to treat these types of dislocations, if it has been longstanding, if the person has not attempted to reduce it, and has remained in its condition, there is no cure at all.
Therefore, one should not allow this to happen. However, if the dislocation is recent and of any of the four types, immediately manipulate the joint by moving it in and out, and left and right.  Perhaps...
If it returns, no other treatment is necessary. If it does not return in this way, a strong, helpful assistant should hold the leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied above the knee, and another assistant should pull from above.
By placing his hands under the armpits, and then tightening a soft bandage around the root of the thigh, a third assistant holds the end of the bandage and pulls, either forward from the hip towards the collarbone, or backward towards the back.
All of them should pull at once, until the patient's body is raised from the ground and suspended. This type of pulling is common to all four types. If the dislocation returns as we have described, then otherwise, a specific treatment is required for each type.
If the dislocation is inward, the patient should lie on their healthy side, and a bandage should be placed around the root of the thigh, between the head of the thigh and the area below the groin.
Then, the bandage is pulled upward from the groin towards the upper body and the collarbone.  A strong assistant then takes hold of the thick part of the affected thigh with both arms and pulls it forcefully outward, which will return it to its place.
This type is easier than other methods of returning this limb. If this type of treatment is unsuccessful, then the patient's legs should be bound with a strong, soft bandage around the heels and knees.
Each bandage should be approximately four fingers apart. The affected leg should be extended further than the other by two fingers. Then, the patient should be suspended from the head using a piece of wood in the house, with the feet at a distance of two arms from the ground.
Then, a strong boy should grasp the head of the thigh, and another boy should hold the patient and push the first boy forcefully, causing the joint to return to its proper place quickly.  As for its specific return, if the dislocation is outward...
The patient should lie on the mat, as described for the case of a hump.  A bandage should be tightly applied to the affected leg and the chest.  Then, two pieces of wood should be placed, one at the patient's feet and the other at their head, and an additional, strongly secured piece of wood placed in the middle of the mat.
Damp cloths should be wrapped around it to prevent injury to the patient. The board should be placed between the patient's thighs to prevent downward displacement during the extension. Then, each attendant should pull in their direction, and the physician should adjust the hip with their hands.  If it returns to place...
Otherwise, place the board on it and apply pressure as described for the hump, except that the patient should lie on their healthy side. If the dislocation is forward, the patient's leg should be extended once in this exact position on the mat.
The physician places the palm of their right hand on the affected hip and presses it with the other hand.  The pressure should be extended downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be extended downwards.
It should be elevated above the ground, but placed on a firm surface, and the hip should be turned outwards, as described in its position on the mat on its face, with the bandages tight.
As we said before, pressure should also be applied to the board on the area where the joint has dislocated.  When the hip joint has returned correctly, a sign will be apparent, which is the extension of the patient's leg.
If you see them level and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned correctly.  Then join the thighs, apply the bandage, and secure it with a bandage that prevents any movement of the hip.
The patient must remain still for three or four days, then the bandage should be removed and the leg should be compared to the other. If you see them equal in size, know that the joint has set, and allow the patient to walk. If you see any relaxation in the joint, however,
Return and bandage it, tightening it as you did initially. Leave it for three more days.  Then remove the bandage, and let the patient walk slowly for several days until the joint strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On the treatment of the knee joint.
The knee joint dislocates in three ways: inward, outward, and downward (i.e., backward).  It does not dislocate forward.  A sign of dislocation is to instruct the patient to bring the leg close to the thigh.
If the leg does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated.  To reduce all types of dislocations, the patient should sit down, extending their legs if able.  A servant should sit behind the patient, holding the patient's torso and gently twisting it backward.  Then you should sit on the patient's thighs.
Place your back to the patient's face, and position their leg between your legs.  Then grasp the knee with both hands, interlacing your fingers around it.  Press firmly with both hands on the sides of the knee.  A second attendant should extend the patient's leg, until the knee returns to its place.
A sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh without force, then bandage it and secure the leg to the thigh. Bind them both with a bandage for three or four days, then release it.  Only allow minimal walking for several days until strength returns.
If the reduction described above proves impossible, use strong traction with the bandages previously described for hip treatment until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-three.
On the treatment of ankle fracture.
The ankle may dislocate slightly or completely.  Dislocation can be inward or outward. A sign of dislocation is that the ankle will appear swollen and prominent on the side to which it has dislocated.
As for treating a simple dislocation, gentle manual traction and realignment will suffice for its reduction.  However, if the dislocation is complete, the patient should sit down while a strong, capable attendant holds the patient firmly from behind at the middle of their back.
Then, with your right hand grasping the foot from above and your left hand from below at the heel, pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand, twice. Then extend the foot with your left hand and push the front of the foot towards the leg with your right hand, without force.
Repeat this process twice as described.  Then, on the third time, push the front of the foot towards the leg while pulling at the heel.  If the foot returns to its proper position in one or two repetitions like this and you see the foot is aligned, then stop. Otherwise, repeat the procedure, for it will return.
If the foot does not return with the methods described, then lay the patient on their back on the ground.  Drive a very firmly secured peg into the ground, positioned between the patient's thighs.  Wrap cloth around it to prevent injury to the patient.  Then the attendant should hold firmly on the patient's thigh.
Then a second attendant stretches the leg, either by hand or with a bandage tied around the patient's thigh.  Each attendant pulls in the opposite direction, and the peg remains positioned between the patient's thighs, holding them so that the patient's body does not move downwards during the stretching.  Then the physician aligns the joint (or fracture) with their hands.
Another attendant holds the healthy leg downwards.  If the joint returns quickly, then take the bandage and splint, and secure it firmly with bandages. Tie the foot down with bandages to the lower leg.  Care must be taken to avoid the nerve located behind the heel.
So that the bandage is not too tight and causes him pain.  Then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tighten it again.  Then release it on the third or fourth day.  The patient should avoid walking for forty days.
If the patient attempts to walk before this time, there is a risk that the joint will dislocate and not heal.  Treatment will then be unsuccessful.  If a hot swelling appears, then use the treatments described previously for other inflammations and swellings until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On treating the dislocated toes.
The dislocated bones should be gently aligned with a slight stretch, without force. This is not difficult but easy. If the dislocation is in one of the toes on the top of the foot, the patient should place their foot on a flat surface.
Or on a board while standing, like a walker. Then you should place your foot on the protruding joint and press down firmly on it with your foot until it returns to its proper alignment and there is no longer a protrusion.
Then place a board under the sole of their foot, covering the entire foot, which should have two ends.  Tie it tightly for three days, then loosen it and keep them from walking for several days until it strengthens and a return is avoided, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation that occur with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both.
When something like this occurs and you attempt treatment and reduction, it is often followed by death.
Therefore, one should not undertake such treatment unless one is skilled in the craft, experienced, gentle, compassionate, careful, and not rash or reckless.  In the beginning, one should use only medications that soothe hot swellings, and leave the patient be.
By God's will, except where you hope for the patient's safety from harm with mild illness, and some hope appears for it, then return it [the remedy] from the very beginning before a hot swelling occurs.
If the member returns to the desired state, then use the treatment that soothes hot swellings and treat the wound with suitable drying ointments. If the dislocation is with a fracture and fragments have detached from the bone, then remove them. Follow in this regard what we have mentioned concerning simple ailments in the preceding sections.
Strive diligently and purify yourself from entering the path of risk, as previously advised.  That will be more enduring for your reputation and safer for your standing, God willing.
